The Red Page
Flinders
The Life of Captain Matthew Flinders,
R.N., by Prof. Ernest Scott, of Melb. Uni-
versity, author of Terre Napoleon and The
Life of Laperouse, will take its place as one
of the great biographies in our language.
The inexplicable fact that hitherto no full
biography of the first man to circumnavi-
gate Australia has appeared is also a for-
tunate fact. Flinders has waited a cen-
tury for his biographer, and it was worth
this silence of a hundred years to find Ernest
Scott. An immense amount of labor has
gone into the foundations of this work.
Every available scrap of manuscript or print
even remotely bearing on Flinders’ life-story
has been carefully traced, in many cases
as far as Paris and Caen (Normandy), de-
ciphered and pondered: the bibliography
occupies many pages, and includes no less
than eight manuscript sources meticulously
cyanided. And to this fervor of research
must be added Ernest Scott’s lucid literary
style and his interest in the personal side of
his subject. Equipment, style, sympathy,
and his subject combine to make a brilliant
achievement in biography.
&
The life of Flinders was packed with
adventure. Robinson Crusoe woke in this
Lincolnshire schoolboy’s mind the ambition
to make discoveries of his own. He studied
navigation works and practised chart-mak-
ing in the quiet of the fens; then the chance
came and he joined the navy as “lieutenant’s
servant.” Luck was witli him from the
start. He went to sea under William Bligh,
who had, in his turn, sailed with Cook. It
was on Bligh's second voyage to Tahiti for
breadfruit —the first voyage had ended in
the famous mutiny-—that gave Flinders, at
18, his chance to see the world. On his
return to England he took part in the battle
off Brest against the French. But Australia
called —an Australia that was unknown and
amorphous.
The continent grew on the map of the
world gradually, slowly, almost accidentally.
It emerged out of the unknown, like some
vast mythical monster heaving its large
shoulders dank and dripping fi’om the un-
fathomed sea, and metamorphosed by a kiss
from the lips of into a being fair
to look upon and rich in kindly favors. It
took two centuries and a half for civilised
mankind to know Australia, even in form,
from the time when it was clearly under-
stood that there was such a country, until
at length it was mapped, measured and cir-
cumnavigated.
During the Middle Ages not only was it
thought impossible that there could be an
antipodes, but it was held to be heretical
to have a belief in the possibility. Later
on the map-makers, annoyed by the large,
empty space at the bottom of their charts,
usually filled it in with delightful pictures
of devils. They must have foreseen the
trusts. When Flinders came out to Aus-
tralia at the beginning of the nineteenth cen-
tury nothing was known about the south
coast; it was not known whether the con-
tinent was one or two islands; it was be-
lieved that a strait separated N.S.W. from
the western part, known as New Holland;
it was not known that Van Diemen’s Land
was an island.
Upon the ship that brought Flinders to
Sydney he found his closest friend, George
Bass. The two did valuable exploring work
in the neighborhood of the settlement. It was
only because Flinders could not get leave
that he did not accompany Bass, the ship’s
surgeon, on his remarkably adventurous voy-
age in a boat that led to his discovery of
Bass Strait. However, in the usual leaky
and badly found vessel in which he was fated
to make all his discoveries, Flinders with
Bass circumnavigated Van Diemen’s Land.
The subsequent fate of Bass is probed by
Ernest Seott to the most probable conclu-
sion. The scant evidence shows that Bass
took up the contraband trade with South
America. There he disappeared, evidently
captured by the Spaniards; and there is no
evidence to disprove the legend that he ended
his life as a slave in the Spanish mines.
PROGRESS!
Back in England six years later, Flinders
married, and was, thanks to his own energy
and to Joseph Banks’ interest, appointed to
the Investigator, another coffin-sliip, with
orders to explore the whole of the Aus-
tralian coast. He had meant to take his
young wife to Sydney with him, but the
Admiralty, which wasn’t on its honeymoon,
forbade. Confronted with the choice of giv-
ing up his exploration or his wife, Flinders
metaphorically threw his wife overboard.
But it must be remembered that he expected
to return within a few years. Two French
vessels, suspected of designs upon the new
continent, started before him; but Flinders
had charted the whole of the unknown south
coast before he met the French ship; and
his management of his ship was so fine and
his care of his crew’s health so admirable that
he brought his vessel to Sydney without loss
of a man from disease, while the crews of both
the French vessels were almost decimated
by scurvy. And Baudin had actually missed
Port'’Phillip. Then Flinders set forth on his
rotten craft, the Investigator, to sail round
Australia. He charted Torres Strait and
the Gulf of Carpentaria, then had to careen
his ship. The carpenters reported that in
12 months there would be scarcely a sound
timber in her, but that with luck and fine
weather she might survive six months.
Flinders recognised that in such a state the
vessel could not he of any use to survey the
coasts, as it would be dangerous to keep her
close inshore. He ran for Timor, and then
sailed round Australia in a grim race against
destruction, with a crew down with dysen-
tery and six men dead. A hard gale, it was
found, would have sent the Investigator to
investigate the bottom of the ocean; “She
must have crushed like an egg and gone
down.”
It being impossible to return to England
in such a derelict, Flinders got passage in
the Porpoise, in order to hand over his
charts and solicit another vessel from the
Admiralty. But the Porpoise and another
small ship were wrecked on Wreck Reef,
over 700 miles from Sydney. Flinders, the
indomitable, brought a six-oar cutter and a
crew to Sydney for relief. After picking
up the castaways Flinders set forth to Eng-
land in another leaky tub, the Cumberland.
She leaked so badly that he had to put in
at Ile-de-France (Mauritius), then occupied
by France. Here General Decaen took him
for a spy, arrested him, and held him as a
prisoner for six years. The whole trouble,
Scott points out, would have probably been
amicably settled if Flinders had been less
THE ADVANTAGE OF BLACK-
LEGGING.
If the soldier betrays his comrades in martial
engagements he is hanged. If the
civilian betrays his comrades in indus-
trial engagements he is hugged.
careful of Ms dignity. As Scott notes, once
that Decaen had made Flinders his prisoner,
circumstances prevented him from releasing
him. The biographer’s account of this in-
cident is a masterly and unbiased analysis
of a proceeding that has given rise to a bit-
ter and still-reverberating controversy.
Judgment has now been delivered, and there
can be no appeal. The last years of Flinders
in England, and his early death are fully
documented and complete.
The chapters dealing with Bass’s fate, the
French menace to Australia, and Flinders’
life on Mauritius leave nothing for further
biographers.
Flinders is not a tradition: the naviga-
tors of our own time count him a companion
of the watch. The excellence of his charts
was such that to this day the Admiralty
charts for those portions of the Australian
coast where he did original work bear upon
them the honored name of Matthew Flin-
ders ; and amongst the seamen who habitu-
ally traverse these coasts no name, not even
that of Cook, is so deeply esteemed as his.
But Flinders did more than chart Aus-
tralia-: he named it. Scott’s chapter on the
naming of this continent is an interesting
story. The word had been used three times
in the 17th and 18th centuries, but without
reference to the continent. The word, there-
fore, was not of Flinders’ devising: it was
re-invented by him. He felt the need of a
single name for the island which he had
demonstrated to be one great continent. In
1804, in a letter, he stated: “I call the
whole island Australia, or Terra Australis” ;
and he noted that New Holland was its west-
ward part, and N.S.W. its eastern.
There are valuable appendices: Baudin’s
account of Encounter Bay, and Peron’s re-
port on Port Jackson. A word must in mere
justice be added in praise of the publishers,
Angus and Robertson, Sydney. The appear-
ance of the book is worthy of its contents.
•
The second collection of Will Dyson’s
Cartoons (London Daily Hekald, 9d.,
obtainable at N.S.W. Bookstalls) proclaims
the ex-BuLLETiN artist as the most power-
ful cartoonist in England. He is purely a
cartoonist, a cartoonist of revolt. His work
shows signs of Norman Lindsay’s brilliant
line, and of the sledge-hammer methods of
the modern German masters. It is inter-
esting to note that Dyson has introduced
The Bulletin view-point to England, even
in the minor matter of the designation of
the useless classes as Fat. With masterly
art he preaches his tremendous sermons
against cant.
IN THE MIDLANDS.
“Mummer, why don’t they forcibly feed us?”
W. J. Chidley was born in Victoria. His
wife is dead; he has one son in Australasia.
As a youth> he was articled to an architect,
but he soon felt he had more of importance to
say about men’s bodies than their houses. A
life-long study of his chosen subject resulted
in his picturesque attire and The Answer.
Of this famous pamphlet he has —in the inter-
vals of being arrqsted and of being vainly
examined for signs of lunacy—sold over 4000
copies, practically the whole profits of which,
he softly says, have gone into lawyers’
pockets. At present he is selling a revised
edition of The Anstver (2s. 6d.), for circu-
lation among adults only. But he intends
bringing out what he calls a “complete”
edition of the famous work, with an intro-
duction by Prof. Francis Anderson (Sydney
University). This introduction has already
been printed. “The greater part of this
work,” savs Prof. Anderson, “may be read
with profit, quite apart from any agreement
or disagreement with the thesis set forth in
the concluding chapters. Mr. Chidley writes
on philosophy and on the various philoso-
phical systems of the past with great fulness
of knowledge and with much critical insight.
He is a man of broad culture and sincere
and unselfish aims. It would be unneces-
sary to make this last remark, were it not
that Mr. Chidley is known to the greater part
of the public as a man who has recently been
prosecuted for circulating what was alleged
to be an immoral b00k.... Mr. Chidley’s
Treatise involves a treatment of that problem
which is regarded by many as immoral,
except in technical language in scientific
treatises. Mr. Chidley’s book does not seem
to me to be immoral either in intention or
treatment. Mr. Chidley may be wrong in
his main contention, both in his statement of
facts and his interpretation of them; but
there is nothing in his exposition wMch
justifies the charge of immorality and in-
decency brought against him, unless discus-
sion of sex problems be held to be ipso facto
indecent... .1 venture to recommend Mr.
Chidley’s History of Philosophy, which he is
now preparing for publication, as a work
which may be read without danger to one’s
morals, and with considerable profit to one’s
intelligence.” Wowsers please copy.
The Bookman, St. Paul’s House, Warwick Square,
London, E. 0., offers prizes of five guineas each
for the best original lyric, the best original
sonnet on any famous event in English his-
tory, and for the best original humorous poem
in not more than 40 lines. Second prizes of
two guineas each are also given in each section.
Poems must reach The Bookman, for Australasian
competitors, by Dec. 1. Write name and address on
copy. Awards will be announced in January Bookman.
Spat’s Fact’ry, by Eaward Dyson (N.S.W.
Bookstall Co.), raises the question whether
there can be too much of a good thing.
Fact’ry ’Ands was a good thing, a brilliant
volume of Australian humor written in a
CONTROLLER OF THE TRAFFIC.
slang that would be undecipherable outside
this continent and confined for its matter to
a locale limited and almost unique. The
humor was broad, the characters were ex-
aggerated, and occasionally farcical, but the
author’s expertness and knowledge of life
made them human. Now the happenings of
Fact’ry ’Ands are continued, and apparently
they can be continued indefinitely. Yet to
the reader already sated with Dyson’s humor
and Dyson’s slang, this new volume has an
air of staleness. The audacity of that first
attempt carried Fact’ry ’Ands to popular-
ity ; we have grown accustomed to that
audacity, and no longer it excites us. The
fault is probably due, not to Dyson, but to
this page. Since the publication of the
earlier book this page has gone bald on the
top of its head, it grows tomatoes in its
suburban backyard, it carries the meat home
in a string bag, and it never, never goes out
without its goloshes.
At his recent Melbourne exhibition Arthur
Streeton in nine days sold £2499 worth of
pictures. A solid fact like that renders
superfluous the statement that last year
Streeton’s work was on exhibition at most
of the European capitals, or that at the Paris
Salon he gained a gold medal and other
honors. Streeton’s show in Melbourne must
have resembled a nine-to-ten winter drapery
sale. A Sydney show is due in mid-August:
there are still two-thii’ds of his pictures left
—some of his best work. At present he is
busy painting a series of panels of Sydney
Harbor for a final exhibition in Melbourne.
Streeton reports that the demand for Sydney
Harbors in Melbourne is steady, and that
the market is rising.
Art critics, trustees of National Galleries
and the blokes who don’t know anything
about art, but do know what they like, will
receive some preliminary education from a
collection of lantern slides of the works of
the best modern British masters that Arthur
Streeton purposes exhibiting in Sydney. The
agitation into which Adelaide has been
thrown by the purchase for her gallery of a
picture by William Orpen, A.R.A.. is suffi-
cient evidence of our ignorance of modern
art. _ The average number of visitors to the
Adelaide Gallery each week is 3000. The
interest aroused by Orpen’s picture has
raised the weekly attendance to 20,400.
Streeton has been asked to lecture about
Ornen _in Adelaide; and his collection of
slides includes six buff-orpentons.
Wild-Briar and Wattle-Blossom by J. J.
Malone (Linehan, Little Collins-street,
Melb., 2s. 6d.), is the utterance- of
a fresh and pleasant voice telling in simple
words the loveliness of Australia, seen
through the author’s misty memories of Ire-
land. J. J. Malone is no poet, but his
simplicity does not offend. His facile style
is best illustrated by a stanza such as this:
there are hundreds more of the same
quality:—
The sun rose o’er the towering hills,
And shot its golden quiver;
And down the gorges twinkling rills
Ran to the welcoming river;
The lyre-bird whistled through the trees,
Whose forest shades protect her,
And, wattles flung unto the breeze
The god’s own native nectar;
The mute lips of the muse it stirred,
And song again it brought her.
The day I rode to Gaffney’s Creek,
Beside the Goulburn water.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Petitioner.
In sad, dark robes, and veiled,
A shadowed grief was she;
And all the court was stirred
With heartfelt sympathy.
Her dainty fingers held
A snow-white handkerchief,
Which caught the pearly tears
That came lo her relief.
When some poor Wound was hurt;
But still she bore her part
in spite of all that told
An ill-used, breaking heart.
A broken-hearted wife.
She sought for her decree,
To free herself from one
Who judged himself as free.
In stern and manly wrath
Her counsel warmly spoke,
And round the crowded court
Applause in murmurs broke;
The sheriff’s tenderness
His sympathy expressed;
His Honor’s tones to her
Unusual notes possessed;
The counsel for the man
Seemed tamed—yes, even he
Was deeply touched, and spoke
Apologetic’ly.
A sweet young spirit wrecked!
A faithful, loving wife,
Condemned to live for aye,
A saddened, loveless life !
The case could only go
One way. The Judge could feci.
He said, that her decree
Her heart could never heal.
He spoke, with lofty scorn,
About cross-charges false. ...
That night she danced till morn,
The Merry Widow Waltz!
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Chapter and Verse.
John Murray, in answer to the Rev. Worrall’s
attack on him for having lent countenuce to boxing-
exhibitions, referred that gentleman to Matthew:
“Judge not that ye be not judged.”
The Curate muttered: “Do not swear,
For evil words return.
They make your brother rend his hair
And spit in wrath and spurn.”
The sinner said : “O Curate dear,
If you but bring your Bible here
You’ll find the Prophets swore—
See Nahum v. and 4.”
Again the Good Man spake his mind
The giddy sport to check;
“You racing man, you go it blind
Until your soul’s a wreck!”
Returned the Sport: “But old Jehu
I guess lie knew a thing or two
On horsey topics. See
Kings chapter ix. and 8.”
“Beware the giddy dance, my son !”
The warning Pilot cried.
“But we have warrant for our fun,”
The tripping youth replied,
“For Israel’s David, how he scored
By dancing hard before the Lord.
7/(.v godly ways are mine.
(Hosea vi. and 9.)”
The Killjoy cried : “Flirt not by night
And frivol not by day,
Lest cunning wiles of woman light
Thy morals lead astray.”
“O Killjoy, dear, I do but tread
Where Solomon the Sage bath led
llow can I then do wrong?
See ‘Proverbs’ and ‘The Song.’ ”
Said Broadbrim : “Drop your wicked guns
And melt your bay’nets down;
Forbear to teach your gentle sons
To shoot their foernen down.”
The soldier laughed : “I’ve heard before —
‘The Lord. Tie is a Man of War.’
I’m fighting in His crew
(Isaiah vii. and 2).”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
These Autumn Days.
Before old Winter whistles up
Ilis howling;, foam-flecked hounds,
And Autumn drains her final cup
And throws away the grounds.
Let’s strike the lyre and sing the praise
Of crystal, clear Autumnal days.
We tired of Summer ere she died.
For her too ardent breath
Each limp, perspiring lover fried;
So do not mourn her death,
These Autumn days when air and view
Still hold the charm of last night’s dew.
The cooling breeze is like a drink
And you can see for miles :
The dew-cleared harbor seems to wink
And smile a million smiles.
The how-wave like a lip draws hack
And foam-teeth show our morning track.
The eyes of Memory are clear,
This limpid Autumn day,
And Long Ago seems verv near
Instead of far away.
Old fountains, long to dust resigned,
Are playing freshly in my mind.
The cooling breath of Autumn brings
Some magic to my heart:
That rather weary organ sings
As if it ne’er would part
With Life, or let the body stop
Scuffling about this writing shop.
To-day T would take ship and steam
Unto the world’s clear edge—
Go driving straightly at my dream
With a good ship for wedge.
T would come home again, all me !
When summer bush-smoke blows to sea f
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 6, 1914.
I’ltICE Su.
PROLOGUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Enter, before the curtain, WAR, an old, bald, bespectacled man, in his gorgeous uniform of Generalissimo. He speaks the Prologue.
Ladies and gentlemen, the players wait.
But ere I ring the impatient curtain up
Upon this drama, I would have you know
What spectacle I have for you prepared,
What play it is that, after many years
Of preparations and postponements, I
Have for your craved approval proudly built.
You may have viewed some pretty plays of
war, '
Wherein a nice young officer arrayed
In scarlet or in khaki swaggered through
Some well stage-managed battles, for his
King. „
(There was no blood to spoil the pretty
The dead that fell fell cleanly, neatly killed ,
And if the hero had to nurse a wound
He lost an arm, whereo’er his sweetheait
grieved. ~
He was not shot in places unromantic ,
His entrails did not clutter up the scene ,
He did not lose his nose.) But this, my
great curtain now divides from
Is War!’War unadorned, the naked thing.
There are no medals and no epaulettes
In my poor drama. Stolid business-men
My generals are, who play a placid game
Di dominoes with little army-coips,
And t Jg s Wlth mUrdered men for their mls ‘
My drama has no decorative scene;
For all is smoke and flame and mud and
noise
And squirming things that once
Dismembered, crunched or shattered, still
alive
Or fortunately dead. No bugle shrills
Its clarion call. No colors blaze in pride
Before these grimy regiments. No charge
Of gallant horses nobly sweeps the field.
But here and there a soldier lies and snipes
A fellow-soldier somewhere out of sight;
Or screaming shrapnel brings untidy death ;
Or from the sky there floods a clotted red
Where grim mechanics grapple fatuously
In brittle little chariots of air.
There are a thousand scenes within my play,
It ranges o’er a mighty continent,
Perchance oer all the globe. It shows each
race
I n blmd and sullen anger flinging death —
Strange, poisoned, sudden, scientific death—
To fellow-men across its frontier line.
It shows a continent all desolate,
So sown lvith barren bullets that no green
Will ever grow again. It shows vast towns
In the grim clutch of hunger-driven mobs ;
It shows sweet villages all smelling rank
With putrefying corpses; and it shows
Dead women and dead children and dead
babes.
The actors in my drama are but men,
Mere men and women, rather like your-
selves,
And starving children like the little ones
Each night in cosy cots you hush to sleep.
The soldiers are but men in uniform —
The ordinary men you see in trams
Or work with in your office. They are all
Just husbands, brothers, fathers, lovers,
armed
With rifles, not umbrellas, curtly told
To kill some other rather decent chaps
Because they live across a little line
Made arbitrarily upon a map.
But though a continent I have for stage
I cannot picture war; for far beyond
Each battlefield another hidden fight
Goes on relentlessly. For every man
That you see smashed by shells, by cholera
killed,
Disembowelled, lopped or dying of gangrene,
Picture a little house, a pleasant home
With wife and children waiting anxiously,
Not knowing—maybe, ne’er indeed to know —
If they are widowed, orphaned. Picture
this
For every one of all these patient men
Hidden in trenches, spattered grey with oil
In aeroplanes, or deep in submarines
Beneath the sanguined sea.
Nay, picture more!
The curtain that I wait the word to draw
Will show you not a drama but yourselves.
This kindly curtain long has held you safe
From all the horrors of my spectacle.
It is a fragile frontier line between
You and the grim realities of war.
And when I ring the curtain up, beware!
The action on the stage will spread to you :
My shells wilt strike and rend the occupants
Of gallery and stalls and circle. You,
The actors and the victims of my play!
You are the soldiers and the soldiers’ wives,
The little children waiting for their dad;
You are the dying, the dismembered dead;
And you the hungry and demented mob,
And you the widow and the fatherless.
The manager impatient grows, and loud
The orchestra of slaughter sounds its paean :
The curtain tautens Shall I ring it up?
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 6, 1914.
WHEN OLD EUROPE BLAZED UP.
Lately the war resumed business at the
old address near the Danube. What exactly
ought to be done with the wild-cat States
and tribes of south-eastern Europe only a
god-like intelligence can tell. They are nearly
all wild and most of them are cat.
The beginnings of them date very far back.
For nearly 1000 years before its final perish
the old Greek Empire was supposed to be in
its last illness, and as far back as the year
500, or even earlier, whole nations came down
to pick the bones of the richest carcase on
earth. Unfortunately for them the carcase
wasn’t ready. The capacity of that
dead body for getting out of bed in
its last moments, and putting up one
more scrap, was the marvel of history. All
manner of broken tribes wandered back
disappointed, and, their old homes and
clubbing grounds being occupied by new-
comers, they pegged out allotments wherever
they could and dwelt in great wrath and dis-
content with their neighbors. When the
Turks secured the long-postponed cadaver at
Constantinople—and, incidentally, found
nothing in the pockets of the deceased—they
began to make great trouble in Central
Europe. So the wrath and discontent grew
worse, and the neighbors became more un-
pleasant, and the confusion was worse con-
founded. Partly owinv to these events there
are some 18 distinct races in Austria-Hun-
gary, and only three of them amount to
much. There are probably 12 separate and
distinct racial hatreds and a few humble
grudges. The nations are grievously
mixed, so that it is possible to find a smail
province where the population is fairly well
divided among three or four or' five conflict-
ing elements, each of which defines liberty
as the right to be on top and to jump with
both feet on its rivals. Consequently poli-
tical freedom, in the conventional accepta-
tion of the term, is a thing to be administered
very carefully. Many ill-assorted folk from
south-eastern Europe have emigrated to the
United States and have proved just as mixed
and just as incomprehensible in Colorado or
around Chicago or in the coal mines of
Pennsylvania as they did in their old dig-
gings. One reason why industrial trouble in
certain parts of Uncle Sam’s country con-
sists so largely of shooting is that the gun
speaks all languages, while the exiles speak
so many tongues that effective and intelligent
combination is difficult.
Providence, of somebody, sent the German
Of Vienna to police this ramshackle set of
peoples, and a good deal must be forgiven
to that stodgy bureaucrat by reason of the
difficulty of his job. It is even worse than
it looks, for many of the tribes have outside
relations. Whether, for instance, the Slav
iu Servia should annex his adjacent fellow-
Slav, the Croat, now belonging to Austria,
who doesn’t feteii use the same alphabet, 'oh
whether Austria should round off its Slav
territory by annexing Servia, and whether
Russia, as the boss Slav state, should inter-
fere, are points which open the way for a
heap of disagreement. Anyhow, an attempt
was recently made to throw light on the
argument by the murder of the heir to the
Austrian empire, and his wife. The un-
pleasant deed was done by Servians, and
was openly rejoiced over by Servians. Also
there was reason to suspect that it was
planned in Servia, apd that the Servian
Government wouldn’t have won a Melbourne
Cup in its haste, either to prevent the crime
or to arrest the perpetrators. The bureau-
crats at Vienna demanded more speed pnd
more retaliation and a number, of othef
tilings,. and failing to get them they threw
the brick .of war and spoke the clod of
hostility. Thereupon Europe uttered a. howl
of dismay, it wondered (1) whether Russia
would be forced, to take a hand against
Austria lest the loyal Slav of Russia should
shoot liis emperor for abandonment of his
fellow-Slav of Servia; (2) whether this
interference would bring in Germany, Italy
and Roumania; (3) whether France would
• pick up its gun to support its. Russian ally;
(4) whether Britain would find itself com-
pelled to help its, ally, France; (5) whethet
the latter would try to recover amid the
confusion the two provinces it lost in 1871}
(6) whether somebody would attempt in the
middle of the shindy to steal the microscopic
kingdoms of Holland and Belgium; (7)
whether the trouble would blow over and the
inevitable row be postponed. The trouble
failed to blow over, and Russia, Germany,
France, Austria and Servia promptly
became mixed, with Britain and half-a-dozen
other countries hurrying up in the back-
ground.
Almost at the beginning of the discussion
things happened that were ominous for Aus-
tralia. The stock exchanges in Russia and
Holland closed with a slam. Those of Aus-
tria and Prussia followed suit. The stock
exchange at Liverpool (England—not the
place near Sydney where the Old Men’s
Homes are) did the same. So did the con-
cern at Manchester. The panic struck far-
off Canada (a heavily-borrowing country
like Australia) so suddenly that the
Montreal stock exchange went home to roost.
The great jobbing establishment at London
didn’t close at first, but business dropped to
practically nothing, and British Government
securities fell to a nominal quotation of less
than 14s. in the f. They were in no demand
at that figure. Finally the London Ex-
change shut —for the first time since the
days of Julius Caesar, or thereabouts.
Also gold payments were suspended.
Paris decided to give its stock gamblers an
extra month in which to settle. All manner
of events arose. In three or four sudden
days affairs reached a point at which
Denham, and Holman, and Peacock and
Scaddan and Massey and the rest couldn’t
have floated a new loan or renewed an old
one except on preposterous terms, and per-
haps not on any terms. A number of finan-
ciers committed suicide owing to the bottom
having fallen out of the wild cat. They
were mostly Germans. The vast armies got
on the move, and everybody put his faith
in Providence and his gun. Also the Red-
fern (Sydney) cadets were called out.
* * * *
A great European war is full of possibili-
ties for Australia. This country will be
in the centre of the whirlpool, to say nothing
of the fact that, unlike Austria and Ger-
many, it may have an Asiatic whirlpool of
its own. If the new loan doesn’t float —and
how easily it may refuse to float is evident
when Russian, Dutch, Canadian and British
stock exchanges can be paralysed by the
mere appearance of war on the horizon —
public works will be suspended and thou-
sands of men thrown out of employment on
the instant. If the old loan can’t be renewed
when it falls due then there will be a sus-
pension of the national credit, and property
values will fall like a house of straw in a
gale. Solid concerns like the A.M.P.
Society and the Bank of New South Wales
and the Government Savings Banks will
almost certainly begin to crack on their
foundations, while smaller concerns will sag,
and still smaller ones will drop to pieces.
The importing business will be cut to the
bone, for it is only the constant flotation
of foreign loans which enables this country
to pay for its mass of foreign imports. Aus-
tralia may have no desire to declare war
against Servia or Switzerland or Bolivia
or any other remote place that never did it
any harm, but if some other Power, in which
it also takes no interest, starts a war, and
the conflict spreads, the results may be
amazing. Our rich, gold-producing continent
has got so badly into the habit of borrowing
gold from countries that don’t produce the
precious metal, that a large-sized scrap at
the other side of the world may reduce it
to beggary. It has sold its prospects for a
thousand years ahead in order to buy steel
rails and locomotives and artificial manures
and tinned sardines and feathered hats from
the foreigner, instead of getting to work and
making the goods itself. It has become more
dependent on the courtesy and forbearance
and good luck of outside financiers than any
other place in history. A week after Cohen
of Europe is driven to state that he can’t
even look at the new lOU the cities of Aus-
tralia will swarm with mendicants. A month
later, when he says he can’t find the money
to renew the expiring loan, Darling Point
and Toorak will be d.riven to consider
whether they can afford to buy a new pair
of boots, or whether the old pair will hang
out a while longer. It .has been the custom
to regard Cohen as a sort of Rock of Ages.
Unhappily he lives on the. brink of a preci-
pice, like any other financier. It may be a
gilded precipice, but it is as precarious as
if it was greased.
* - - * * *
But the evil possibilities of a purely Euro-
pean war, so far as Australia is concerned,
are mostly financial. They are bad enough,
still they are financial. If some adjacent
Great Power—perhaps an unreasonable and
combustible brown creature which objects to
anybody save itself practising an exclusion
policy—took the opportunity to fish in the
troubled waters around otir coasts new
complications might arise. He couldn’t be
dealt with by the simple process of filing our
schedule. Australia would have to stand
on its own defence, and its defence is poor.
Yet the event is only the sort of thing which
may be expected. Every country with a
grievance or an ambition selects the handiest
time to settle the question. Russia tore
up the Black Sea treaty when France, the
principal guarantor, was in a state of armed
and disastrous annoyance with Germany.
Britain sent its famous expedition into Tibet
when Russia was busy with Japan and mis-
fortune. Also, Britain robbed its friend
Turkey of Cyprus when Turkey was having
a bad time with Russia, and its bayonet was
bent and its fez squashed. Roumania stood
out of the recent Balkan wars, and then it
robbed Bulgaria of a big strip of territory
because the Bulgars were too exhausted to
resist. Austria occupied Bosnia and Herzego-
vina at the same date when Britain took
Cyprus, and took thfem from the same person
and for the.same I’eason —i. 6., that the timd
was ripe, if Japdn chooses to resent,Aus-
tralia's absolutely ftedessary Colored Exclu-
sion policy, pnd selects the most convenient
time. to do the deed, it will be
simply, playing the ordinary game. It
will be no worse than any other
Power, and no worse than any Labor Union
or Employers’ Union which goes to arbitra-
tion and then disregards the award. Nothing
will be gained by appeals to treaties, oi- to
the Hague Peace Foolishness—which lids
already fled for home, Or to right and justice.
Uhe country with the strongest force decides
what is right and justice, and its decision
stands. All that Australia can do in such d
cdse is to take stock of its resources.
* * * *
These are some of the resources
(1) An army in which hardly a single soldier is
more than 21 years of age.
(2) A very small and very raw navy.
(3) A grievous lack of factories for the supply
of rifles, cannon, ammunition, warships, railway
trains, steel rails, uniforms, boots and the thousand
and one essentials for a campaign.
(4) A busted credit —for it will be busted then.
This country has of late found it hard enough to
borrow the money it wanted, even though the lender
was at peace, and the borrower wasn’t in trouble.
(5) A lamentable habit of planting all the big
cities and as much as possible of the public wealth,
the manufactures, the finance and the machinery of
government in big, ill-defended coastal cities. When
an enemy arrives on the nearest coast it is a far
cry to Delhi, Moscow, Teheran, Vienna, Berlin,
Paris, Madrid, the city of Mexico, Puebla, Tobolsk,
Birmingham, Lyons, Dresden, Munich, Oabul, Buda-
pest, Sofia or Pretoria. But when a nation puts
practically all its portable assets on the shore and
has no inland city worth twopence to retreat to, it
is taking chances.
(6) The railway congestion policy, by reason of
which the capture of one or two bloated and ill-
defended cities on the coast would enable an enemy
to cut the railway system to pieces.
(7) The mixed railway gauges, by reason of which
a comparatively small army, proceeding from (say)
Melbourne to the defence of Queensland, would pro-
bably spend from three to six weeks on the road
owing to the necessity to shift men and horses and
guns and stores from one train to another at the
borders.
(8) The fact that nearly half the Australian
coast-line has no railway communication with the
big centres of the south-east. The extension of the
railway from the south has been indefinitely post-
poned—apparently because two dreary opportunists
are afraid of losing votes in one State if they fix on
another as the southern terminus.
(9) The habit of importing goods (and loan money
to pay for the goods) from abroad, instead of im-
porting sturdy artisans (and defenders of the coun-
try) to make the goods here.
(10) The habit of sinking the fiscal issue.
(11) The utterly accursed habit of assuming that
the good times will last for ever, and that none of
the ill-fortune which has struck every other people
at one time or another will ever strike us.
* * * *
The defences don’t look massive, and if,
when our turn comes, events move as swiftly
as they did last week, there will be small
chance of improving them. This country
may yet be found, on Bruce Smith’s birth-
day, making its great effort to defend the
North from behind a butter-box.
[James Edmond, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
THE BASTILLE AT DARLING POINT.
The great full-blown, all-round European
war has started at last. It is appro-
priate, in the circumstances, for the
community to gather round its imported
grate, and stir up the fire with its Brum-
magem poker, and draw closer to the blaze
its foreign-made arm-chair, and consider
the position as it is likely to affect Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
A first effect of w T ar involving the
great money-lending nations must be a stop-
page of foreign loans. Britain, France and
the rest will need all their cash for them-
selves. The Australian States may then
have insufficient to pay their interest bills as
they fall due; they will certainly not have
enough to maintain their public works. In
consequence, most of the men employed on
railway extensions and duplication works,
in the Government dockyards, at Canberra,
on the Transcontinental, at the Holman
Ministry’s proposed Conservatorium of
Music, and in a thousand other directions,
will lose their jobs. Trade between Europe
and the Commonwealth will cease almost
entirely for obvious reasons. The shrink-
age in imports and exports will mean that
thousands of wharf-laborers, carters, shop
and warehouse employees, mechanics and
operatives in manufactories and garages
and engineering shops will be thrown out
of employment. Simultaneously the smaller
retail men will begin to go bankrupt. Most
of the mines will close down, partly owing
to the paralysis of industry abroad, partly
to the fact that there will be no vessels
available to take their products. Unem-
ployed actors, boxers, footballers, vaudeville
performers and restaurant waiters will
swarm on all sides, since the world always
begins by economising on its amusements in
time of stress. As the position grows more
acute the bigger business places, both in
the cities and the country, will drastically
reduce their staffs. In a very few weeks it
will be as though 50,DU0 strikes were raging.
Picture the situation of, say, Sydney in
such circumstances. Sydney will be in a par-
ticularly bad way, since most of the unem-
ployed will naturally gravitate there in the
hope of Government doles or food or relief
of one sort or another. But the Government
will have no money with which to pay doles,
and the shortage of food will be appalling.
For the first time Australians will realise
how many of their necessaries are imported.
According to the latest figures of Knibbs’s
Year Book the Commonwealth imported in
1910, from the U.K. alone, milk (preserved,
etc.), 'to the value of £124,116; confec-
tionery, £345,733; fish, £147,793; meats,
£34,260; grain, £36,825 ; and so on. Food-
stuffs of animal origin to the value of
£873,697 entered Australia from abroad in
this year. Practically all this country's
groceries and tobacco come from foreign
parts, and most of its drinks. Within 24
hours of the commencement of hostilities
between Austria and Servia “the prices of
necessaries all over the Continent had risen
100 per cent.,” to quote one of last Friday’s
cable-messages. In Australia many neces-
saries tvili not be merely expensive; they will
be unobtainable. When an unemployed crowd
is few in numbers and merely hard iip it
begs; but when it numbers thousands and
is starving it simply takes. There may
Come 4 time, in Sydney, wheil the alteady
somewhat futile police force Will abandon
the hopeless task of keeping order among
the armies of ravenous men and women who
will throng the streets and parks. In those
days the richest man wili not be the million-
aire who bolds an immense parcel of C.S.R.
Co. shares and a trunk full of gas debeh-
tiifes, and who owns streets of city pro-
perty, but the far-sighted individual who
lias fortified his suburban home, a.nd sunk a
well in the garden, and stocked the out-
houses with biscuits and tinned food. While
the crowd is looting the kitchens of the big
clubs, and retiring to the Domain to con-
sume the proceeds, and while the grocery
and confectionery departments at the Civil
Service Stores and Lassetter’s and
Anthony Horderns’ are being raided,
and while a thousand bars are in-
voluntarily supplying the multitude with
free drinks, the citizen who has made
provision against the coming storm will be
able to live, for a while, in security and
comfort, more or less.
* * * *
He will do well, however, not to
be idle. Every leisure moment will
need to be employed by him < throw-
ing up earth-works around his resi-
dence, laying barb-wire entanglements,
digging moats, sowing the approaches with
hidden mines, and in general preparing for
the worst. He will by these means insure
himself against trouble until the mob has
commandeered everything that is easy to
secure in other parts of the city. By that
time, however, word will doubtless have got
abroad that there is a rich store of eat-
ables at, say, Darling Point. Thrir owner
will automatically become a public enemy.
Amidst howls and execrations a move will
be made for his fortress. He will not be in
a position to fight. Beyond giving a display
of that truculent impassivity which one
observes in the sacked immigrant, lie will
probably do nothing while the revolutionaries
are hammering at his gates. The sort of
Bastille which is rushed together in a week
or so does not take long to fall. Unless the
miraculous intervenes the Darling Point
resident will have a bad time. He and his
family will be among the thousands of vic-
tims offered up to the importing and borrow-
ing habits. The whole situation constitutes
about as good an argument as a country
could want in favor of borrowing less and
manufacturing more.
[John Bede Dalley, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Mild Protest by the Undersigned.
It would be interesting to know on what
principle the Sydney morning papers are
proceeding in regard to the Commonwealth
Electoral Act. Clause 181 of the Act says:
On and after the date of issue and before the
return of any writ.... every article, report, letter or
other matter commenting upon any candidate shall
be signed. Penalty, fifty pounds.
On Saturday last the Sydney Telegraph
printed a report of a speech by Joseph
Cook at Ballarat in which that statesman
alleged, amongst other things, that his rival,
Fisher, had “been handing out bribes.” In
another report Conroy was made to accuse
Tudor of having been responsible for the
arrival in Australia of the Meat Trust; and
in yet another Irvine charged Fisher with
being a “briber” and a “desperate gambler.”
None of them was signed. On the other
hand, a name and address were appended
by the Telegraph to a number of in-
nocuous little paragraphs, of which the fol-
lowing is a fair sample:—
The war in Europe and the apprehensions of exten-
sion are still overshadowing all other questions. The
Federal elections are suffering in common with every
public event. All the same, the progress is still
slowly proceeding.
The Herald’s political reports (1/8/T4)
were on the same lines as the Telegraph’s,
and they also were unsigned. But a signa-
ture was attached to a three-quarter column
of items such as this :—•
Persons who will be absent on polling day, Sep-
tember 5, from the electorate for which they are
enrolled, may now record their votes on making the
necessary declaration.
The law of the land is broken just as irre-
trievably by the publication of a signature-
less report of a speech in Federal politics as
it would be by the printing of a paragraph
drawing attention to the date of the elec-
tions, or to the influence on them of a
European war. At a rough estimate the
Herald and Telegraph already owe the
Treasury about £IOOO between them—assum-
ing that the English language, as it appears
in the Electoral Act, has any meaning. Far
be it from the writer to uphold this idiotic
measure. His protest is merely, against what
looks like injurious differentiation on
the part of the authorities. If one writer
has to give himself an unwanted advt. every
time he makes a remark about Federal poli-
tics, why not all? Have the Liberal papers
been accorded a private license to break the
law, and if so, why? The Cook Govern-


merit’s wide-flung reputation for imperish-
able probity of an almost Methodistical
character demands that this matter should
be cleared up.
[John Bede Dalley, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Penelope in the Pulpit.
Dear Bulletin, —Early next month 10,000
Anglican parsons and churchwardens will
assemble and meet together in London to
discuss the “possibility” of women entering
the priesthood. Meanwhile newspapers are
shrieking against the “extravagance” of the
idea, and bishops and such pooh-pooh the
lady-parson proposition for “scriptural rea-
sons,” in the first place, and “in view of the
traditions —of the church.” Archbishop
Clarke, of Melbourne, put the objections in
those terms, but why the church’s man-made
regulations should be any more unalterable
than a country’s man-made constitution the
prelated Tory ’aforesaid didn’t condescend to
explain. Now, to any inquiring sinner who
drops casually into an Anglican place of
worship at worship-time the. gentleman in
(he pulpit seems almost as much out of
place as a male doctor at an accouchement.
The congregation is 85 per cent, feminine and
infantile, the children being simply disciples
of mother or auntie in the matter of church-
going. Of the small minority of men who
are listening to the other man, every two
out of three would probably prefer to be
receiving exhortation from a nice female
shepherd. Religion, iii all denominations, is
mainly a matter of feminine interest and
juvenile entertainment. In the Anglican
denomination it is particularly noticeable
that the men, as a rule, have lost all interest
in Church creeds and dogmas, prayers and
thanksgivings. Outside of the choir none
of the hymn singers are men, and the choir
is a worldly-minded organisation of amateur
artists whose soul —if they have one —is con-
cerned only with the technicalities of vocal-
ism. As for the organist, when he isn’t a
woman he is usually addicted to drink.
Church-workers in the, .parish are Women all
along the lihe; the waydeds, the .vicar
and the cbr'Ate ht'e men, and who shall say
that this ohe-sided arrangement is h success
now that “tta'ditioh” has lost its sanctity
and faith is confronted by the .facts and
theories of modern enlightenment? Within
the past 35 years there, has been only one
religious movement which possessed a force
of its .own, and is not wholly a success of
sound business managemept. The Salv’army,
the Harmy which preaches the crudest of
Christian doctrine, has effected religious com
version, of a sort, in tens .of thousands of
men. This for the reason that its preachers
have been largely women .as well .as men.
“General” Boom's first blood-and-fire cam;
paigns, with their testimony meetings, and
knee-drills, would have gone for very little
had there not been Happy Dinahs to bang
the cymbals and address the scoffers. The
Halleujah Lass has been the all-impo.rtant
factor in the diffusion of vociferous holiness.
The woman preacher, so contrary to the
“traditions of the churches,” has done mote
for Christianity, maybe, than any 10.000
parsons ordained since the Church of Eng-
land was born. Why should she not take
holy orders and climb into the pulnit?
Women do not really need the attraction
of a reverend man to draw them to church.
For that matter, a reverend pastoress might
prove a stronger draw, for the sex dearly
loves to see one of its own members bossing
any sort of theatrical show. And it is cer-
tain that a petticoat in the pulpit would
attract more men than go to an Anglican
church nowadays. In short,, the proposal
to admit women into the priesthood is. so
essentially reasonable, and so likely, to bring
new spirit into a very tired religion, that
the bishops are bound to denounce it as “un-
thinkable.” Nothing is thinkable to the
high episcopalian mind until it has been put
into practice.—Yours, etc., O.K.

Facts and Fogs.
A demand for the loan of Fisher’s ear: —
It would be of assistance to the electors and of
service to his party if Fisher would come down
occasionally from the general to the particular. I
was impressed by two of the projects he referred
to in his policy speech at Maryborough (Q-) * . a
State steamer service and orphan pensions, itt
both these matters I am satisfied Fisher's impulse
is on the right lines; yet I read him with disap-
pointment and dismay, These ire two important
questions, well worth fighting for; but Unless
Fisher mends his methods he is going to get mighty
little of that added support which these proposals
are put forward to secure. There is a practical side
to all projects of the kind, and it is this the com-
munity naturally wants to hear about. Take old-
age pensions. Where they were first introduced the
man in the front of the fight had the idea down in
black and white. He was able to estimate that
there would be a certain number of pensioners the
first year, so many the second, and thence on ; that
the cost each year would be this or that; that the
charge could be met in a certain way. The public
could understand this and argue the matter in the
light of ascertained facts and wax enthusiastic about
it. Fisher, on the other hand, just talked in the
air.
Heavy, sombre references to “the poor, ragged
schoolboys of England, inmates of institutions of
which the name is a disgrace to civilisation,” have
no point for Australians. All that kind of stuff
may be taken as read, or at best used merely to
support what is definite. Joe Cook or the gentle
Willy Kelly could use the same phrases without loss
of dignity or detachment from Liberalism. What
the people should hear from Fisher is a definite
proposal based on our own necessities and social
obligations. He should give us all the relevant
facts and figures. If he hasn’t got them, then this
Fabian attitude of his can have no possible value
to the constituencies. Likewise with State steamer
services. Fisher talks of these and his State
Produce Distributing Department in the same
nebulous strain. They would, he says, be factors
in reducing the cost of living—which may be true
or may not. Then he goes on without quoting a
single figure or outlining any plan to declare that
“these steamers carrying produce from one port in
the Commonwealth to another would beneficially
assist the wage-earners” ; that the operations of the
State Produce Department in “lifting the produce
of the farm, the forest and the mine, and making
advances to the producers at a lower rate of interest
than they now pay would encourage production and
confidence.” Has Fisher or anyone on his behalf
figured all this out—the cost of it, the possible
trading results, the approximate saving to be
secured by the purchaser of the fat lamb and the
nourishing potato? Can he say anything with cer-
tainty to either the suburban householder or the
proprietor of farm or orchard or vegetable garden ? •
If he can, he should. If he cannot, what’s the use
of offering the elector great masses of dough?
[C. A. Jeffries, 214 George-street, Sydney.]

Kalgoorlie’s Pains and Prospects.
Dear Bulletin, —Kalgoorlie has been suffer-
ing from dry rot for a long time, and just at
the present moment the disease is merging
into moribundity. Those whose memories
are saturated with the fragrance of the
champagne and devilled chicken days may
turn from the picture with tears, and they
are bound to admit that the Kalgoorlie in-
habitant who can to-day afford the humble
beer and the filling bread and cheese is doing
not so badly. If there is any town
in the Commonwealth where house rents are
lower than they are in Kalgoorlie, that town
is deserving of profound pity. The high-
collar official in the Town Hall who used to
write out permits for new buildings has
gone on very extended leave, and if there
are any carpenters and bricklayers left it is
because they can’t raise the railway fare to
get out of it, Gent. One is compulsorily
honest, and of the small army of commission
snatchers there aren’t sufficient left to form a
nucleus crew for a bumboat. The paper
which used to represent Labor m Labor s
Westralian headquarters has migrated to
Perth and taken what promises to be a lease
of vigorous life. The big drapery folk have
either closed up fob good or have opened shops
in Perth, to pass the time profitably until
prosperity hits the Old Camp once more.
And the pubs—ye eods ! The pubs ! -Ihey
are the only bits of humor in the town. Ihe
licensees go in and out in a swift procession,
and if a man is seen entering a saloon-bar it
is even betting whether he is a cash customer
or only the new licensee. When Bill. comes
in with a cheque from somewhere in the
Mulga, and at daybreak breasts the bar at
which he loaded up last night, looking for a
free drink ftom the jovial landlord of a few
hours ago, h'e finds a total stranger at the
beer-pump. And this stranger scornfully
requests him to go where he got drunk, un-
less he desires to be shifted by a John llor.
The miners themselves have become tame and
subdued. About Christmas last the unions
announced that on the first day of May they
intended to give the mine-owners and specu-
lators the shock of their lives. Well, the
day named has come and gone and. what has
happened? A bommittee of the unions inter-
ested has btought in a report to the effect
that things may bfe allowed to gb on as they
are, that the times are inopportune, and; so
far as non-unionists are concerned, members
of Unions are to wrestle in prayer with the
unbeliever, and lead him gently by the ear
to the union booking office. The fighting
unions, in fact; are face to face with the
prevailing decay, and they don’t quite see
how they can shift it. They ate perfectly
well aware that 75 pet cent, of the mines on
the belt are living from hand to mouth and
are so weak from warit of nourishment that
a rude jolt would send them into the land
of slumber for ever. So far as the few
mines still paying are concerned, there is not
one which is not in a position to calculate
with tolerable exactness the time at which its
last ton of dirt will be raised. But all this
does not justify any assumption that Kal-
goorlie will shortly be wiped out. and become
mere heaps of old iron, old bricks, splintered
wood and beef and jan cans. Far from it. Kal-
goorlie is the natural centre and hub of a
huge auriferous country, and is already be-
ginning to realise her ambition as a great
commercial market place. When the out-
back miner wants a holiday he as seldom
takes it in Perth as the coastal farmer does
his in Kalgoorlie. The miner regards Kal-
goorlie as the king-pin in his industry, and
it is to it he comes with his little cheque.
He would sooner pay sixpence in Kalgoorlie
for a long sleever and meet his pals, whose
interests are his interests, than he would pay
threepence for his moisture in Perth, with
the big chance of meeting nothing more
friendly than toughs and confidence men.
The back country is going ahead, and every
wa.vback prospector and dryblower is an asset
to Kalgoorlie. The trans-Australian rail-
way is a matter of the near future, and there
is nothing more certain than that the big
railway workshops of the W.A. end will be
at Kalgoorlie, and that will make for steady
prosperity. The State Government has
brought in a Pill to fill in the blank place
between Ksperance and Norseman with rails,
and Kalgoorlie is the natural market for the
produce of the big farming district which
this route will open up. Stock will pasture
along the national trunk line, and for it, too,
Kalgoorlie is the market and distributing
centre. In short, however depressed the
Old Camp is to-day, it possesses hopes fore-
shadowing a future which, if not so errati-
cally coruscating as the past, will be solid
and lasting, and keep its name permanently
in large type on the map of the continent. —
Yours, etc., Black Swan.
(Able-bodied member of the Australian Compulsory
Peace Society—ever so many members, and still
swelling.)
More Superannuation Arrangements.
Dear Bulletin, —N.S.W. State civil ser-
vants are waiting with anxiety to know the
details of Premier Holman’s promised super-
annuation scheme, and to find out who will
steal the money this time. In the mode-
rately long ago there was a fairly honest
superannuation fund to which public ser-
vants contributed under compulsion. Then
it was resolved to reduce the tariff so that
the country might import more and employ
fewer people in local manufactures. A rush
of impecuniosity came upon the Treasury,
and the civil service was cut down whole-
sale in order to save money. A lot of the
sacked were entitled to retiring allowances,
which fact made a difficulty, for the Govern-
ment was busy manufacturing a surplus by
charging a lot of this year’s public debt
interest to next year amid the applause of
the S. M. Herald and Telegraph, and it
hated to have its surplus disturbed. So
a great deal of the civil servants’ pension
fund—contributed by themselves under com-
pulsion—was used to pay retiring allow-
ances. It was butchered to make a surplus.
Then the fact was widely announced that
the fund had gone bankrupt on a gigantic
scale. It wasn’t announced, widely or other-
wise, that the Government would stand by. it
and make good the loss. A panic set in.
Many civil servants regarded their pensions
as lost and the money they were still paying
into the fund ds lost also. They Wete. will-
ing to accept almost any terms. Finally
this offer was made to them: They were to
cease paying into the fund. They were to
forfeit their pensions. The money they had
actually paid in was to be credited to them
without any back interest, and held up or
prigged or hypothecated till they left the
service or died. Between the date of, this
arrangement and the retirement or death
the amount actually paid in was to earn 6
per cent, simple interest. Three per cent,
is mighty little —much less than the Govern-
ment can borrow for, but then .this wasnt
borrowing but bushranging. And whereas d
per cent, simple interest on fIOQ for 36
years is £9O, 3 per cent, compound interest
is £142. The difference was more bush-
ranging. And when .men left the State sei-
v,icc, not through death or old age, but
through transfer to the .Federal authority,
the old State geni.uses still hung on to then-
money and refused to settle the old account.
If there is to be a new Civil Service Super-
annuation Fund in the Ma State the exact
nature of the. robbery ought, to. be specified
in advance. Brigandage and piracy on the
high seas shouldn’t be sprung suddenly on a
sedentary community whose nerves are none
of the best.—Yours, etc., Henry Horse-
collar.
The N.S.W. Government proposes tb estab;
lish a State Fire Insurance Office, and Chief
Secretary Gann got the first beading
of the Bill through the Assembly last
week. Wade, of course, attacked it
as ah interference with Private Entel-
prise, and alleged that the Maoriland
precedent was no precedent at all, because
the private cos. in M.E. had formed a com-
bine. As a matter of fact there was no
more of a combine in the Shivery Islands
than there is in Australia. They don t
call it a combine, of course; they call it a
tariff, and there isn’t a single non-tantt
office hereabouts. Not only are rates fixed
absolutely, but the combine decides how
many agents a co. shall have in a town,
and when a member reckons he has struck
a new idea, the combine decides whether
he shall be allowed to work it. If he goes
ahead first he is liable to be brought to
reason with a fine —as one was lately, lliat
is no more and no less than the M.B. com-
bine amounted to; so Wade falls through
the bottom of his own busted argument.

“Black Swan” ; With his usual happy smile
Westralian Premier Scaddan laid on the
table of the Assembly one day recently the
accounts of the State steamship service for
the year ending 30th June, 1913. The fact
that the balance-sheet is just a year overdue
doesn’t worry Scaddan any more than do
the cold-blooded comments of a prying
Auditor-General. From the document it
appears that the value of the fleet, in tle
Government books, is £97,001, and the loss
on the working of the vessels up to the dam
named is set down at £22,813. From the
financial statement of the Auditor-General
for the year ending 30th June, 1914, pre-
sented to the Assembly a few days after the
belated sheet, it is gathered that the steamers
are pursuing their disastrous course, the mss
on the year’s transactions being £19,256. The
figures for another of the State enterprises
are even more disheartening, and when Scad-
dan saw them his smile broadened consider-
ably. The State Implement Works, it now
appears, exhibit a deficit of £41,186 for the
year, and no reason is assigned, nor is any
prospect of improvement foreshadowed.
Even the State hotels have not experienced
the estimated thirst. They were expected
to produce an income of £46,000; as a mat-
ter of fact they took only £32,562, and as
they spent £30,321, they can hardly be said
to be doing much for the deficit. The theory
of State trading is right enough, but a great
deal depends on whether State enterprises
are used honestly to make money for the
State, or are employed by a process of politi-
cal corruption to find jobs for a quite un-
necessary number of supporters of the gang
in office. In other words, a State steam-
ship enterprise depends on the management,
just as does a private enterprise.

Undeterred by the fate of the dredges Man-
chester and Poseidon, lost at sea with all
hands, the Sydney Harbor Trust Commis-
sioners have arranged to import three har-
bor deepeners, picked up cheap at Rangoon.
These are named the Seagull, the Swallow
and the Curlew. The first-named has
arrived safely, but the others have yet to
gamble on the face of the waters with D.
Jones, the party who recently went aboard
another of the Sydney Harbor Trust’s for-
eign purchases and nailed the sign of the
coffin on the mainmast. Wonderful is the
fetich of the cheap, imported article, which
keeps many local artisans out of work,
drowns several others, throws their wives and
children on the State, and frequently doesn t
turn up to attempt to meet the bill. In
fact, about the most expensive luxury this
long-suffering country knows is the cheap,
imported dredge.
The shriek of the expiring alderman is one
of the ordinary sounds of Sydney. Sub-
urban councils now mostly realise that a
Greater Sydney is inevitable; nevertheless
the aldermen are determined to go out mak-
ing noises like frogs that have been trodden
on. Those of Glebe have given the key-
note ; and these days, when a suburban
council meets, the business of most import-
ance is the consideration of a circular letter
from the Council of the Place Where the
Smells Come From, inviting co-operation in
resisting the Greater Sydney proposals.
Then does Alderman Mugwump howl dis-
mally about the muddlement of the future,
while Alderman Guff shrieks about
tyranny, and Alderman Tripe, declares the
whole scheme to be un-British. But, in
spite of all these threatening disasters, the
suburbs refuse to thrill or boil worth tup-
pence. The fact that the suburbs know
their own disgraceful roads, and have smelt
their own dreadful smells, and have got
tired of listening to 46 different lots of
frogs croaking in 46 distinct council cham-
bers, probablv has something to do with
the refusal of the suburbs to boil or even
bubble.
War and Loans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Waddell drew a melancholy picture of stag-
nation, consequent upon the closure of the money
market, and declared that any mah who blinked the
possibility was not a statesman.., .When the Pre-
mier made one of his beautiful and able speeches at
Cootamundra he should tell the people how he pro-
posed to raise the money. The Government was not
far from trouble while it was spending nine millions
a year out of loan moneys and he again warned the
Government of the financial Nemesis which would
overtake profligate and improvident financiers on the
Treasury benches. —From N.S.W. Assembly debates.
Keenly regardant as you are, camcrados,
you will probably have observed some-
thing remarkably like a WAR about.
1)0 you know what WAR means? .
Not the two-men-and-a-boy wars of the
Hebrews and the Hittites and the Jebu-
Not "the kindergarten class of wars such As
the Wars of the Roses-, the War of
Jenkins’s Ear, or the war that Don
Bra-cieneacio declares against Senor
Ippolyte of Siluria or Barataria;
Not even the elegant, hysterical,_ modern
wars in Belgravia and in the vicinity of
the capital of the Finest Nation.
Oh dear, no ! „ ,
That is not at all the sort of WAR I refer
I refer to the genuine, thorough, wholesale,
world-encompassing conflict which is
not much farther away than next week
—if so far !
I refer to the WAR that will see Six Great
Powers and a horde of the Powers That
Hardly Count rushing to battle.
The WAR that will upset the modern world,
and throw back much of its boasted
Civilisation about 150 years !
The WAR that in its primary or initial stage
has convulsed finance, commerce and
the glorious Future of a dozen countries
like ours! .
l’he WAR that, without our seeing a foreign
ship of soldier, or hearing one rifle-shot
fired, will bring unimaginable distress
and ruin to some, and excessive incon-
venience to a whole lot of us!
The WAR that will set about 11,000,UUU
armed men in the field and muster navies
totalling 1200 warships—not. to mention
the small (but extremely vicious). craft.
The WAR that will cost its participators
about £600,000,000 for one brief twelve
months, besides the Inevitable Bill for
Damages! ...
The WAR that may mean the wiping up, or
out, of more than one vain-glorious
State! _ , , ,
The WAR that will present a Butcher s
Bill that makes one -shudder to think
of ’
Do you’ know what WAR means, camerados?
Well, it means all that, and a lot more.
What are we doing about it?
q ul .— a hem ! —statesmen are busily engaged
in safeguarding our interests.
Oh, certainly!
is flying from every Ship
of state in the world, and our—er
statesmen are shortening sail and mak-
ing everything snug alow and alott,
camerados.
The Bourses and the Stock Exchanges of
the world are closed, or closing, up, the
Money Market has developed locomotor
ataxia ; even dear, old UNCLE is feel-
ing unwell and has shut his office and
gone home to think, but
Ah, yes; but, camerados, .
Our—ehoo !—statesmen remain magnihcentfy
calm, and refuse to feel even slightly
apprehensive. . . .
Sublime impassiveness; glorious placidity.
They still pursue, unhasting, unresting, their
grand and loanly way!
They display, amid the wreck of credit an
the crash of shutters, their unconquered
spirit of unrelenting borrowing.
They positively decline to be dismayed, dis-
couraged, dispirited or disillusioned.
Honor these great statesmen, camerados;
acclaim them for the stern, unbending
Borrowers that they are! .
Whoever —whatever goes down in blood and
ruin, the eternal and immutable Loan
of our —er —leaders shall go on !
Surely to Heaven (or The Other Place) a
mere trifling scrap in effete old Europe
will not prevent our unparalleled
Holman from placing more loans?
The thing is preposterous —unthinkable
excruciatingly comic, camerados;
Why. this (and the next) is the very time
when an UNCLE is likely to be posi-
tively useful! . ~ .
Our sturdy Borrowers will. soon drag that
poor old trembler out into God s glori-
ous loanshine!
Shorten sail? Reduce expenditure. Stop
Loans? Nonsense!
As well stop the universe! ..
Common sense? Common sense be jiggcied .
Who wants common sense?
Loans ! Loans ! Give us Loans, or give us
euthanasia! . ~
Is it possible that this miserable bogey,
WAR, can stop our Loans?
Great Holman guard us!
WAR is Hell, camerados!
N.s.w.
O. C. CABOT.
Helpless!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I never fired a gun in my life:
There’s nothing that I can do
To help myself, the kids and the wife—
And I am a young man, too!
When shops close down and the desks are
shut,
I’ll only be in the way !
What sort of a figure do I cut
In this thund’ring world to-day?
There’ll be a gun for the baby son—
If Britain and he should last;
But where is my training and my gun,
Now trouble is coming fast?
Oh, must I issue a High Court writ
And sit by a shot-lashed sea,
And match with powder my lawyer’s wit
On a shore of lunacy !
We were fools, my friends! Let’s still be
fools!
While the tumult sinks and swells,
Let them hear our only fighting tools —
The idiot’s cap and bells!
A High Court writ and a lawyer grim,
And a very frightful Fee,
And we stand on Armageddon’s rim
Equipped as all fools should be.
N.S.W.
MARS HANNIBAL SMITH.

Swift & Company Ltd., Australasian Agents, 26/30 Clarence St., Sydney
“The Young Soldier”— his summary:—
When The Bulletin was going to press the rags
and flinders of the military news were still coming
in. Britain, it was alleged, had at last done the
deed, and declared war. Italy, Sweden and Roumania
were standing out. Apparently Germany in its haste
made the same ugly and unconsidered rush which
France made in 1870, when it leaped into the fray
without making quite sure that its then allies,
Austria and Italy, were ready to leap also. If
Kaiser William had toiled and perspired in the
telephone box for another two days before commit-
ting himself against Russia, he might have made
sure that his alleged friends would follow him, but
he lived for the first time up to his title of William
the Sudden, and left his friends behind. They have
now no visible craving to catch up. Australia's
Joseph Cook is also trying to be Sudden, and talks
of sending forth an expeditionary force of 20,000 men.
Fortunately, before Cook’s handful could be
organised and shipped to the scene of action, either
the war would be over or Europe would be insol-
vent. Two Broken Hill mines and some mines else-
where have closed, and dozens of other closures are
impending. Two big Orient steamers have stopped
in friendly ports to await developments. Sydney
Harbor has been closed during the dark hours of
night, and it is reported that the ferry-boats may
soon be prohibited from going past Bradley’s Head,
which will be darned awkward for the fellow who
wants to take his best girl to Manly. Australian
wheat and wool exports are growing precarious by
reason of the enormous cost of marine insurance. The
daily papers have begun to publish information
about the different classes of civilians who are liable
to be called out in a great emergency—beginning
with the unmarried, untrained youth and ending with
the married, father aged 60. Australia hasn’t begun
to see war on the far horizon yet. Still it is
getting a heap of excitement. Among other things it
has seen the German liners warned off the Australian
coast, and it has begun to sing the “Marseillaise.”
Amid all these frenzies and possibilities the N.S.W.
Government is still blewing loan money on its Con-
servatorium of Music.

Randolph Bedford’s reply (R. Bedford
lias his present postal address at The
Bulletin office, 214 George-street, Syd-
ney) :
Mr. Teesdale Smith’s alleged reply (B. 30/7/’l4)
disproves all that I didn’t say, and leaves the essen-
tials of my charges unanswered. The double dissolu-
tion did suppress the Teesdale Smith inquiry, and
Teesdale Smith’s desire for publicity at even such
a cost as £lOOO has nothing to do with the work
of a Royal Commission or its automatic death. The
wild offer of £lOOO prompts me to ask to whom
Mr. T. Smith offered the money—to J. Cook or to
the Commission or to K. O’Malley? I hold no brief
for the Castles machine, but I do hold a brief
against J. Cook and W. Kelly, who gave T. Smith
a non-competitive contract. I did not say that the
Castles machine was thrown out of commission for T.
Smith, but I did say that, after having done 22,000 or
so cubic yards of track-forming at less than 3d. a yard,
it was not repaired as it should have been, and the
highly fortunate T. Smith got a muck-shifting con-
tract at higher rates. I did not say that the
machine was thrown out on T. Smith’s account, but
it pleases T. Smith to obscure the issue by implying
so. My figures came from the official replies of the
Home Affairs Department to Fred Bamford’s ques-
tions in the House of Reps., and as Mr. T. Smith
seems under the impression that he is a public bene-
factor I hereby give him the figures again. The
Castles machine and day labor cost 2 2-3 d. per cub.
yard. T. Smith’s contract was —for cuttings 4s. 6d.
a yard and for banks 2s. 6d., material from the
cuttings to be let into the embankments for 1J
chains from the mouth of such cuts. That, in itself,
was bad enough, but not bad enough for the toy
Minister of a fool Department, and the liighly-
patriotic T. Smith. T. Smith has been paid, not
4s. 6d. a yard for banks made of spoil from cut-
tings, but 4s. 6d. and 2s. 6d., or 7s. a yard, an
astounding variation of the original terms of the
contract. The contract now is for 107,279 cub. yards
of cutting at 4s. 6d. —a total of £24,137 —and
134,744 cub. yards of banks at 2s. 6d. —totalling
£16,843; a grand total of £40,980. The correct
figures would be 107,279 yards at 4s. 6d.= £24,137
and 27,465 yards (the difference in the yardage
of cuttings and banks) at 2s. 6d. —a total of
£27,570 instead of £40,980. Mr. T. Smith is
hereby exhorted to stick to the highly lucrative
game of muck-shifting. Whenever he takes up a
pen he cuts himself. I further advise him not to
offer that £ICOO so loudly, lest I call his bluff
myself.

Fanatics of the Freetrade variety dredge
satisfaction out of some queer ash-pits.
Recently one W. E. Macklin, who has spent
many years in China, was entertained by a
small and infrequent Sydney body, which
calls itself the N.S.W. Freetrade and Land
Values League; and he made that curious
institution feel quite proud. He told it that
Freetrade was the fixed faith of China; and
the works of Mencius, a Chow who flourished
before clothes were worn in Britain, showed
that it always had been China’s idea of the
right way to achieve national greatness.
The quaint Freetrade League is evidently
so satisfied with the progress China has
made in a thousand years that it hugged
Mencius as a long-lost brother, and gave
him three cheers to go on with.
[ln case the ghost of Freetrade should have some
bearing on Federal politics—Harold Mercer,
Katoomba, N.S.W.]
