In these times of juggling with European
news, and wonderment as to where the
next loan is to come from, the Mysterious
is on a boom. Hence the season of Le Roy,
i alma and Bosco at the Palace (Sydney)
should be profitable to all concerned. The
show deserves to prosper. Le Roy has pro-
vided something new in necromancy. Many
of the tricks that were most popular when
the combination was at the Tivoli, nine years
ago,_ such as Bosco’s joyous muddle in re-
placing the heads of a decapitated hen and
duck, were repeated on Saturday night, and
others have.been added. The principal illu-
sion, in which an early Christian flapper is
thrown into the lions’ den by the Emperor
Nero (he had a hasty temper with reluctant
flappers, but was a fairly good poet and often
contributed to The Bulletin’s Christmas
number) is excellently presented. It is a
show in itself, except that Talma (the
flapper) can’t act for nuts, and dresses for
the part of the meek and lowly virgin in dia-
mond hair-clusters and other revue-girl
accessories. Apparently Talma believes
she has been given a mission to improve her
partners’ work by bright and airy business
generally. The mission is sound, but the
business is bankrupt. The supporting vau-
devillains help to make up an entertainment
worth booking. Any night during the next
couple of weeks will do.
“The Girl in the Taxi” opens at Her
Majesty s (Sydney) on Saturday night next,
-the Girl has now been in almost every
environment that the dramatist can imagine.
She has been in the Train. She has been in
. has been behind the scenes at
the Gaiety. She has grown up and been
the Worst Homan in London. She has fre-
quently been in a sinking ship. She has been
m a state of spiritual exaltation, and also
in the coffin, as witness the lamentable case
of Little Eva. Now if somebody would write
the Girl m the Bath the list might be about
complete.
Remarkable is the popular success of the
hist essay ot the Australian Opera League
Its premiere on Monday, at the Sydney Re-
pertory Theatre—now comfortably re-
seated, provided with private boxes, a new
and artistic curtain, and accommodation for
a large orchestra —attracted a huge and en-
thusiastic audience. The Australian drama
began sporadically in three cities, with
“BUNTY PULLS
THE STRINGS”
(SYDNEY ROYAL).
Weelum at the plate
modest aims and modest means and tenta-
tive support; but the Australian Opera
springs fully grown from untilled soil. Two
miniature "grand” operas are presented in
the six nights' season, Fritz Hart’s “Pier-
rette” and Alfred Hill’s “Giovanni,” the
former in one act and the latter in three,
and both, it is regrettably noted, un-Austra-
lian in theme. “Pierrette’ tells the story, in
four characters, of the return of Pierrot
and Pierrette to the garden of convention,
the reception of the girl by her relatives, her
renunciation of Pierrot, the discovery that
their mutual love is deeper than they had
thought, and the consequent second elope-
ment of Pierrette. In order to get the fan-
tastic atmosphere, the figures of the uncle
and aunt are made unsympathetic. The
libretto is commonplace and essentially dra-
matic ; but the musical treatment is bril-
liant. Fritz Hart belongs to the ultra-
modern school. His melodic themes are
dexterously interwoven in the continuous
texture of his musical narrative; and the
score calls for close attention on the part
of artists, orchestra and audience. An
obvious vagueness of intention is observ-
able in the orchestration, especially in the
nocturne interlude played to an empty
stage. Such a nocturne should hold its own
though deprived of dramatic action, yet it
proves too slight to excuse its length. Rut
there is an overbrimming sense of humor,
gaiety, tenderness and rhythm in the whole
opera. A quartette at the supper table is
infectiously humorous, and the Pierrette
gay duet is repeated with taking abandon.
Fritz Hart fails only in the expression of
the deepest dramatic effects, though rightly
he has avoided as much as possible this
weakness by the choice of so slight a theme.
Miss Elsa Warmann’s robust voice is equal
to all the demands of the composer, and her
appearance and acting are especially com-
mendable. Reginald Roberts makes a taking
Pierrot. The uncle, Frederic R. Collier, is
admirably in place.
“BUNTY PULLS THE STRINGS."
Rab Big-gar declares war on his father, and
refuses to open the ham shop.
“Giovanni” is the story of the village
sculptor youth who leaves his playmate and
mother to win fame in his art and the inter-
est of Princess Violetta. Confronted with
the choice of returning to his dying mother
or remaining in the Princess’s toils and
winning new fame as a sculptor, Giovanni
takes the easier way. He wins his fame,
but finds that the Princess was but playing
with him. He smashes his statue and is
about to shoot himself when Amina, his vil-
lage playmate, enters his studio and brings
about the conventional happy ending. The
music is full of pure melody, with the num-
bers complete in themselves, though there
are many leading motifs, including, it may
be mentioned, a “cow” motive. Unlike
“Pierrette,” this opera calls for highly
dramatic effects, and herein Alfred Hill in
the last act proves wanting. But the humor
and the melodic charm of “Giovanni” are
entrancing. It is unnecessary to single out
any number for special mention: the
melody ripples through every act. William
Everard had to take the part of Giovanni at
short notice, and though his voice does not
ring true in the higher passages, in acting
he more than capably fills the part.
Florence Young’s splendid voice is finely
telling in the part of the Princess. Muriel
Barton is admirably placed as Giovanni’s
mother, and Dulcie Huxtable is sweetly in-
effective in the part of Amina. T. Courtney
Hood and F. Perc. Mitchell acouit them-
selves well, the latter’s doubled part of
Padre and Porter affording his fine voice
admirable chances. The beautiful setting
of “Giovanni” is due to the brush of Souter.

As it is impossible to prophesy, all that
this paper commits itself to is the adver-
tised statement that “Madame X ” intends
at present to stay at the Sydney Criterion.
The Bulletin is anxiously awaiting con-
firmation of the report that, at midnight on
Sunday, Colonel Beaumont Smith mounted
the box office at the Adelphi Theatre, Syd-
ney, with a revolver in each hand and a
dagger in the other, and addressed an
excited crowd of 300,000. In the course of
an impassioned speech he declared that “The
Barrier” was doing excellent business, and
that he would answer only to himself and
Lieutenant Marcus for what had happened.
Consols immediately rose to 136, and the
Bank of England announced a sale of cash
at under cost price.
The Buckler season at Sydney Little
Theatre will close on Saturday evening.
Then a gloom will fall on the city, as it
does on the bush township when Alderman
Boggs dies, and all the population follows
the funeral in a spring-cart on a wet Sun-
day, and an inscription is put above the
dust saying that the gaiety of nations has
been blown out.

At the Sydney Repertory Theatre, on
July 28, the Lotus Players presented four
one-act Australian plays. “Way Out Back,”
by Mrs. C. Peterkin, was not so bad as
“Venette the Dancer,” by V. Haldane-
Coward ; though neither play could show
any possible reason for its existence.
“Wattle Tree Farm,” by Alfred Hill, con-
firmed the pleasant impression given at its
first performance a few weeks ago. But
‘Gerties Glad Eye,” a real Australian
comedy by J. Clarence Lee, was an up-
roarious and well-deserved triumph. This
playlet has been, produced by amateur organi-
sations many times; and each time it has
been a ln the character of
Maud .Mary, a ten-year old Austra-
lian kid, delightfully played by Essie
Patterson, the author has made a
genuine contribution to the Australian
stage. . Some day some Australian manager
will discover the money-making possibilities
of Maud Mary.
Unless present plans are changed at the
eleventh hour, Bunty Pulls the Strings for
the last time this (Thursday) evening, at
Sydney Royal.

There is good business at the National
Amphitheatre (Sydney), and good reason
for it in the turns of James F. Kelly and
Emma Pollock (comedians), Williams and
Warner (musicians), Herman and Shirley,
Brown and Lawson, the banjo-thrilling
Stavordales and the sharpshooteragettes,
Misses Clinton and Beatrice.
He was a libelmonger who stated that
the Imperial Salon de Tango Hug had re-
moved from William-street, Sydney. Also,
his grandmother inhabited a peanut grove.
The music boom in Adelaide continues.
Leonard Borwick has been interpreting
Chopin at the Town Hall, caressing the piano
as if he loved it and were having a qui'et
talk with it. Harold Bauer has now arrived,
with his more strenuous methods, for a series
of concerts. The Anderson co. holds the
Royal with melodrama. “The French Spy”
is good of its kind, and gets some fine acting;
so does “The Face,” which has made an
unexpected reappearance at the Window,
and deserves to have something besides
bouquets thrown at it. Vaude and Verne
are back at the King’s r unlike most other
patter pairs, they generally contrive to have
something new to say. Fields still drops
bricks and plays weird billiards; Talleur
Andrews has a delightful ballad, “Dream
Girl” ; the Four Vagabonds harmonise; and
Warren, St. Leon and the Weldons do ath-
letic turns. The King’s has a good bill, too,
with the gymnastic Lamys, Hayes and Rives
the very-wet Neptuna, and “Juno Salmo.”
Viewed in the light of other days, when
competition was keen and the combine was
unborn, the present revival of “A Royal
Divorce” at Melbourne Royal seems rather
cheap and tawdry. The brilliant “Court”
that Nap. summons to his divorce proceed-
ings is represented by one Talleyrand, two
or three marshals and four ladies in sub-
urban splendor, who carry their arms un-
comfortably. Again, when he boards 11.M.5.
Northumberland to meet a job lot of British
admirals, the force drawn up in honor of
the captive consists of eight unimpressive
seamen and six marines. There is more
smoke and noise than pomp and glory about
the stage setting of this inopportune re-
minder of France’s bygone politics. Yet
in a measure the improved acting of certain
of the principal characters atones for scenic
shortcomings. Irene Browne, less man-
nered in her utterance than of yore, makes
Josephine a dignified and interesting sufferer.
Edward O’Neil is good in his part; likewise
Bentley. Victor is also satisfactory, and
Lizette Parkes is easily the best of the
Stephanies up to date. As for Julius
Knight, the monotonous heaviness of his
voice is forgiven for the sake of his fine
Napoleonic make-up, his rich assortment of
poses and his occasional flashes of power.
Why isn’t there an influential movement
towards working up a testimonial perform-
ance on behalf of invalided Harrie Ireland,
the tall Bland Holtian actress with the
glorious auburn hair? Harrie has had the
cruellest luck. She was forced to leave the
stage because of her “rheumatism,” as it
appeared to be, and for a while she had fair
prospects of making a do of the confectionery
shop in the St. Kilda Theatre building. She
ran the business and her friends and her

“A ROYAL DIVORCE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said Boney to Josey:
“My love and my queen,
Of Fortune most rosy,
My star has been;
“But one disappointment,
One fly in the ointment,
One wasp in the posy,
You know what I mean,”
Said Boney to Josey,
“You brought me, my
Queen.
On you was the onus
Of earning the bonus;
And no baby Nap has,
No gold bowl of pap has
Appeared on the scene.”
Said Josey to Boney:
“Your scheme I endorse ;
Your wife and your crony
Will quit you, of course ;
“With mute lamentation
I give up my station
To live all alonev ;
No other resource
Is left to me, Boney,
“I yield me to force.
I’ll go from the palace,
And bear you no malice,
Whenever it suits you—
You monarchs are brutes,
you 1 —■
To get a divorce.”

“The Mikado,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
is treated in a broad-comedy spirit by the
Gilbert and Sullivan co., and for
12 nights their representation of the quaintly
libellous Jap. opera will be more popular
than artistic. Sometimes the fooling is per-
mitted the freedom of a panto., and nobody,
save Arnold, as Pish Tush, follows the rules
of Japanese travesty. Even the facial dis-
guises and the mincing methods of tradi-
tional impersonations are wanting in Ruth
Lincoln and Dorothy Vane, neither of whom
puts any heathen charm into her appear-
ance. McKeown, as Nanki Poo, suggests
a plump female person with her hair
screwed up for action in the domestic clean-
ing way; Ivavanagh is a fat, genial Pooh
Bah; and Wallen’s idea of Ko-Ko is a
hybrid humorist who accepts his sentence of
lingering death as a harmless joke. In this
atmosphere of irresponsibility the arrival
of the Mikado (Wilson) and Katisha
(Ethel Morrison) goes for less than usual.
They strike no terror to anybody’s soul :
moreover, the modern, easy-going style of
the ruling autocrat would invite familiarity
under any circumstances. Take it alto-
gether the opera as produced at Her
Majesty’s seems a milder musical attraction
and a less Gilbertian entertainment than it
has usually been in the past The per-
formance breaks no records, but is bright
enough to ensure good business for its
allotted span of two weeks.
“The Burglar and The Lady,” staged at
Melbourne Princess’s on Saturday night,
disclosed Allen Doone as a gentle, calf-
bound edition of Raffles, cracking cribs in
defiance of Sherlock Holmes and his gloomy
men. Doone inserts himself into a bank
and an impressive private residence, and each
time his enterprise is a scientific success.
Holmes is so expert in misconstruing the
obvious that the burglar’s triumph is a fore-
gone conclusion, and Doone is so conscious of
the undetective’s inferiority that he doesn’t
hesitate to cheek Mr. Holmes in public, and
also halt on a risky midnight “job” to warble
irrelevantly of Erin, or burble of love to the
first damsel that arrests his roving fancy.
Probably it is not so much the lady’s
influence as Holmes’s inability to “pinch”
Raffles that eventually forces the latter to
reformation. Anyhow, by the third act he
tires of being the pride of the cracksman’s
profesh. and when this writer left him
towards the close of the fourth act he was

Allen Doone plays Raffles in his soft, inde-

shortcomings of a character that doesn’t
allow of more than one tribute to the sham-
rock per night. Onslow Edgeworth vainly
tries to make S. Holmes interesting by
theorising audibly and scowling dreadfully.
Clive Farnliam, as the Rev. Banfield, is a
credit to the cloth. Miss Keeley appears as
Norma Grey, who turns Raffles’s thoughts
to blameless pursuits. Miss Bashford
inhabits the garments of a German nurse-
girl ; and others are cast for comparatively
minor parts. The scenery, by J. S. Mann,
becomes effective only towards the conclu-
sion.

Matters of imminent interest at Mel-
bourne Auditorium are two more Mischa
Elman concerts, due on Saturday and Mon-
day next. Just now, however, a violinist
on a public platform is rather remindful
of the late unlamented Nero, who fiddled
when the political situation was ablaze.
Walter E. Deaves and Co., with their sot
of Merry Mannikins, were the most striking
of the newcomers at Melbourne Bijou on
Saturday. The smart, unusual dances of
the Small family earned an uproarious vote
of confidence, and the act of the Lyrical
Four, singers and instrumentalists, was
sufficient apology for their inclusion on the
bill. All but one of the Stagpooles continue
to be as superfluous as before, and to give a
martial tint to the programme. Gardner’s
pups of the bulldog breed are reappearing
for a space.
Antony and Cleopatra finished their inn-
ings at West’s Melbourne premises on
Saturday. This week West is filming “Sixty
Years a Queen,” a stiff and solemn record of
the late Victoria’s reign, issued for the
benefit of the aged and uninformed.
For one day of the year Bruce Smith
holds Britain up as a glorious example and
on the other 364 days he throws mud at it.
That Australia should pay for its Fleet out
of current revenue is, he assures his con-
stituents, quite idiotic —nobody but the
crude, ignorant and unjust Labor party
would think of it. Yet the dear old Mother-
land, which has been piling up its Navy
during recent years, instead of going deeper
into debt to do it. has actually been paying
off debt (mostly incurred •in war-time) as
follows: —
1904 .. .. £770,778,762
1909 . . . . 711,475,865
1914 .. .. 661,473,765
It’s a terrible mistake, no doubt, not to
get so heavily in debt in peace that there’s
nothing left to borrow in war-time; still,
Britain will probably go along in its crude,
ignorant and unjust old way, and disregard
Bruce Smith, disgraceful and all as that may
be.
[S. H. Prior, Bulletin Office, Georgc-street, Sydney.]
“H.H.”: I would like to know how the
wretched, unwholesome “afternoon shift,”
which forces men to try to snatch a frag-
ment of sleep amid the glare and clatter of
day, came to creep into the coal-mining in-
dustry. It is necessary on ships and long-
distance trains, and it is often essential on
wharves if any kind of a time-table is to be
adhered to. But it seems to me that the
man who digs up fuel from the earth, and
the man who digs up potatoes from the
earth, and the person who digs down fuel
from the trees are all people with whom
night work is absurd slave-driving. The
poorest case of the three is that for night
work in coal mines. The mine-owner is
tearing up and selling a most valuable asset
that can never be replaced. It doesn’t grow
again like trees and potatoes. Why this
absurd haste to be rid of the asset?
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Kaniva” : Robert Kennedy and his pupils
sang to the Pioneers at their last monthly
meeting in Melbourne. Robert was one of
a talented family who, with father David,
toured Australia and Maoriland in the
early ’7o’s. As Scotch entertainers they
were unrivalled, and they did so well that
they didn’t move on for years. Then three
members of the co., James, Kate and Lizzie,
went to Italy to study music, and were
burned to death in the theatre at Nice on
March 23, 1881.
“The Oldest”: Apropos Allen Doone and
“The Colleen Bawn” (B. 23/7/T4). I think
Henry Edwards was the original Hardress
Cregan in Australia, and C. W. Barry the
Father Tom; likewise, unless memory fails,
Miss Fanny Young (Mrs. G. W. Daniels
and sister to Charles Young) was Anne
Chute. Some time afterwards a company of
amateurs played “Eily O’Connor,” the origi-
nal drama, for the purpose of raising funds
to put a memorial over the grave of Miss
Ellen Mortyn, one of G. V. Brooke’s com-
pany. On that occasion Miss Rosa Dunn
played Eily, and Mr. J. M. Forde Danny
Mann. It brought in enough to place a
memorial over the grave.
“C.”: What with Tith„ Essie Jenyns
Wood, Ellen Terry and a few others, the
thing that was chiefly represented at last
week’s Her Majesty’s (Sydney) matinee
was Age. Testiness was inevitably a fea-
ture of the proceedings. During rehearsals
a lady participator took exception to the
methods of one of the actors, Said he,
loftily: “Madame, at _ this stage I can
scarcely require correction. I have been 60
years on the stage.” “I should have thought
you had been 80 years on it,” she riposted,
and it was heartily agreed that the drinks
were on him. When the race-conscious G.-G.
Ferguson “went behind” after the show, by
the way, the first question he asked was,
“Where are the Bunties?” You could have
knocked some of the octogenarian loyalists
down with a meat-axe, so scandalised were
they at this callous disregard of the rights
of eld.
“Monkbarns” : Bunty sets me going on the
development of the Scot on the stage. Shaks-
peare and the Elizabethan dramatists had
very limited ideas about the extent to which
the characteristics of McParritch might
be wrought. Macklin was the first to de-
velop rich Scotch character, with Sir Pert-
inax Macsycophant, in “The Man of the
World,” and Sir Archy Mac Sarcasm in
“Love a La Mode.” After Burns had made
his impression on the world without, Scott
came with a glory of illumination, which
reached the stage in adaptations of “Rob
Roy,” “Guy Mannering,” “The Antiquary,”
“Heart of Midlothian,” and nearly all his
novels. Thenceforward the Scot trod the
planks familiarly. Macklin acted well up
to 90 years old, and lived to be 100, so they
say. He restored the true Shylock—“the
Jew that Shakspeare drew.” Yet it is re-
markable that the carrotty-headed, low-
comic Shylock appears to be the type which
Shakspeare allowed upon the stage. Scorn-
fully he may have foreseen even such a
development as Irving’s almost-chivalric
impersonation of a wronged man. Fanny.
Kemble said: “If Shakspeare saw Irving he
would re-write the play.” However, the
Shylocks of Beerbolim Tree and Asche. re-
vert to the vulgar. Tree’s is just a variant
upon his Fagin. The actor must mould
the part to his own personality. Incident-
ally, what a perfect Shylock was lost in
General Booth! On second thoughts, was
he lost —except to the stage?
“Rosna” : The death of Teddy Payne, who
has played at George Edwardes’s Gaiety
for 21 years past, removes one of
London’s best-known comedians. Payne
never changed his make-up and never
changed his management —two facts that
made a strong appeal to the average unpro-
gressive Britisher. He stood five feet four
and boasted of being the tallest man of his
family, and the ugliest.
“Keridon” : Yosi Matsumoto, the Japanese
actor of the Niblo co., saw the dress
rehearsal of “The Mikado.” Asked his
opinion of the show, he gave it promptly.
“The ladies had their kimonos folded wrong
way across front. That way was for
funerals. Of course, the Japanese of the
opera were hundreds of years ago. All
officials wear uniforms European style now.
Your executioner he make Yosi smile. No
kill now with swords. Man stands on plat-
form. Trapdoor opens. He falls and spoils
his neck. x\.nd Mr. Poo Bah he work too
much. Not like that now. The acting of
the ladies was most wrong. In Japan the
men do not kneel to ladies. Ladies kneel
to men. But this is opera style, why not i
Ladies use handkerchiefs when they cry in
the play. Japanese style is the large sleeve
to wipe the eye. Still, Yosi enjoy the morn-
ing, thank you.”
Melbourne’s ancient St. George’s Hall
(next to the Theatre Royal), now known
as Hoyt’s Picture Palace, will soon be pulled
to pieces, and a new picture theatre and a
shop or two will arise on the site of the
building where nigger minstrelsy and vaude-
ville flourished in the long ago. Hoyt’s
Ltd. have got a long lease of the ground,
which extends way back to Little Bourke-
street. The theatre in view, designed by
William Pitt, is to seat 2500 people. Dur-
ing the alterations business will be carried
on at the big hippodromic hall in Wirths’
Park, lately vacated by Spencer, and mean-
while, ere the old Bourke-strect building is
demolished, Hoyt’s will run pictures at both
places. Hoyt is the nom-de-kinematograpli
of dentist Arthur Russell, who started the
picture spec, on his own and subsequently
formed himself into a co. with £40,000
capital.
“Kaniva”: Tom Ford, recently dead in
Sydney, was for a long term an identity in
Melbourne. His step-father, W. H. Ford,
ran a theatrical costumier’s business, and
Tom was his right-hand man. Ford, sen.,
was dresser to G. Y. Brooke, who was very
partial to young Ford. The latter was a
good vocalist and banjoist. A few years ago
he returned to his native land, and there
snuffed out. He had a sister, Maggie, well
known on the stage, now resident in Syd-
ney, and better known as Mrs. Allan Hamil-
ton. W. H. Ford may be safely called the
father of the- stage, as some 80 years ago he
was appearing in an act with his parents,
who were members of Ducrow’s famous
equestrian company in London. He also
strutted his hour or so upon the stage, run-
ning shows in N.S.W. and Queensland as
far back as 60 years ago. He claims to
have given Sliiel Barry (Andy Donohue)
his first start, and was costumier to Wm.
Saurin Lyster through most of his operatic
campaign.' Although hurrying on to his
90tli year W.H.F. still enjoys fair health.

“Isaac Mclke”: Another calamitous im-
ported idea seems to be getting a grip here.
This is the rush certain managers make to
secure the services of anybody who, for some
reason or none at all, gains a little notoriety.
Now touring in a vaudeville company in
Westralia is the man who was arrested on a-,
liner at Colombo not long ago, and brought
back to stand trial in connection with an-
alleged criminal assault upon an ex-barmaid'
in a Perth-Fremantle railway carriage. Cer-
tainly the trial was sensational, and unsavory
enough; but surely that is not a sufficient:
reason why the acquitted man should be-
foisted upon a long-suffering public as at
star. Even in London, where the masses-
bow down for at least nine days and worship
anybody who emerges successfully from a
sensational trial, managers have found that
such a turn may be resented. Witness the
case of the artist who figured in a Camden
Town murder a few years ago; or more
recently, that of the girl who, with no assets
other than a brassy face, succeeded in im-
nosing upon a horde of stockbrokers, money-
lenders, etc., to the extent of fifty or sixty
thousand of the best. As music-hall turns
they were both fizzles. So was Mister John-
sing. All the same, some day I am going
to annex the mace, or the public debt, and
thereafter look for some enterprising Yank
to book me risrht away at a fabulous salary.

HOW HE HELD THEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Quoth tiie undent star mummer to one o£ to-day:
“I notice your public don’t sit out the play;
In my time the audience seemed to have brains.
They stayed in their seats, sir, none bolted for
trains!
“Till the very last words of the author I spoke,
Not a soul in the house from the spell e’er awoke.
My acting, dear lad, was magnetic, in fact.
To-day where are they, sir, who really can act?
“Egad, but I held them! I see them there still;
All hypnotised, mesmerised —slaves of my will!
The press would oft say of my great mumming feats:
Tie had all his audience glued to their seats 1’”
The speaker, his face simply glowing with pride,
Paused awhile and the younger pro. slowly replied:
“The audience glued to the seats ! I declare
That’s a very cute method of keeping them there!”

“F.P.C.”: At last, it seems, the British
War Office has got the hang of the prize
rifle-shooting business ; and it is announced
that, on the other side, all prize meetings
must be carried out under Service conditions.
The tremendous change which this implies
may be judged by the comment of an expert
THE INVALID'S DIGNITY.
NURSE (to suffering patient who has been trampled on): “This is the third time you
have been brought here in this condition. Why are you so foolish as to continue
to live with such a brute of a husband? ”
PATIENT: “ What's that got to do with you? It's the business of the likes of you to
attend to the likes of me."
that, “if the King’s were conducted under
purely Service conditions, the chances are
the merest novice would easily win against
a crack shot.” Naturally, then, the change
does not suit the pot-hunters who shoot
under conditions that are.not warlike. Un-
der the conditions they have been used to, the
ranges are carefully cleared, accurately
measured, and each shot is marked. Under
Service conditions shooting would take place
over unmeasured ranges, the country would
be rough, each shot would not be marked,
and five cartridges would be loaded into the
magazine, as against one. Under the present
Defence system, we are keeping two armies,
one that can manoeuvre and can’t shoot, and
one that can shoot and can’t manoeuvre.
What the man from the Bogan wants to know
is why both armies can’t shoot and manoe-
uvre and thus be one army? Plainly it is a
rotten return for the millions we spend when,
in the final result, the novice can beat the
supposed “crack” when the “crack” gets
down to real business.
* * * *
A recent witness in a Richmond (Vic.)
police court objected bitterly to being termed
an “immigrant.” She hadn’t come there to
be insulted, being a lady, however appear-
ances might be against her, and a respectable
woman into the bargain, and so on. The
hard, cold facts were that she had arrived in
Melbourne from the Cold Land a week before,
and met in Bourke-street a charming lad who
took her to Sandringham. At Sand-
ringham they drunk heartily to one
another’s bright eyes, and then cast
themselves —or mayhap fell—beneath a
ti-tree to rest. When the lady came
to, at five o’clock the next morning, her
gentleman friend had fled. and with
him a diamond-and-ruby gold ring, a gold
cameo ling, a pearl-and-sapphire gold ring, a
gold snake ring, a star brooch and her repu-
tation. In Paris they call the type of man
who puts it across a trusting and affec-
tionate female a “rasta,” and it is regarded
as a term of detested obloquy. But what on
earth is the matter with “immigrant”?
* * * *
“The Oldest Inhabitant” produces another
pub :
An ancient Sydney pub will come under the
hammer within a week or two. This is the River-
view Hotel, on Cook’s River-road. Sixty years ago
it was the Bold Forester, kept by Levi Barden.
Cook’s River-road had then some haughty homes on
it, and some historic beer-pumps. There was a
Pulteney Hotel, kept by George Rose, and a Star
and Garter, kept by Thomas J. Bown, subsequently
distinguished as a fire-fighter. The Duiguids of the
original Commercial Bank lived out that way; so
did F. W. Unwin, the old-time solicitor; William
Fanning, from whose house Huxley, the scientist, got
his wife; Tom Holt; Michael Metcalfe, the early
underwriter; and Win. Savigny, who ran a school,
now historic; Sam 11. Terry; and Talbot, a wool-
broker. Some of the fine old mansions still stand,
huddled up amongst jerry-built cottages and heaps
of brick-dust.
The curious fact was lately men-
tioned in a daily paper that while
Sydney is 50 miles from Gosford
by railway it is 200 miles by road.
Some forgetful cove left a gap of
14 miles in the highway, and the
gap remains to this day. Conse-
quently the foot-traveller is ex-
pected to go round by Newcastle,
Maitland, Wollombi, Wiseman’s
Kerry, etc., and but for the bless-
ing of Providence he might have
to go round by Timbuctoo.
AT THE CLUB.
MAJOR L APITUP: “Haw. I was looking up my family tree yesterday.”
VICTLM: “Bottle tree , I suppose.”
Westralia’s action in handing to Victoria
a sex aberrationist gives a South Australian
parson, J. C. Kirby, a tremendous advertise-
ment for his pet scheme of dealing with such
cases surgically. What is the sense, he asks,
in either flogging, life-long imprisonment or
release with its chance of further ruin for
girl-children?
1 have great respect for the Salvation others, but
by all their prayerful activities they cannot alter the
constitutional pathologic basis of a man’s abnormal
impulses.... The combined Salvation Army and the
whole army of doctors cannot guarantee that the
sex-perversion has been taken out of Bennet.... Max
Nordau says that the erotomaniac stands in the
same position to the other sex as a dipsomaniac does
to intoxicating drink. Something must be done for
the safety of Society... .In Indiana, California and
New York the law in such cases is to asexualise, by
a method, nearly painless, called vasectomy. I have
a little pamphlet from Dr. Shary, of Indiana, the
Government physician. He has operated on 236
cases. There is no expense, he says, to the State,
no sorrow or shame to the friends, and there is no
lifelong imprisonment for the person who suffers
from these moral aberrations. The thing is over in
a few days, and the nrisoner released. Moreover, the
person is released from these fearful impulses during
the rest of his life. This matter is quite distinct
from the general question of the sterilisation of the
feeble-minded, and deals only with those who are
sex-perverts, and who hitherto have been dealt with
as criminals, whereas they should be dealt with as
constitutionally diseased.
Caldwell, a former Adelaide M.P., got on to
the same subject years ago, and made many
converts ; now, with the tremendously ener-
getic Kirby hammering at it, it looks highly
probable that the time is getting ripe for S.
Aus. to take another more-or-less startling
legislative lead.
“10-2-4” : There is a sociable international
soap factory in Sydney. The manager is an
Australian-Britisher, and with him are asso-
ciated two Australians, a Russian and a
Frenchman. They are all excellent friends
and their one desire is to go on quietly mak-
ing soap for Australia. But three great
European Powers have reached out for their
respective men and the useful soap business
is threatened with extinction, while the
soap-makers go away to Europe at tremen-
dous expense, to lie on their stomachs and
shoot for their various homelands. An
Australian artist, who knows and likes them
all, and doesn’t want to lose their company,
is trying to arrange a four-handed duel with
soft-soap in the Australian works, the result
to be cabled to the various European head-
quarters, after peace has been honorably con-
cluded and the place tidied up for the next
day’s business. It would represent a tre-
mendous saving in time, money and hospital
expenses—to say nothing of broken friend-
ships. But the European lunatic asylum
isn’t likely to listen to such well-soaped
reason. At present there are too many
vuns going off for Reason to make herself
heard.
Alberto Terrace, a landmark in Darling-
hurst-road, Sydney, has been sold for £65,000.
The terrace, which consists of IS four-storey
houses, was built by degrees and old John
Solomon. When first completed it was
known as “Solomon’s Glory,” and to be
quite fashionable it was sufficient to rent a
chunk of it. In recent years the well-to-do
have moved their distinguished patronage
from Darlinghurst, and the sign of the Hash
House is over Solomon’s erstwhile pomp.
Nowadays his radiance is reflected from his
descendants—a host in Sydney Jewry.

“Rule Britannia” : She was the type of
loyal British matron who doesn’t understand
the cables and puts all her eggs on the
British throne—if I may use such a vulgar
expression. The gentleman-caller had been
explaining to the best of his ability the
tangled international trouble, and she had
been giving him a dignified if somewhat
impatient hearing. When at last he had
laid bare the secrets of the diplomatic world
and shown how the leading nations and the
innumerable official figureheads were starting
out to wreck the temple of white- civilisation
and set fire to history, she drew a deep
British breath and remarked severely: “/
suppose King George could stop all this if
he wanted to!”
Melbourne solicitors and amalgams are
deploring a remarkable decline in Supreme
and County Court business. The sort of
litigant who used to risk a half-crown to
gain a shilling, and who usually lost the
two and six proceeds nowadays in the less
expensive way of Petty Sessions. Neverlhe-
less the number of civil cases heard in Petty
Sessions in Victoria is only about 18 per
cent, higher than it was 25 years ago,
whereas the Supreme and County Court
cases have in the same period dwindled down
to one-tentli and one-twentieth of their
former numerosity. Which indicates that
the Melb. lawyers, although they didn’t slay
the goose that laid the golden eggs, have so
scared the bird that she has ceased to be a
prolific layer. But still there is too much
money “earned” by licensed practitioners in
the business which costs the Government so
many thousands a year for the upkeep of
courts and judges. When are litigants and
property buyers and sellers to be supplied
with tfie services of salaried Government
amalgams? When is the Government that
pays the heavy expenses incidental to the
conduct of legal business going to get some
of the profits in part repayment of its ex-
penditure? It is a question which might
very well be hurled at all the lawyer candi-
dates for Parliament in all the 2cates.
QUERY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T note the latest war scare and the cableman’s
alarms ;
I learn that “stocks are falling,” and that Russia’s
up in arms,
And 1 wonder —yea, by thunder !
What a d —d official blunder,
And troubles “international,”
Most shockingly irrational,
Are thought of by the peasants who are farming on
their farms.
When Austria and Servia are both “upon the brink,”
And Italy is waiting for her mighty ally’s wink,
And France is getting ready
For a plunge into the eddy,
And our Fatmen’s conversations
Are of “foreign complications,”
Then I wonder what the workers in the factories
must think.
When the toffs who rule the people and are sumptu-
ously fed
Extol the Nation’s Glory and the fighters who are
dead ;
When the journals publish leaders
Full of butter for the bleeders,
And talk with great verbosity
Of racial animosity,
The nation’s trouble mostly is the rising price of
bread.
Oh, the masses! When their heroes are “responding
to the call,”
And the stay-at-homes are singing “Let me like a
soldier fall” ;
When the Ministers meet daily,
And the Flag is flying gaily,
And lords and dukes lunatical
Are densely diplomatical,
Oh, the masses, patient asses! Do they ever think
at all?
VICTORIA.
O. K.


PURIFIED PETROLEUM AS A HEALIN6
REMEDY FOR DIGESTIVE DISORDERS.
The value of purified petroleum as a healing
remedy for digestive and bowel disorders is
now universally recognised. Unlike animal
or vegetable oils, petroleum passes through
the stomach unchanged, hence its soothing,
lubricating, disinfectant action is exercised
throughout the entire alimentary tract. To
obtain the best results from petroleum, it
should be administered in the form of an
emulsion. In this form, the petroleum is
broken up into minute globules which mingle
freely with the contents of the stomach
and are thus evenly distributed throughout
the digestive area, where the.r soothing,
healing powers are exerted to the fullest
extent.
The original and standard preparation of
petroleum for internal use is Angier s Emul-
sion, which has been largely employed by the
medical profession ever since 1892. The
petroleum in Angier’s Emulsion is purified by
a special process and combined with the
hypophosphites of lime and soda in the form
of a perfect Emulsion that is pleasant to the
palate and acceptable to the most delicate
stomach.
Apart from its soothing, healing properties
Angier’s Emulsion (because of the hypo-
phosphites which it contains) exerts a marked
tonic, invigorating influence, which makes it
of value in a wide variety of disorders. The
medical profession prescribe it extensively not
only for lung troubles and wasting diseases,
but even more largely for gastric catarrh,
gastric ulcer, mucous colitis, nervous dys-
pepsia, chronic constipation and in many
obscure digestive and bowel disorders. Of all
chemists at 1/3, 2/9 and 4/6,

THE SAVING CLAUSE.
“All men are such beasts.”
"Ah, not all of them.”
"Well, all the nice ones.”
The tips-in-lieu-of-wages policy cropped up
at Melbourne Police Court the other day,
when a Y.M.C.A. janitor excused a trifling
theft on the ground that his wages were only
£1 a week. The Y.M.C.A. countered with
the claim that he got “quite a lot
in tips.” What right the Ass. had to take
advantage of the assumed generosity of a
few patrons to grossly sweat an employee it
did not pause to explain.
THE COMEPLIMEET.
JESSIE: “ Please, Auntie , the new lady next door says her compliments, and will you play
very tow, because her husband is extremely musical.'”
A Maoriland murder case 20 years old
was dragged into the limelight again at
Wellington a week or so ago. For about
five years prior to 1895 Minnie Dean carried
on a baby farm at Winton (M.L.), and
during that time slew several infants.
In fact, all the infants committed to
her care disappeared, including one
named Colin Scoullar McLachlan, an
illegitimate child, who had been en-
trusted to her from Oamaru about
1890. In 1895 the law fell like a
leaning tower on Minnie, and she
died suddenly one morning in Inver-
cargill Gaol, which is a cold, wet
place to die in. The father of the
child McLachlan had died in 1894,
and the codicil of his will
left certain moneys to his
illegitimate offspring. This
cash was reserved by the
Public Trustee on the
grounds that the child
might some day be found
alive. There wasn’t suf-
ficient. in the estate to
pay the other legacies in
full, and so the surviving
legatees (both in Scot-
land) recently applied to
have the amount reserved
for the youngster paid to
them. It was pointed out
to the Judge that no one
could prove that the child
was dead and that the
Court was up against a
stone wall. But Chief
Justice Stout ruled that
this was a case for com-
mon sense rather than
mere law. He stated
that he had no doubt in
his own vast intellect
that Minnie Dean mur-
dered the child a year or
two before the codicil was
made, and that in conse-
quence the gift to the child really never
took place. So he ordered the Public Trus-
tee to hand the spare cash to the legatees,
who live in the land where business is busi-
ness.
Sydney Telegraph said last week that the
outlook of war in Europe had completely
diverted interest from the Australian elec-
tion. But it didn’t divert attention from
the horse. Last week the person
whose name appears underneath saw a surg-
ing crowd round some notice outside a pub.
Thinking it might, be news of a decisive
battle that fat man (his name appears
underneath) ran as hard as his boiler could
drive him to read. After fighting and but-
ting his way through the mob he discovered
it was the result of a pony race meeting.
Ten yards away there was an announcement
about the war, and not a soul was looking
at it.
[C. A. Jeffries, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
A Kalgoorlie woman lias just taken a
fall out of Westralia’s obsolete impristm-
ment-for-debt law. Only the bailiff can
arrest the man or woman who is ordered by
the Court to pay tip, or turn up and explain
why it isn’t done. The goldfields lady
settled matters by simply going to bed. For
the poor bailiff and his two assistants she
wouldn’t budge an inch, and she preserved
the same magnificent inactivity when a
policeman was called in. He was power-
less unless she smote with her feet or hands
or tore his uniform. She did none of these
things, but snuggled down quite comfortably.
So the law is at a standstill.
Europe carries about five royalties to the
acre, and, up to the last black hours that
fled before Armageddon, they had spent most
of their tame lives trotting about pleasure
resorts and getting snapshotted far from
their frowning, ancestral palaces, and hic-
cupping ancestral wine-cellars. When
European war broke out in real earnest for
the first time in many years all these assorted
kings spilt their assorted drinks, upset the
cafe tables, and, shouting to their suites to
leave the luggage and follow at top speed,
stampeded towards the nearest railway sta-
tion, wharf or garage. There hasn’t been
such a rush of kings for quite a while. Dur-
ing the past week or so only the most
remote European escaped being bowled over
and trampled on by a frenzied royal person,
who was issuing mobilisation orders as he
ran, and declaring martial law with his
feet.
In Melbourne, where the thermometer has
lately been doing its best to sit on the lap
of zero, a plea has been raised for the post-
man, in his sweltering uniform of red and
blue. Mrs. A. Baillieu, as president of the
St. Kilda branch of the People’s Liberal
party, led the wail. She wrote to the
Department that, during last summer, her
heart palpitated for the panting., perspiring
postman, who went his round with his coat
in one hand, his bag of letters in the other
and his cork helmet crushed under his arm.
The Postal folk were guilty of gross cruelty
in not providing its letter deliverers with
lighter uniforms, she said, and suggested
that, in the coming summer, the postman
should be dressed in khaki, like the boy
scout. This military uniform did not ap-
peal to the Postal heads; nevertheless Mrs.
Baillieu’s pathetic picture moved them into
announcing that official sanction had been
given to have summer tunics made without
lining and the stiff collar-band. Another
victory for the Liberal party !

“Melb.” hires a taxi for half-an-hour:—
Parer’s venerable Duke Restaurant, in Bourke-
street, is closed, and will be replaced by the Star
Picture Theatre, which has lately occupied the Wax-
works. Parer’s restaurant replaced the Canterbury
Music Hall, established in opposition to the Surrey
Music Hall, Melbourne’ first theatre, known at differ-
ent times also as the Victoria and the Royal, near
the Bull and Mouth. There is a tradition also of an
Adelphi Theatre in Flinders-lane. The Waxworks
were established elsewhere by Mrs. Williams, in emula-
tion of Madame Tussaud. She married Sohier, a
phrenologist, who advertised always: “Know Thyself.”
The present site of the Waxworks, before occupancy
by Kreitmayer, was the Casino de Venise, leased at
one time by Joe Thompson, the bookmaker. The
Waxworks have been kept up at the rear of the
Star Theatre. The three Parer brothers, Steve, Phil
and Johnny, established the Duke about 55 years
ago. Another Spaniard was Arenas, who kept the
Spanish Restaurant, near Cole’s Book Arcade. A
third was Rubira, who mounted from the John Bull
Tavern, in Little Collir.s-street, to Ilosie’s, in Bourke-
street, where he remains. Mrs. Rubira was Miss
Guidotti, of the Opera, and she was Spanish, too.
Then there was the charitable Be Freitas, the Por-
tugee, with Iris Mechanics’ Restaurant, where the
famous “eight o'clock rush” used to occur when the
boom broke. When Greek meets Spaniard then comes
the tug-of-war. The Greeks lord it in Melbourne
restaurants, and the Spaniards have been driven
back. The Parers gave up the Duke for Nissen's
Crystal Cafe, and they have sold that, too, the pur-
chasers being the Centreway Co., front Collins-street.

“ Jeames” :—-
The cabled intimation that Mr. Freeman-Mitford
wants to have the editor of the London Sporting
Times criminally prosecuted was the first hint the
general public had that the recent marriage of Lord
Redesdale's eon and Mr. Friedlander-Fuld’s daughter
had been a failure. Young Freeman-Mitford was in
the diplomatic service. He secured last January in
the Friedlander-Fuld maiden the matrimonial catch
of Germany, if not of the world. Her father
announced his intention of taking him into the busi-
ness, as was done in the case of the j’oung doctor
who married the heiress to the Krupp billions. And
now they are separated, and He Wend Fenton, of the
Spouting Times, has levelled at the luckless Freeman-
Mitford the charge which thousands of Englishmen
and thousands of Germans, of a certain type, have to
sustain, either privately or publicly, nowadays. The
victims of these dreadful accusations are mostly
either prominent politicians or effeminate-looking
youths, about whom nothing much is known except
that the3 r wear side-whiskers, and tint their waists
are too pronounced and their hair too long. The
framers of the indictments are usually strenuous
characters who have been accused themselves
of pretty well every offence barring this, in
their time. De Wend Fenton, for example,
was regarded as “pie hot” —to borrow the
phrase of agonised backers —when on the turf. Some
of his efforts in the riding line were masterly in
their impudence. When he was selected to edit the
Spouting Times, in succession to John Corlett,
“Pitcher,” Colonel Newnham Davis, and others of the
staff were so scandalised that they incontinently aban-
doned their billets. He is a picturesque writer, and
has turned out a capable editor (what he doesn’t
know about London and the world of sport
being a negligible quantity) ; but he has landed
England’s leading purveyor of journalistic obscenities
in more trouble in the course of a couple of years
than his predecessor did during quarter of a century.
On the other hand, he has never been even so much
as dimly suspected of wearing side-levers. Freeman-
Mitford, assuming his case is good, will probably have
at Fenton on a criminal libel charge, though it is
notoriously hard to get a British jury to convict on
this issue, and infernally unpleasant for the plaintiff
if it acquits

“Billy Blue” : One of the oldest pubs in
the suburbs of Sydney is the Bald Faced
Stag, in crowded Leichhardt. It was first
opened in that name in 1849, by the late
Charles Hearn, who was born at Camden
(N.S.W.), in 1816. His father, Abraham,
a Gloucester man, had a farm at Camden ;
but it wasn’t a success, so he opened the
Spread Eagle Hotel on Brickfield Hill,
Sydney. After a few years he went to
South Creek, and then purchased some land
from Captain Piper, at the corner of the
Balmain and Parramatta roads, and in 1831
built there the Australian Hotel. In 1849
his son Charles took it over, and changed its
name to the Bald Faced Stag, and the Bald
Faced Stag it has been ever since. The
original humpy was long and low, with a
verandah all round. In front was a horse-
trough, cut out of a huge ironbark tree,
while over it was hung on another stout
ironbark post a swinging sign with a bald-
faced stag painted on either side. Charlie
Hearn and his pub were famous for the
fruit grown at the back in a spacious
orchard, and visitors from Sydney during
the fruit season were numerous. Charlie,
the orchard and the original pub are now
all gone, and a brick building, flush to the
footpath, supplies the wants of the thirsty
present-day foot-travellers. In the old
days you took a nice drive out into the
country to reach the animal with no hair
on its countenance.
THE DESPERADOES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Prompt action on the part of Mr. Clarence Blaki-
son, who has come to Australia to join Miss Nellie
Stewart in the Mine, du Barry company, saved the
life of Quartermaster Crouch, of the Orontes, in
Melbourne on Tuesday. Crouch slipped off the gang-
way while walking' aboard, and Mr. Blakison and
Albert Nayler, A. 8., one of the crew, saw Crouch’s
predicament, and running down the gangway they
slid down the piles and leaning over the side of the
tender they caught hold of Crouch and rescued him.
- Thrilling item from Sydney Sun.
When careless Quartermaster Crouch fell headlong in
the sea
The multitude on ship and pier were shocked to a
degree.
The Waters round the Melbourne wharves are brown
and cold and deep;
The very place where one might start on his eternal
sleep.
But Mr. Clarence Blakison and Nayler—-the A.B. —
Were fortunately standing near. They could not
choose but see
That Crouch had fallen overboard, and sharply as a
knife
They pledged themselves, at any cost, to save the
latter’s life.
Now see where Clarence Blakison creeps to the
water’s brim,
And mark how Nayler, the A. 8., unfearing follows
him.
They lie upon the tender’s deck as though it were a
couch,
And, while the welkin splits with cheers, extend their
hands to Crouch.
The perils of the enterprise are disregarded quite,
In view of Quartermaster Crouch and his distressing
plight;
With good old British pluck they haul until, ’mid
yell and shout
And rapturous acclaim, they drag the Quartermaster
out.
“Death or the Abbey!” Nelson cried the day he
sealed his fame ;
Nayler, A. 8., and Blakison no doubt said much the
same
What time they grabbed their dripping friend, and
felt the tender list:
Almost one hears their deathless words: “Crouch or
an injured. wrist!”
N.S.W.

Yankee parson Charles Sheldon, author of
“In Ilis Steps.” and originator of the anti-
saloon crusade in the United States, has
arrived in Australia on a tour of redemption.
The Redeemer had John the Baptist- as
forerunner. Sheldon, more up to date, is
heralded by J. and N. Tait.
A line from Melbourne; —
Harold Hansell, who published “Dewdrop Dauby”
and other books of frisky humor what time he was
clerking for the Bank of Victoria in Melbourne, is
understood to be sighing for a billet in the Common-
wealth Bank. So white and bright a young Aus-
tralian should not long sigh in vain. Hansell left
the Bank of Victoria because its anti-Labor prin-
ciples of management hurt him sorely. Having tried
other things he reckoned that banking is in his bones,
and the Government institution evidently ought to
be their resting place.
Last Thursday the Federal election scored
its first victim. Thomas Reeves, of Cudge-
gong (N.S.W.), a man aged 71, remembered
suddenly that he had put himself on the roll
in two places, being also recorded as a voter
at Appin (N.S.W.). There is hardly a
smaller place on earth than Appin—save
Cudgegong. And it is 25,000,000,000 to
one that nobody would have noticed or cared
for Thomas Reeves, or mobilised the Federal
army on his account. But the sensitive
patriarch couldn’t stand the “disgrace,” and
shot himself.
MILESTONES ON THE PANAMA
ROUTE.
Regularly every mouth For a long time past
batches of Chinese, averaging about a hundred each
trip, have passed through Sydney on their way to
Tahiti, which is rapidly becoming a Chinese colony.
—S. M. HERALD.
The murdered Archduke Franz Ferdinand,
whose ghost is the cause of so much shindy
in Europe, when visiting Australia about
20 years ago, stayed for some days at Narra-
mine, Frank Mack’s station, in the Dubbo
(N.S.W.) district, where he was initiated
into the pleasures of kangaroo hunting, 'lie
was an extremely quiet personage, and
apparently had no desire to kill anybody
under the sun. - v
There may be one or two, but there cer-
tainly can’t be many, older native-born of"
Victoria than Edward Randall Scott Staf-
ford, who is still a brisk figure at St. Kilda.
His troubles began in Elizabeth-street, near
Collins-street, on February 2S, 1838. His
father had charge of the Customs at the time.
The son was long in the Forestry Depart-
ment, and was a well-known “sport” in his
day. Incidentally he is something of a
botanist, and Avas the first to grow prairie
grass in Australia. His birth certificate was
handed out by Bishop Broughton.
Norman Lockhart, solicitor, of the N.S.W.
Crown Law Department, lnls fled for Eng-
land to assist the Bar retained by the Hol-
man Government to resist the appeal to the
Privy Council in connection with the firing
of the Governor-General, his swag and camp
followers from old Government House, Syd-
ney. He has an awkward job. Profession-
ally. he has to do his best to keep the Domain
Bastille for the people; socially, the local
Due d’Orleans and the duchess will never
forgive him if he doesn’t succeed in losing
the case and winning back the ballroom.
“Gordon G.”: Recently haled before The
Great Magistrate, Michael Morris, one of
Vic.’s best known J’s.P. He held the record
for being the only honorary Justice in Aus-
tralia who had sentenced a prosecuting ser-
geant or inspector of police for contempt.
The circumstance arose during the hearing
of a small case at Minyip (Vic.). The offi-
cer who appeared to prosecute at a critical
stage audibly opined that the Bench (Morris,
chairman) hadn’t the intellect of a roasted
peanut, or thereabouts. Morris, who was
nothing if not prompt, immediately pro-
nounced a sentence of five hours, and ad-
journed the Court. Of course, the offender
hurriedly apologised, and the apology was
courteously and generously accepted. The
business of the Court proceeded. The party
with the apology was O’Callaghan, sometime
Chief Commish of Victorian police.
There is only one survivor left, in Aus-
tralia, of McDouall Stuart’s transconti-
nental journey in 1862. The jubilee of it was
kept with some elaboration, but three of the
veterans have dropped since then, and the
52nd anniversary was noted simply by a
friendly call, by some of the S.A. Geogra-
phical Society, on Stephen King at his home
in an Adelaide suburb. There is one more
member of the little party living, J. W.
Billiatt, but he lias long been in England.
The names of the whole party have been
engraved on the pedestal of Stuart’s statue
in Victoria Square, Adelaide.
William Russell Wilson Bligli, a
descendant of Captain William Bligh, famed
as the cause of two mutinies —those of the
Bounty and Botany Bay—died at Black-
lieatli (N.S.W.) in May last, aged over SO,
and left an estate valued at £25,977. He
was a crusty bachelor, and gave his house-
hold effects and the use of his town and
country residences to his niece so long as
she remained unmarried. The residue of
the estate he left to trustees to convert into
money, for the benefit of his nieces, but on
much the same terms as to marriage. Pre-
sumably the old misogynist (or did he
merely hate husbands?) “took the law of
it”; but, if it’s right, the sooner it is made
wrong to penalise any woman for marrying,
the better.
Lionel Robinson, the wealthy London
stockbroker, who has just bought a fine pro-
perty near London for £60,000, is a son of
the late A. B. Robinson, for many years
commercial editor of the Age, and a brother
of Arthur Robinson, M.L.C. He showed
unusually early a keen faculty for making
money. A Melbourne judge relates that
when Lionel was only 13, and living in the
Royal Park with his (the judge’s) family,
he hurried in one morning before breakfast
to see the judge (then a barrister), and
harangued him about some shares. The
barrister listened to the youth’s clever argu-
ments, and said, “If you talk like that at
court one day you’ll make plenty of money.”
Lionel replied that he was going to make
money, but not in court.
“Rapid Fire”: I have heard some silly
rumor that Sir Edward Grey, the attempted
peacemaker of the world, is, in English pub-
lic life, Secretary of State for Foreign
Affairs. I have also heard it asserted that,
in private life, he is a cultured widower with
a taste for fishing. I reject both of these
stories as absurd. I am certain in my own
mind that Grey is a well-meaning curate
whose lodgings are adjacent to a particularly
rowdy tenement house called Europe Build-
ings. When the first screams of the present
disastrous feud broke out, the Rev. Mr. Grey
put on his black hat and fled round the
corner to flutter two white dove-like hands in
the enraged faces of the contending parties,
while he begged them in anguished tones not
to do so, and to make friends, like dear, good
people. Several times in the past he pulled
off the amazing trick and saved the general
conflagration, the intending combatants
being too amazed at his fluttering intrusion
to concentrate on the business on hand. But
the whole tindery place is ablaze at last, and
the well-meaning peacemaker in the black
coat can only moan protestingly at a dis-
tance, while the heat scorches his eyebrows
and melts the nozzles of the fire-brigade.
MAJOR CAHILL , Queensland Commissioner
of Police.

count for much, but Servia has floundered
into a hopeless-looking war under the man-
agement of a Cabinet which consists of
Patehu, Pashitcli, Protitcli. Stephanovitch.
Janovitcli. Jankovitch, Javanovitch and
Djouritevitch. [And the row with Austria
was mostly over the murderous deeds of
Tankovitch and Giganovitch. So King
Peter Karageorgevitch has fled from his capi-
tal. The whole foolish country is bevitched.
“The Link” writes :
0. W. Leadbeatbr, one-time priest of the Church
of England, now' Chief Panjandrum and Supreme
Mugwump of Theosophy (under Annie Besant — r o
connection with ariy of the half-dozen other Thioso-
phieal societies in- existence), descended suddenly mi
Auckland, M.L., two weeks ago, delivered two public
addresses,, held various secret conclaves with the
faithful in these parts, and then scooted off to Syd-
ney. His two addresses (subjects, “Life After Death”
and “The Doctrine of Re« Birth”) consisted of dull
and dreary twaddle as old as Pythagoras or the
Egyptians from whom lie derived his ideas. C.W.L.
pretends -that he has personally visited “the astral
plane” and acquired knowledge at first hand; but.
if so, he has nothing to show for it. He also
claims to have stood with Mrs. Besant in the
presence of the Supreme Director of Evolution on
the globe; and on the strength of this he intimated
some time ago that it was quite impossible for “dear
Annie” to tell a lie, and therefore everything she
said must be accepted unquestioningly by all good
Theosophists. A peculiar attribute of this head-
beater is ‘that be must not on any account defend
his character against attack. Hence, when a year
ago he was flagrantly libelled in Adyar (Madras),
where he lives, he simply lay down under it with
saintly resignation. Some of his less divine asso-
ciates, however, took action —brought, in fact, three
libel actions,' and lost the lot! Another privilege
of this Great Occultist is that he must not be ques-
tioned —he knows how much to tell you, and there’s
an end on’t! His first lecture in Auckland was an
elementary talk about “spooks,” showing that High
Theosophy apparently ends where spiritualism begins.
When bead beater was last in Christchurch his chair-
man innocently announced at the close of the lecture
that the Great Occultist would now reply to ques-
tions, whereupon the Supreme Mugwump fled preci-
pitately from the platform, hid himself in a dark
room, rent his raiment and shouted “Blasphemy!”
(or something of the sort). At any rate, lie could
not be persuaded to face the audience again. At
Auckland he was too well guarded for anything of
that kind to take place. His place of abode was
unknown, and no person was allowed to have access
to him.
ELECTION LOGIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. ,1. Bennett, the Fusion candidate for Gipps-
land, who is opposing J. 11. Wise, said he was in
favor of the initiative and referendum, and also Pro-
tection, but he would vote against these measures if
his vote for them would defeat his party.—Aue.
“The Referendum is a thing
• 1 always have respected.
Into the fight for it I’ll fling
Myself, if I’m elected.
And should it come to pass some day
That there is danger that it may
Win through—l’ll vote the other way I
“Moreover, for Protection high
These many years I’ve spouted.
But if 1 see il coming 1
Will promptly have it outed!
For other measures, too, I’ve yearned ;
And should it chance that I’m returned,
I'll do my best to have them spurned!”
An independent Liberal bloke—
Not Caucus-bound, like Labor —
It was who in this manner spoke.
A cit. addressed his neighbor:
“This Bennett is the best I know ;
1 like his politics, and so
To G. 11. Wise my vote will go!”
[Written by B. J. 11. Owen, who may be found
at 108 Albert-street, Fast Melbourne, or at the pub
round the corner, in Victoria Parade.]
The Bulletin has one far-sighted con-
tributor, anyhow. That is the verse-smith
who, years ago, pegged out the pen-name of
R. McGeddon.
Tommy Bath, Minister for Lands in
Seaddan’s Wcstralian Ministry, has deter-
mined to take a spell from that political job.
He is-off to Ban Francisco Exhibition as
Commissioner for the Groper State. He
will do as well there as the next man; and,
anyway, he deserves a kind word for his
good-hearted management of affairs at the
Lands Office.
Politicians differ from actors in being their
own press-agents. Benjamin Benny, a highly
respectable lawyer of Adelaide, being a Lib-
eral candidate for the Senate, has announced
carefully that he is not to be confused with
either Penny or Denny, who both are local
members of the same profession. Bertram
Penny jumps joyously into the discussion
with an explanation of how different he is
from Benny. The only contribution by
“Bill” Denny-—who is by far the best known
of the three, having been Attorney-General
in Verran’s Government—is a Bernard Shaw-
like remark that “the only chance Mr.
Benny ever had was in being confused with
someone else.” But the chance for confu-
sion of names is not a patch on the position
of a generation back, when Hay, Bray and
Way were three of the foremost names in
Adelaide, closely pursued by Jay and Kay,
Gay and Gray, May and Ray, and quite a
quantity of Day.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Fven before Germany and Russia had been
drawn into the war, all tlie ’principal Stock
Exchanges of Europe had been closed against
business. This is the first time the London
Exchange’s doors have been shut. Almost
simultaneously, in order to check the out-
flow of gold, the Bank discount rate was put
up to 10 p.c.—a/rise of 7 p.c. in three days.
For over 40 years it had never ex-
ceeded 7 p.c. This extraordinary advance,
coupled with the subsequent suspension of
specie payment-—in other words, the making
of Bank of England notes legal tender —had
a shivery effect in Australia. The signifi-
cance of the steps was pretty generally mis-
read. European countries . were naturally
straining themselves to got gold, and a
potential enemy’s gold for choice, and it
was necessary to do something drastic in
order to make the coin available for possible
national needs. Britain’s own paper is good
enough within the country ; but it is no good
outside. On the other hand, gold talks every-
where ; and there is no telling where the talk-
ing may have to be done. Fortunately
Monday was a Bank and Exchange holiday
in Australia, otherwise the London news
might have had a more distuibing effect than
it had on Tuesday, when the difference
between our position and Britain’s was a
little better understood. As a matter of
fact, there had been far too much panicky
selling of first-class securities in the previous
week. Several bank stocks suffered very
severely; yet, in view of their considerable
holdings in London, where call money is now
worth probably 7 p.c., the chances are they
are making bigger profits than they did
before the world became a seething pot of
devil’s-brotli.
As for the financial institutions them-
selves. Australia owes a great deal to those
managements which have for some time past
been gradually strengthening their cash posi-
tion. until it can be said with the utmost
confidence that, taken as a whole, Australian
banks were never in so good a position to
meet a crisis as they are to-day. The com-
plete Australasian figures for the June
quarter are not available yet; but for the
March quarter the l Australian banks’ coin
and bullion and Commonwealth notes (which
are backed by coin and bullion) were equal
to 56.80 p.c. of all the banks’ liabilities at
call; and invariably, as the wool and other
payments are made, there is an increase,
usually of from 2 p.c. to 6 p.c., within the
next quarter. It is quite safe, then, to say
ll at the banks in Australia could at this
moment pay in coin and legal-tender notes
69 p.c. of all that they owe on current
account and such-like; and in coin alone,
exclusive of notes, they could pay 51 p.c. of
all their liabilities at call. When the deposi-
tor wakes up at 2 a.m. and remembers his
own few pounds of credit balance, and re-
flects that there is £36,000,000 or more in
coin and bullion in the banks’ tills and
safes to pay him with, be may turn over
and go to sleep like a sensible man. In the
third quarter of 1892, when (lie banks owed
an enormous amount of money on so called
“fixed” deposit to English and Scotch in-
vestors, all they had was £20,586,000 in coin
and bullion. Compare those two figures—
-20 millions then and 36 millions now—and
you get some idea of tlie remarkable change
for tlie better which lias come over tlie Aus-
tralian banking position.

This co. has a few hundred audits to pre-
pare for besides its own ; and as it found
that it couldn’t abolish tlie Christmas gorge
it decided to abandon December to the gorge
and put its own extra work into June.
Hence the figures in the last line are for 18
months. Averaging tilings, tin 1 profit for 12
months would be £14,284, which shows a
slight shrinkage. However, as 64 months’
profits were sufficient to pay the 18 months’
dividend at the rate of 10 p.c.. shareholders
haven't much cause to complain. It is in-
deed au extraordinarily profitable business.
Here is a co. which need scarcely risk its
principal, and is still able to show a shade
over 10 p.c. p.a. of profit on its total funds.
Yet while making this profit it and others
like it are unquestionably doing good work
for the public. Before the permanent trustee
was invented, a man making a will never
knew who would administer his estate when
he was too dead to do it himself. lie pro-
bably chose a friend, but the friend was no
more immortal than he. In fact, especially
if he were a man not old, he inclined to put
his trust in one older than himself. If his
executor died, that executor’s executor was
(and still is) entitled to take up the job;
and this latter executor might he a man
whom the original testator wouldn’t trust
with tuppence. Even if the man named as
executor lived he might shift his quarters
or change his habits. The trustee co., on
the other hand, doesn’t depend on the life
of one man or a hundred men. Thus con-
tinuity is provided for. More than that, the
beneficiary is guaranteed against the misuse
of trust funds, first by the fact that the cp.
is in this business as a business and can’t
shift to some place where it isn’t known if it
does wrong; then by the other fact that it
puts up capital and is worth powder and shot.
Of course this and other trustee cos. don’t
work for nothing. But the ordinary charges
are small: supposing an estate worth £20,004)
is in hand for 10 years, and produces 5 p.c.
pai.. the charge is £5OO, plus £25 p.a.=£2so
for 10 years; total £750, or £75 p.a.=7s. Gd.
p.c. p.a. of the capital value. If (as gener-
ally happens) the- estate is longer in hand,
the charge works out at less ; ss. p.c., for
example, for 20 years.
As indicated above, the co. charges one
commission otr the capital value, and an
annual commission on the income. The
practice is to reserve some of the corpus com-
mission, only taking credit for it as the
trust is gradually worked out. For the past
IS months the position is as follows: —
Profit as stated by the co. . . . . . . £15,441
Added to Commissions Reserved Account . . 5,986
Total profit . . . . . . £21,427
The previous, statement that the profit was
£21,427 needs qualifying therefore to this
extent. And there can hardly be a question
as to future profits being large. This shows
the growth of estates in hand :-—-
1895 .. .’. .. £2,424,413
1900 3,928,520
1905 . . .. .. 5,753,223
1910 . . .. .. 8,175,990
1911 8,853,882
1912 9,791,590
1911 11,440,448
Without doubt there are also hundreds of
wills iu existence, made by men still living,
in which the co. is named as executor. On
the other hand, a shrinkage in values (capi-
tal and annual) will cut into the co.’s earn-
ings. which are on a fixed percentage basis;
and such a shrinkage is highly probable.
Further, the State has just gone into the
business; and in Britain a similar State
office lias had extraordinary success: the
amount of business negotiated since Jan.,
3908, when its door was first opened, is over
£91,000,000. And because a Public Trust
Office is only out to make enough profit to
form a reserve sufficient to safeguard tlie
general body of clients, it seems highly pro-
bable that concessions will be made from
time to time which are bound to affect the
business and profits of trustee cos.

The half-year’s profit is made up as follows:

But a shipping co. distressed by excessive
profits can easily find ways of stuffing a
trifle out of sight. The simplest plan is to
pay for ships, etc., out of profit and say
nothing about it. The co. did this for some
years ; but now the time has come when it
doesn’t want any more ships, so it has to
produce the same result by writing down its
property, and that “smother” is more ob-
vious. Six months ago steamers were said
to be werlb £157,125. Now. though this
paper hasn’t heard'of the sale or loss of any
Manly ferries, the item is down to £150,757.
The difference —£6388 —plus anything spent
on improvements, has apparently been
written off, ostensibly to cover depreciation.
Another view of the case can be got this
way:—
Dec., ’l3. June, ’ll.
Assets other than steamers, free-
holds and plant .. .. £50,586 £56,465
Liabilities . . . . . . 2,247 909
Surplus liquid assets .. .. £48,339 £55,556
Thus, after paying the 131 p.c. on the much-
watered capital, and making additions to
plant, and paying for renewals, etc., the co.
is £721 1 better off than it was six months
ago, less whatever value ships, etc., have lost
as the result of the half-year’s wear. And
th: past six months’ figures are not extraor-
dinary. The co. to-day owns up to £247,619
of net assets; and the steamers are probably
undervalued. Of that lot, £25.000 repre-
sents capital actually paid up 10 years ago
and £4335 paid up since; leaving £218.284
as profits made and left in the business in
excess of the very handsome dividends paid.
On capital actually paid the present div. is
at the rate of about 96 p.c. p.a.
Six months ago Ihe co. had under £46,000
cash in hand, and now it has over £53,000;
and as it is announced that no more ferries
are wanted for a while, the directors are
worried again. How can they get that
money out to the shareholders? They can’t
do it by issuing more bonus shares. That
plan would simply absorb a little more profit
term by term. They could, of course, declare
a cash bonus—but that might cause trouble,
the socialistic tiger being abroad in the land.
Possibly tenders will have to be called for
a scheme bv which the unfortunate share-
holders may suck up the profits without
arousing the public which has supplied them.
As for flies in the rich keg of butter, The
Bulletin can see only two : The Socialistic
insect before mentioned, and the possibility
of heavy claims arising out of a ferry dis-
aster —heavy claims followed by the indis-
position of Mum to take the children by
boat to Manly when they might go by tram
to Coogee.

The bank has no brass-plate outside Bal-
larat, and this policy lias its advantages. In
particular, the manager can see how every-
thing stands without moving from his office
chair; so the bank has a particularly small
expense bill to meet. On the other hand, it
depends entirely on its own town and the
district roundabout. When that slumps, the
bank has no branches in other more prosper-
ous parts to equalise matters. As to the
present strength, shareholders’ funds (before
the last dividend was paid) amounted to
£174.834, and liabilities (deposits, etc.) were
£237,150. In other words, the bank is in
the exceptionally strong position of having
£1 of its own for each £1 7s. Id. of outside
money. And to meet these liabilities it has
£108.413, or just over 45 p.c., held liquid.
Calculated on capital and reserves, the profit
lias moved thus :—•
Dec., 1910.. £5 2 8 p.c. Dec., 1912.. £5 7 3 p.c.
June, 1911.. 5 4 6., June, 1913.. 5 5 9 ~
Dec., 1911.. 5 5 9 „ Dec., 1913.. 5 5 9 „
June, 1912.. 5 4 10 ~ June, 1914.. 5 6 0,,
A bank that took more risks would show
better figures, but, on figures, it is doubtful
if any institution in Australia could show
better security. Moreover, reserves are so
large in proportion to capital (£85,000) that
if banking business in Ballarat fell flat, the
co., by investing in 4 p.c. stocks, could go on
paying its shareholders the 8 p.c. dividend,
and still be able to accumulate reserves.

This co. was formed in 1888, with a paid
capital of about £20,000. Subsequently, as
the good habit of selling wool in Australia
grew, the co. had need of more money to
finance clients, and further cash was got in.
Also, other businesses were absorbed. In
1908 50,000 new shares were issued at a pre-
mium of 2s. (Id. ; and, as the business still
grew, in 1912 a further 62,500 were sent to
market at par, making the present capital
£125,000 in £1 shares paid to 10s. But
though the capital has thus grown pretty
fast, and though dividends have very pru-
dently been kept moderate and the surplus
profits accumulated, the co. has had to depend
more and more upon outside money to finance
the business. This next table goes into
details: —
1908. 1913. 1914.
Capital £75.000 <: 124 093 £125,000
Reserves . . . . 26,545 55,381 64,160
Liabilities .. .. 141,380 221,434 244,632
Sundry debtors. . . . 200,305 305,994 327,974
Premises, plant, furniture
and goodwill . . 42,382 94,633 105,533
Cash 238 281 284
Liabilities are lumped on one side and
sundry debtors on the other; and “sundry
debtors” may be very different cattle in a
business of this kind. For instance, they may
be quite safe advances against consignments
or they may be risky loans on pastoral pro-
perty—money lent to help people buy
stations, etc., at the very high prices ruling
of late. Anyway, with £327,974 owing to
the co., and a good deal of it possibly owing
on the securities just mentioned, tlie stock
must be regarded as an investment carrying
considerable risk. Certainly the reserves are
fairly substantial: but when they do come
the losses in the pastoral industry come in
such big lumps Hat a reserve which would
he ample in most businesses is promptly
licked up in this. In estimating the size of
the risk it would be a great advantage if the
co. would show the advances made on flocks
and herds and pastoral property separate
from those against market consignments.

This society has been running for 45 years
without disaster; and The Bulletin takes
that to be a remarkable proof of (1) cautious
management, and (2) prosperous local con
ditions. The basis of the concern is cer-
tainly all wrong. Take first this compari-
son :
Debenture Ad- Propertiei
Capital. Liabilities. vances. in hand.
1900 .. £60,770 £21,092 £74,889 £456
1905 .. 60,920 49,722 112,575
1907 .. 76,291 69,820 145,162
1908 .. 86,014 66,858 155,314
1909 .. 95,799 05,076 165,815 510
1910 .. 105.571 79,348 183,427 521
1911 . . 117,913 81,062 201,055
1912 .. 124,312 69,580 204,782
1913 .. 140,320 84,581 222,580
1914 .. 150,545 101,099 255,731
There you have over a quarter of a million
of money lent out, and. as far as this paper
can see. there isn’t a penny of fixed capital.
What is described as capital may be with-
drawn ; and the borrowed money, secured by
debentures, is lent to the society at rates
running from 4| p.c. for 32 months to 3J
p.c. for deposits at call. To meet its deposits
at call it had £1874 in the bank on April 21.
Admitting that the money which the society
lends is to be repaid by monthly or fortnightly
instalments, and admitting that a kind of
reserve is thereby constituted, it is conceiv-
able that, in a scare, there would be a rush
for money; and as this would happen just
when people were least inclined to buy, the
slump would be so great that heavy losses
would be inevitable. Yet the society is pro-
hibited by its rules from holding a reserve in
excess of 1 p.c. of its advances. In spite of
its 45 years’ freedom from accident, and in
spite of the society’s excellent intentions,
The Bulletin has no hesitation in saying
that the country which permits the opera-
tion of a concern on this basis has no proper
regard for the financial well-being and secu-
rity of its citizens. In a less-favored district
and with a less cautious management it
must have come to grief long ago.
