For the six months to June. 1900, the profits
were £7OO. and the dividend 3jt p.c. Now,
on watered capital, the dividend is 20 p.c.,
and profits £OOBB. What they will be in
another eight years no one can say ; but as the
boom in borrowing and construction will not
last for ever —one bad season will shake it—
this paper will be very surprised if they
remain at £OOOO. When the slump does
happen this co. is going to be hit in two
places: 41) the expenditure of borrowed
money will stop, and (2) the increasing
scarcity of gravel on the front path will be
overlooked. The directors attribute the fall-
ing off for the six months to bad weather and
shortage of trucks. Perhaps the State and
various other new quarries had a little to do
with it. too. Incidentally, a quarry is a
wasting asset, and in ordinary circumstances
dividends are more or less a return of
capital. But, apart from the fact that this
co.’s supplies of gravel are replenished by
floods, it is writing off pretty large sums
each half-year, so that when the last load
of metal is trucked away the shareholders
won’t be left with a large supply of atmo-
sphere figuring in the books as a stone
quarry.
The next comparison shows how the boom
has affected the co.’s finances: —
June. 1909. June. 1914.
Liquid assets . . . . £5230 £18.848
Liabilities . . . . 7987 1,738
Excess liabilities. . £2757 -—-
Surplus assets . . £17,110
In five years—the five years during which
the boom has been at its highest—-(he posi-
tion has improved by £19,867. cf which, how-
ever, £6BOO has come in as new capital.
Meanwhile plant, etc., lias bee > add' d to
largely, so that probably more rl-n £19,867
of surplus profit has either be i saved up
liquid or put back into the br?i less. Be-
sides that, large dividends have been paid,
and necessary renewals and repairs made —
a performance wive 1 : suggests that, in good
times, a blue-metal and gravel quarry is a
much better proposition than the average
gold mine.
The North Coast Co-operative Co.
4Byron Bay. N.S.W.) was. on account-of
the dry months at the beginning of the year,
able to turn out only 8,940.4891 b. 4about
3991 tons'* of butter for the six months to
the end of Anril, as compared with
9,22. - ).6201b. 44119 ions 4 for the correspond-
ing portion of 1913. However, the average
erice. including exports to London, was
11.31 d. as against 10.9 d. in 1913. Another
of the co.’s hobbies, pigs, also suffered
through the dry spell, and the number of
hogs assassinated fell from 21,714 (at 5.12 d.
per lb. ) to 19.163 4at 5.48 d. per lb.). On a
turnover of £490.000 the half-year’s profit
was £2398, which suggests that the co. pays
its suppliers the utmost that it can for their
goods. _ Ample allowance is also made for
depreciation for the six months, the profits
being shown after £2500 has been written
off. Reserves (mostly to protect the co. in
case it overpays on shipments) amount to
£21,867. Fortunately it eets prompt cash
for most of its products, otherwise (since it
has £61,351 in plant) its capital (£31.356)
would he wholly inadequate. Even as it is,
the assets other than fixed plant amount to
less than the current liabilities, and that
looks like over-trading.

[6
The Union Bank, whose £25 shares have
lately been at £6O, thinks that it would be
mdre convenient if they were split; and a
scheme having that end in view was being
finalised when the war crisis intervened, and
hung up all such comparative trifles.
The outlook for Australian metal minors is
very gloomy. There is a fair market for
refined lead and silver in China and Japan;
but except for a little sent to the Central and
other English refineries, practically the
wjiole of the lead and zinc concentrates go. to
Belgium and Germany in German bottoms.
Of the copper, Germany and America take
about equal quantities; and there isn’t much
left over for anybody else. Tin ingots go
mostly to Germany and Britain; tin concen-
trates are largely shipped north to the
Straits Settlements, where they are smelted
and gambled in. These latter shipments may
continue, but the others have stopped sud-
denly. It isn't healthy for ships to poke
around the seas with valuable cargoes these
days. A German ship which happened to be
in a Russian port when the war started is
safe; it can be detained, but not confiscated.
The same with a ship which puts into port
during a war, unconscious that there is a
war. The same (with certain modifications)
with a ship which puts to sea before a war
starts. But a ship which puts to sea after
war starts may be captured and confiscated
by the enemy, along with all its cargo. The
mining cos. will not, therefore, be able to get
their stuff away; and it is very doubtful
whether they can get sufficient accommoda-
tion from the banks to enable them to go on
normally and pile up their outputs. Already
two big Broken Hill cos. have shut down,
and others will certainly either follow or
limit their operations.
* * * *
The first sign of the industrial trouble
which is probably going to be Australia’s
worst share of this war-upheaval came to
The Bulletin from far-away Whim Well,
on the Nor’-West Coast of Westralia. Whim
Well is owned by an English co., which is
Understood to ship its product to Germany.
On Monday afternoon a wire from there fell
in: “Mines closed down. All hands dis-
charged. Fortnight’s wages unpaid. Majority
stranded. Ship at anchorage 2000 tons ore
aboard.” This was from the secretary of the
local union. The unfortunate fellow's proba-
bly hadn’t heard even a rumor that Hell had
been let loose in Europe, and therefore didn’t
know what had struck them.
With the money market gone nobody knows
where, Mount Cuthbert (Q.) is more anxious
than ever about that second section of rail-
way line which has yet to be completed
before smelting can be thought of. In the
interval there is the interest on debentures
to worry over. Cuthbert would like to rake
in the Is. per share of uncalled capital if
the market would suffer it. Lots of money
is v'anted to bring even a rich copper show
to the productive stage, and Cuthbert’s Ival-
kadoon, Warwick Castle and Mighty Atom
are infants in the early stage of development.
There may be 4000 tons of ore at the Kalka-
doon above the 150 ft. level, but Cuthbert’s
smelter would soon eat that up. Altogether,
the crash has come at a mighty awkward
time for Mount Cuthbert.
* * * *
Even before the European pot boiled over.
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) wms in trouble, and
was putting men off from the poor places.
Last Friday, when it was decided to shut
up the London metal market, at least two
Australian tinbuyers received instructions
to close their books. Bischoff is in no
financial position to stand up against this.

To make things worse in North Queens-
land, the Cliillagoe Co. finds itself so hard
up that development work at the new Mulli-
gan coalfield has been stopped. The co.
was more or less relying on the Gov-
ernment taking over the railway; but
evidently there’s nothing doing.
Great Fingall’s accounts for last financial
year show a surplus of £2BOB. Add the
amount brought forward and the total is
£15,808. A Is. dividend and £971 written
off the cost of prospecting outside ventures
absorbed £13,609, leaving £1699 to be carried
forward. The general managers estimated
that at December 31 last there were 69,442
tons of 395. 2d. ore in sight below No. 14
level. As regards the future, if the co.
working the mine honestly can for the next;
year or so make a couple of thousand pounds
a month profit it will do very well. The
talk about the property being as good as ever
is rubbish.
* * * *
After loyally whooping about the great
value of the Marvel Loch district (W.A.)
the local paper has now started to tell the
truth. The principal mines, it says, arc
breathing their last, and the smaller shows
are only struggling to exist because there
isn’t enough money to pay burial expenses.
It might have added that Yilgarn is full of
places which have not and are never likely
to come up to original expectations. As a
profit-earner the Edna May, with its short
shoot and three- or four-acre ore deposit, is
probably worth more than all the rest of the
Yilgarn mines put together, and a strong
doubt still exists about the permanency of
(>ven that show at depth.
•X- * * *
Phillips River (W.A.) has got its State
smelter going at last. A staff of engineers,
fitters, etc., has been overhauling and re-
modelling the works since January last,
and the general opinion on the spot is that
the Government could have built a smaller
and more suitable smelter at the port of
Ilopetoun, some 80 miles away, for less
money than has been spent on altering and
improving the present plant. About 5000
tons of ore and old residues representing
60 days run have been scraped together, but
it is doubtful whether sufficient material
will be forthcoming to keep the plant going
when the accumulation has been treated
The trouble is that the shoots of gold and
copper on the field are short and erratic;
and the smash in the metal market doesn’t
help matters.
Things that chairman G. P. Doolette told
shareholders at the Bullfinch meeting: (1)
they must not be frightened out of their
holdings by adverse criticism: (2) he be-
lieved that those who sat tight on their
shares would come out on top ; (8) present
developments justified the statement of
lately-deceased manager Fred Morgan that
the mine was one of great potentialities;
and (4) the creep had deprived the co. of a
lot of payable ore in the northern series
above the No. 2 level. This ore, he said,
would be all won in course of time, and
preparations were being made to work it
from a shaft sunk in solid ground to the
east. The southern series, from which the
co. had to get most of its mill supplies since
the creep occurred, had stood to the mine
well, and was of such large extent that up
to the present it had only been scratched.
Owing, however, to the lower grade of this
ore, larger inroads had to be made on the
remnants of the original rich shoot on which
the show boomed and bloomed ; but a block
of high-grade ore below the 100 ft. level was
now being stoped upwards from the 200 ft.
workings. The mine will probably go on
producing for a long time to come, but in
estimating profits it is just as well to remem-
ber that Doolette is an apparently-incurable
optimist.
* * * *
Ivalgoorlie is elated over the intersection
at 2900 ft. of high-grade telluride ore in the
Golden Horseshoe. Croakers are locally in-
vited to do as Judas did, and pessi-
mists are advised to follow suit. But hold
on ! The No. 4or Boulder-boundary lode in
which the telluride has been found has
always been regarded as the most persistent
of all the Ivalgoorlie gold deposits; and
nothing that may happen in this lode will
alter the fact that as most other shoots hq.ve
gone down they have become smaller and
poorer. All the big mines are affected.
Some of them, notably the Brown Hill and
Associated Northern, are breathing their
last; the -Ivalgurli and Boulder’s days are
numbered; the Ivanhoe is badly damaged ;
and the Associated, Perseverance, South
Ivalgurli, Lake View Consols and even the
Horseshoe are not what they used to be.
What sane mining man would expect it to
be otherwise?

(Ivalgoorlie) accounted for 67. Murchison
108, North Coolgardie 96. East Murchison
62. Mount Margaret 60. YTlgarn 71. Cool-
gardie 45, Dundas 23, Yalgoo 25. Phillips
River 22. North-East Coolgardie 37. Broad
Arrow 35, Pilbarra 31 and West Pilbarra
and Peak Hill 8 apiece. The gold-treat-
ment machinery working or lying idle in the
State at the close of the same year was
3337 head of stamps, 11 prospecting, 39

and 2 Tremain mills. 81 other crushers,
8 puddlers. 385 grinding pans, 1269 leach-
ing vats, 327 agitating vats and 215 filters.
The total value put on this lot was
£3,498,500. East Coolgardie produced 55.42
p.c. of the year’s output; Murchison came
next with 0.80; then Mount Margaret 7.08,
East Murchison 0.77. Yilgarn 6.84, North
Coolgardie 5.28.
Mount Oxide came into court in Sydney
last week and told, the Equity Judge that it
could very well pay its debts. By this time
official-liquidator Palmer had started on the
job to which he had been set a few days
before; so the Court allowed him 20 guineas
for his trouble and called him off. Which
was probably a mistake. The inside of the
business badly needs investigating, and an
official liquidator would have done it best.
Having got its ore-dressing plant ready,
the Mammoth (N.Q.) has started up its
furnace again.
For 12 months to April 80, Vegetable
Creek (N.S.W.) produced 198 tons of tin
concentrates. Luckily the price was excep-
tionally high, averaging £l2O per ton. so
this long-struggling concern was able to
make a profit of £9470. After allowing for
royalty payments there was £5855 in the till
on April 80. With the profit on about 70 tons
of oxide produced since the accounts were
made up, there should be over £6OO available
to pay the 3d. dividend and provide for a
special settlement with the landlord. To July
13 the total output of oxide lias been 5414
tons, worth £64,019, and no less than £14,245
has been paid off the landlord's purchase
price, originally £25,000. After the special
settlement is made in August there will he
about £8750 still to pay. Reserves are esti-
mated at 381 tons of oxide; but only a small
proportion is immediately available. The
present position isn't good, for wages claim
£7OO per fortnight, which is roughly £6B
per ton of concentrates, and the tin market
is knocked dizzy.
* * * *
Carpathia (N.S.W.) looks like small divi-
dends if tin doesn’t slump any more. For a
week it milled 180 tons ore for 12 tone, of
concentrates (04.1 p.c.). For all that, no
one dare back it to put out 500 tons of oxide
a year. Returns that drop from 17 tons to
six tons a week don’t inspire confidence;
hence the stock has a market value of only
about £50.000. The mine is just as erratic
as the battery return, and in spite of to >
fact that some of the workings are very wide
the despatches were only 100 tons last week.
Carpathia is like the Vulcan (N.Q.) in hav-
ing wide faces and small reserves, the pay
•stuff being in big isolat I patches of pipes.
Managing-directors (J ovett and Hoover
didn’t add much to the Zinc Corporation’s
printed report at the annual meeting in
London. Oovett’s most interesting state-
ment referred to the South Extended, next
door. ■ The Corporation’s zinc lode runs into
the Extended. The latter 00. had one of its
numerous reconstructions last year, and the
underwriters were left with most of the
shares. For £35.000 the Corporation then
bought 73 p.c. of the property. The Ex-
tended won’t pay to work as a separate con-
cern (that’s Govett’s opinion and The
Bulletin’s), but the Corporation is now
negotiating to buy out the minority and work
the claim as part of the other mine. For
the rest, they repeat the.formula that with
speller and lead aggregating £39 per ton, the
co. can show a profit of £150,000 a year after
paying the interest on pref. shares. A rise
of £1 in lead bevond that means a trifle more
than another Cd. per share and a rise of £1
in z ; nc about 6d.
Bears have been having a meal off the
New Langi Logan, Ararat (Vic.), but mana-
ger Toole still smiles. If he were an impres-
sionist he would be getting and reporting 2oz.
prospects from bores and drives, instead of
running up rises to drain the wash, or con-
tenting himself with sober-sided shaft-sinking.
His time for a flutter will arrive when lie
starts out. to bore into the Catlicart gutter
from the No. 2 shaft reef drive. A rich
prospect or two here will set the market
bouncing. Meantime, a Government boring
party working north of the Langi Logan
lease is alleged to be showing that the main
gutter is east of that claim. Even so, it
doesn’t say that the bores from Toole’s levels
and crosscuts lied as to the existence of a
big field of wash or as to the dirt giving good
prospects. Another yarn current is that the
No. 4 rise has been lost. The report says it
was left to drain, and this is probably right.
Once upon a time Cassilis, Gippsland, had
as many developments as blackberries on a
bush ; but since Nicholas left, manager Lil-
burne has been battling against dry weather
and a light purse. Cash wasn’t plentiful
enough to even let him test his pet theory
that rich stone pitched south. Now he has
got going at No. 7, and alighted on a shoot
that has ranged up to soz. in value in the
level and up to 9oz. in the rise. Lilburne’s
deduction from this development is that the
shoot which was supnosed to have cut out at
No. 6 level has pitched flat to the south. The
country there is pretty well unworked; so
Cassilis may have enough left in it to put
its values on a profitable basis again.

Catlicart, Ararat, and brother Bryant for-
sakes Cathcart to take Browne’s place. He
will find himself handicapped by narrow
ground and heavy wash.

“AN UNIMPORTANT FAILURE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oniv' one unimportant failure wasp recorded yestcr-
d&y.——. An early item of the war patlic days.
B'.dtt was an unimportant man,
One of the unimportant host;
• To unimportant work he ran ;
Withal to Mott Mott mattered most.
Caesar, Napoleon, Fred the Great —
; All these were shadows in his sight;
But Blott was solid, and Blott’s fate
Kept Blott awake on many a night.
• There came a day when War’s hot breath
Blew down the markets, and a lot
Of little things went down to death.
Ini luded in the mass was Blott.
“An unimportant failure’’ —thus
The cables vaguely flashed his fall.
Nobody made the slightest fuss;
Blott was no more, and that was all.
Each man unto himself is Blott,
And kings and nations merely names;
The Universe, immense and hot,
Spins on Blott pivots, splashing flames.
“An unimportant failure!’’ Yes,
To others, if you lilce. But not
To him who knew (we know no less!)
He was the One Important Blott!
O Brother Blott, our fate's the same!
’Though each proclaims with puny fuss
lie is the One Man in the Game,
At last the shadows fall on us!
N.S. W.
TOBLAS BLOTT.

WOMEN’S WEAK NERVES
Many women suffer from liyper-
sensitiveness of tlie nervous system,
induced liy many causes. Excessive
nervousness is the bane of many lives,
and often a severe attack of nervous
breakdown follows on heavy, exacting
social or domestic duties. Where the
nerves are shattered by physical or
mental strain this is the medicine to
be relied on to quickly strengthen them.

Miss A. M. Cardwell, Coree, Booth
Street, Annandale, for many years head-
mistress in N.S.W. Public Schools, writes:
“When teaching in N.S. IK. State
schools I often resorted to Clements
Tonic. It was such a restorative it
never failed me. No household should
he ivitliovt it.
(Signed) A. M. CARDWELL.”

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 3, 1914.
My Dear Arini. —
"The Blue Danube” isn’t quite up to
schedule just now. as a waltz tune, while
the Austrian is banging the Slav on its
hanks. Re the war, by the way, how thin
and faint, like the tinkle of a distant sheep
bell, is the voice of the innumerable Aus-
tralian politician when Europe is taking off
its coat for a fight. While the Austrians
were busy shooting down the Serbs in their
own capital, a belated interview with a Mel-
bourne politician crept into the press. “I
have had no official intimation of war,”
said the Minister for External Affairs. Per-
haps somebody was so busy mobilising
5.000,000 men and trying to borrow
£000,000,000 of petty cash that he forgot to
send a cable.
The Governor-General’s garden party at
the Eadith Walker loan establishment, Yar-
jilla, seems a feeble, far off, trivial show,
since the Great Row began over Peter’s tin-
pot kingdom. Owing to the placard that
invited this paper’s social scribe having been
inadvertently buried by some careless urchin,
and not exhumed till the day after the event,
my news of the historic party before the
war is second-hand. An informative friend
remarks that there was an unprecedented
rush on the forage department. When the
sub-royalties were ready for their little snack
the A.D.O. department had to gather up a
few crumbs to help the tea go down the
right way. A mild air of resentment on
the part of that portion of Society which
bad been shut up in a ferry-boat with its
worst hated friends for over 30 minutes, and
a bumped expression about the usually im-
passive motor-car brigade, which wore its
best hat on its ear when it alighted, were
the most noticeable features of the garden
party. The fashion of bunching up tbe skirts
behind was carried to such a frenzied ex-
treme that two-tliirds of the company looked
like Runty when she was ready for tbe
week’s wash.
The only sartorial event of the Yaralla
garden party was Mrs. Holman’s picture hat.
which, according to latest advices from the
front, was rather smaller than the War. but
much nicer looking. It was beautifully
mobilised, and occupied a strong position.
Governor Brunet, of New Caledonia, who
arrived here with his Madame Brunet in
Ihe Pacifique on Friday night, may stay
longer than lie anticipated. Who knows?
He may even fall in love with our gums
and wattles, and stay till he becomes Ihe
Oldest Inhabitant, and may end by being
buried here with a waratah blooming on the
bosom of his long home.
It is rather a coincidence that the Forbes-
Trefusis lady should look in here, just as
we are trying to gather up our sinews for
war. Last time this tall English dame was
in Sydney she reigned at old Government
House as chaperon to brother William—the
youthful Governor Beauchamp, ivho took
newspaper chaff in such good part. As Lady
Mary Lygon she was associated with our
last war cry—the echoes and troopships of
the S’African squabble. I forget whether our
returned visitor was here before the row
began, but she is certainly connected with
the horrid, trembling uncertainty of “News
from the front —’special ’dition !” —and the
shrill yells of newsboys out of season. Ladv
Mary married —just wait a minute till I lift
it down off the bookcase —tbe Hon. IT. W.
Hepburn-Stuart Forbes-Trefusis. If he
were on the other side, I expect he’d be
buckling on his belt in some kind of fighting
gear, for he used to be in the Scots’ Guards,
and he is on the reserve of a battalion of
Light Infantry.
The F. B. Freehills, formerly of this town-
shin, were in the grey old city by the Thames
early in July. They had been on the
European Continent —a most undesirable
address just now—for some time, and must
be mighty glad to be on the right side of the
Channel.
Miss A. Ducker, the Sydney heiress who
set out on a world tour with her own motor,
some friends and a chauffeur, was in London
a few weeks ago.
The American architect Griffin and his wife
were in Sydney a few days ago. Probably
tbe Federal village at Canberra will take a
complete rest now. A report is abroad that
the new loan has gone lame in consequence
of the war. and been scratched for all
engagements. In other words it won’t run.
The perennially youthful Essie Jenyns
(Mrs. .T. R. Wood) was the graceful Nerissa
io the Portia of Ellen Terry at the huge
matinee recently flung at the veteran actress
in Her Majesty’s Theatre. It seems odd
.enough, when you come to think of it. that
the famous Ellen, who might have been rest-
ing in her cot in Kent, should have been
goaded by Fate into straying so far from
her native sod in these thrilling times.
Some loyal liege said that judging from a
hurried glimpse of the Governor-General on
Sunday, he was a very speedy man. Ilis
Ex.’s collar and tie, if not exactly suspended
in mid air, were anywhere round his neck,
and he dashed off by Sunday’s express for
Melbourne. Official tea parties and frills
are now packed up as public nuisances not
wanted by G.-G. Ferguson till the heavy-
weights in the big fight call for a rub down
and a referee.
Mr. Bob Bell, the genial managing direc-
tor of the N.S.W. North Coast Shipping Co.,
writes to a Mosman friend from the kilted
shores of Oban, which is in Argyllshire,
Scotland, not far from Mull, Skye, Colonsay
and other places which are strong towers of
defence to the British Isles because the in-
vader can’t pronounce the names of them,
and is consequently repulsed with great
slaughter. Mr. Bell and his wife hoped to
leave London for Australia on August 28,
but warlike events have happened since the
peaceful postcard was dropped into a Hie’-
land post office that smelled strongly of
usquebaugh.
One of the queer vagaries of the Gentle-
man Help department is the crash of titles
that follows the person who is gazetted in
attendance. For instance, the most unwar-
like lamb is immediately handed the courtesy
handle “Captain” to go on with. Mr. Cur-
wen, on the Governor-General’s bodyguard,
has never been a soldier, but he is a “Cap-
tain” nevertheless. Mr. Wakeman wears the
same honorary gaud, and Mr. d’Apice, the
local hon. aide (a billet that is paid for in
social glory) was a lieutenant in the cadet
forces. No one grudges him the title of
Major-General or Field Admiral if he’d like
to wear either prefix round his best hat, for
he is one of the best. But this democratic
country seems to be getting as full of un-
earned increment in tbe shape of honorary
titles as the United States in the days when
Dickens fell up against so many colonels
and judges that he couldn’t find a single
civilian. (It was in the U.S., by the way.
that an old-soldierl.v-looking man was asked
by the hotel landlord what rank he had
held during the Civil War. And when he
said lie had been a private the landlord fell
on his head and wept, and said there would
be no bill.)
The Arts and Crafts’ Society has just
issued its peaceful ultimatum or manifesto in
the form of a seventh annual report. Miss
Fairfax is president of this flourishing
fraternity, which now counts 74 members
on _ its roll. “Decided progress” says the
ultimatum, “marks the past year’s work.”
The annual exhibition is to be opened on
August G by Lady Helen M. Ferguson at
3 p.m. The R.A. Society’s rooms in
Vickery’s Chambers will house the show.
Mrs. Arthur Allen has loaned the impres-
sive ballroom at Merioola (Edgecliff-road)
to help the languishing funds of the Twi-
light Home. Over 200 tickets, at a guinea
apiece, were sold last week for the concert
on August G in that large dancing room.
The Ferguson lady is going to shed the light
of her diamonds on the affair. Muriel
Starr, Mrs. Alex. Gordon, Miss Spain and
others are going into the concert business
for the Cause. The party begins at 4 p.m..
and will be over in time to permit the meek
and lowly to rush home to their early even-
ing chops.
Mr. Gerry Cooper, son of the Australian
baronet of that name (of the House of
Daniel), is genuinely addicted to music. He
is going to unload a pet hobby, called
“Thursday 5 o’clocks,” in the King's Hall.
The first concert, which is dated August 27,
will purr softly in the Italian manner. In
case there may be any misunderstanding I
hasten to add that this is not a public
charity. Book your seats at Baling’s in the
same old commercial way. Tenor Philip
Wilson is associated with young Cooper in
the venture.
An Australian in London blushes behind
her fan and tells me this: “On Ascot Sun-
day, when there’s a great collection of glad
rags in the Park, there was a wonderful
display of decollete Society grandmothers.
I noticed that the slit had moved from the
skirt to the bodice. The want of bodice,
and general impression of bare shoulders —
seen through the wisps of tulle—-is supplied
with a slight antidole in the shape of trans-
parent sleeves. Still, they are a cover-
ing for the angular or beefy arms of dow-
ager duchesses and such. Many of these
ladies are taking to bright purple silk hose
for afternoon wear. Anyone who libels the
British peeress to-day as a drab, sad-colored
person of dowdy plumage ought to have seen
her at one of these dress parades in June.
I suppose it’s the joyous clothes of the
former Totties of the Gaiety co’s who cap-
tured the junior aristocracy, that are
driving elderly duchesses of the old order to
drown their sorrows in purple stockings.”
Fiddler Raymond Pechotsch has written a
charming little song, “For Thee,” which is
served up with violin obligato. Dufault sang
this graceful lyric the other evening to ap-
plause that drowned the siren shrieks of
adjacent tubs and scows in Darling Harbor.
The Symphony concert, with conductor
Joseph Bradley in his usual stand, was
starred by the success of fiddler Cyril Monk
in a beautiful solo from a Concerto for violin
and orchestra, by a German composer, Max
Bruch. In order to keep the balance of
power, perhaps, some new Russian thunder,
in the form of Kaliiinilcoff’s Symphony in G
Minor, was supplied by the orchestra. When
a drum went off suddenly, like a gun, the
female hat in the front seats was obviously
startled. Saint Saens’ symphonic poem (a
fine mealy mouthful), called “Phaeton.” was
a good orchestral performance. But Saint-
Saens’ notion of galloping horses —as written
for the violins —wouldn’t be much good to
him if lie ran a racing stable. Phaeton’s
aerial steeds don’t pound the clouds any
faster than a butcher’s horse when it is
steadied to turn a corner. However, the
orchestra did its level best to whip them
into a trot.
“The Dream,” a poetic fragment from
Alfred Dill’s new opera, “Giovanni,” was
something of a nightmare owing to Phil
Newbury being apparently too indisposed to
let the words escape his clenched teeth. This
was at the Symphony orchestral concert on
Saturday afternoon. Hill, who was con-
ducting the fiddlers during this performance,
must have writhed with anguish to hear his
new musical baby smothered by the tenor.
Yet Newbury’s voice, a week or two ago,
was clear and resonant as a bell at the llie-
land Society’s greeting to the Ferguson.
Tenors are uncertain cattle at best, I’m
told.
Lancelot Harrison, B.Sc. of Sydney Uni.,
recently won the Coutts Scholarship. This
entitles him to two years at Cambridge and
a year in Berlin on scientific research work.
His wife, who used to be Amy Mack (a
sister of Louise, who once wrote this letter
to you, Arini), therefore hies to Europe with
her clever husband. For the last seven years
or so the plump and popular little Amy
Harrison has been in charge of the Women’s
Page in the S. M. Herald. In the doing
of this work she has made many friends,
who turned up to bid her farewell, and hand
her an address—written by Ethel Turner—
and a purse of sovereigns. This meeting was
held in the Mayoress’s official den the other
morning, with our municipal mother in com-
mand of the proceedings. Mrs. Alfred Hill
and Mrs. Pickburn were to the fore in
arranging the Harrison adieu. The gentle
Cullen lady. Mrs. George Rankin (a brisk
and up-to-date youngster of 70-odd sum-
mers), J. D. Fitzgerald (of the Town-Plan-
ning campaign), the. Laytons, Mrs. Lindsay
Thompson, imss Rose Scott, General Sec-
retary Ellen Desailly, of the Kindergarten
Union, and a score or two more women
bustled round the proceeding's.
Miss Ida Meecham, only daughter of Mr.
Walter Meecham, was married to a Scotcli-
byterian parson named Stewart Robertson
on July 28. The knot was securely tied at
Burwood by the groom’s clerical brother, who.
had journeyed from Bathurst for that pur-
pose. An extra twist in said knot was given
by Parson Reid, of Woollalira. The kirk
organ was manipulated by the Rev. Horace
Croft, of Mosman. I don’t think there were
any more sky pilots on the premises, but
there was no time to look under the seats.
The birde, who is a pretty and charming dam-
sel, is related to about half a British regi-
ment, or something of the sort. The late
Colonel Meecham. of the Cameronians, was
the best-known of a round half-dozen uncles
who were all tall sons of Mars. Bridesmaid
Una Croft fluttered round the bridal plu-
mage at the right moment. A few friends
and relatives were invited to the wedding
feast, which was munched at the Edward
Crofts’ home, Burwood. Ferns, wattle and
daffodils trimmed the speeches.
Paul Dufault’s little tea-party at the Aus-
tralia. the other afternoon, was a festive
affair with a tasty garnish of new millinery.
Olive Godwin’s small black toque was punc-
tuated with the sharp flare of new bows
that are rather like French cockades set in
half a slice of bomb. Mrs. Brunton. who is
pretty Dorothy’s mamma, and might be mis-
taken for a very young auntie, shoved her
dark Titian locks into one of the new squash
hats. Dorothy’s flaxen curls bobbed in the
vicinity of Fiddler Ernest Toy, and Pauline
Bindley’s fluffly little person was over the
way. The Fourth Estate contributed a
musical cricket or critic or so. and Mrs.
Freddie Shipman poured out tea with a soft
smile, for the Dufault concerts have been
attended by a satisfactory chink of cash.
Marjorie Williamson, eldest daughter of
the late .T.C.W., has taken some of her flap-
per friends off to Kosciusko.
The United Charities’ Fund secretary,
Mr. Arthur Upjohn, is already on the trail
of volunteers who will sell roses for the
Cause on October 24. The Cause includes
GO Good Works. Already over 3000 lady
bushrangers have agreed to bush-range tbe
public, and sell them roses on their own
terms. And the public will doubtless put up
its hands and surrender at discretion. The
Boers, during the late war, defined their
own people as “handsuppers” (those who
surrendered early), and “bitterenders” (those
who hung on to the last). But who can be
a bitterender when a pretty girl offers to
sell him a rosebud for 7s. Gel.?
Perhaps the New Citizens’ Association,
which is incidentally connected with a
broom-handle and other useful weapons for
the improvement of towns and cities, will
meet on August (i, in the Town Hall.
Things are happening too rapidly to be sure
of anything this week. But if the associa-
tion does feel calm enough to go on talking
about better roads round Sydney just now —
it’s mostly feminine —it is respectfully re-
minded that better roads mean cash in hand.
Higher taxes to keep the same in order will
also be necessary, I understand. Blue
metal seems to wear out . almost as fast as
cotton stockings, only you can’t darn
macadam unless you do it; under your
breath, Arini. The British Scientists
(they are to travel over said roads) are
probably an absent-minded lot, who won’t
see anything except a new species of gnats,
or microbes, an inch beyond their learned
noses.
The Women’s Horticultural Guild held
another meeting the other evening, and
appeared to be bustling about its business in
eax-nest. It suggests tying a longer tail to
its skirt, and hopes to grow up into a limited
company (d.v. and the nasty young war
permitting) forthwith. Mr. Chiel, a lead-
ing member of the Botanic Gardens’ staff,
was in charge of the gathering, which had
been rounded up by hon. organising sec. Miss
Brace.
Miss Agnes Ilogg, a much-liked school-
marm in the big Paddington State school,
left for Norfolk Island in the Makambo.
She is going for two years to that lovely
isle—now the most peaceful spot in the
troubled Pacific —as second in command. A
few days before saving good-bye to the
youngsters under her care, Miss Ilogg was
laden with little notes of farewell in all
sorts of childish fist, while the women
teachers handed her a presentation bag of
great merit. This strenuous school-marm
belongs to a well-known family of Braid-
wood pioneers. A maternal grandfather,
one Finlay Mcßae, settled on the land
thereabouts in the year 1838. Mrs. Elliot,
Wife of the former Administrator of the
island, was on the wharf to wave the hanky
of adieu to the traveller, who was sur-
rounded by bouquets, colleagues in the
dominie department, and niece Ethel Ilogg.
The last-named bustled along to say good-
l»ve behind a young mountain of Australian
llora. She looked like Birnam Wood mov-
ing against Dunsinane —if my Scotch his-
tory is correct.
The Dufault concert on August 1, in the
same old Town Barn, was as successful as
every previous effort of the artistic Paul.
Handel’s immortal theme, “Where’er you
walk.” was the vocal vom 0 f the evening.
Pauline Bindley kindly allowed the audience
to hear a word distinctly now and again.
This young Victorian singer’s pretty voice,
full of quality and easily trained to twitter
and trill, is just now blurred by a crude
style and careless enunciation. Violinist
Toy suffered various encores. Jacques
Pintel at the piano was musical matter in
the right place.
Mr. Murphy, the present local ruler of
Norfolk Island, returned to his pine-crowned
eyrie in the Makambo.
War permitting, the Freddie Shipmans
leave Sydney on August 15 for Wellington
(M.L.). They join tenor Paul Dufault in
Auckland on the 28th. They’re going to put
in three days at Honolulu, and then board
the Ventura for the city of the Golden Gate.
Violet Paget is going to be presented with
a complimentary benefit in the Little
Theatre on Saturday week. This is a more
handy thing to have by you than a bouquet
or an illuminated address, anyhow.
The London Daily Mirror mentioned the
appearance of Alice Crawford, the Victorian
girl now on the London stage, as she passed
in the crush at Ascot. She was garbed in
“dead white.” and held over her head the
onlv little early Victorian sunshade seen at
the meeting.
The youthful Mirrie Solomon, who was
going off to Vienna to continue her musical
efforts, is, like many millions more, waiting
anxiously to see the Peace Society slay the
war-dogs with its umbrella. If the umbrella
is lost or breaks, or the war-dog bites it in
two pieces, or if tbe dog takes no notice of
the umbrella, then Mirrie will postpone her
adventure.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
From a Victorian gallery-man:—
Though Victoria’s new? Premier is not likely to he
the same force in local politics as his predecessor
was, his term will probably see fewer ructions. The
great difference between the two men is that A.
Peacock draws a great number of director's and ether
fees, and runs the Government as a sort of side-line,
whereas 'Willie Watt’s superabundant energy forced
him into trying to carry the whole political burden.
Peacock will conciliate where Watt would fight,
and, consequently, the warring factions in the Minis-
terial party won’t have the same opportunities to
get into holts.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 3. 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The G.-G. arrived back from Sydney in
panic haste to-day (Monday), and was imme-
diately fallen upon by a troupe of Federal
Ministers. Having assured each other that;
the war in Europe was “a very terrible busi-
ness,” and having decided that none of them
could do anything much to stop it, they pro-
ceeded to act accordingly. The latest advices
of my tame runner were to the effect that
they " are still doing it as hard as they
possibly can. The only interesting item which
lie (the runner) was able to extract from
Lord Frederick Blackwood related to Mr.
“Monty” Walker, of Yaralla, where the G.-G.
has been staying. The luckless twin has
broken his collar-bone playing polo, a game
into which he had spiritedly entered only a
week previously, with the purchase of three
or four one-hundred-guinea ponies. It is
such horrors as these that make one realise
the deadly seriousness of Life.
The Caulfield Steeplechase meeting on
Saturday drew an immense crowd, and yet
there was not even half a Governor on view.
As 1 have always understood that one of the
chief uses of Governors is to draw crowds
to the races, the Caulfield happening strikes
me as rather a powerful argument against
employing these imported luxuries further.
Rather like hiring a claque for the prepos-
terously successful Lauder. The Browns, of
kittle Lonsdale-street, were there (she in a
red velvet toque of the 3912 build, lie in the
bowler hat which got so terribly dented at
their daughter’s wedding last January), also
their friends the Smiths, who had that little
bother with tin l bailiffs the other day. But
here am I telling you about all the wrong
people. Mrs. Dick Casey again appeared in
that laurel wreath. She still refuses, why
I know not, to explain how she earned it.
A bachelor doctor (1 withhold his name for
fear of making the rest of the profession
jealous) escorted the smartest girl on the
lawn—a niece from furrin’ parts. She wore
a coat, skirt and cape all in one, and how she
got into it, Heaven, in its infinite capacity
for solving the impenetrable, alone knows.
The baffling garment was fashioned out of
dark-blue cloth, and its effect was to turn
the heads of many of the onlookers dizzy
with envy. Anyhow, they said it was that.
Unbelieving friends put it down in several
cases to drink, and hence argument and much
ill-feeling. Dorothy Moss, of the beauteous
complexion, paraded with her fiance, and
sustained a heavy bombardment of congratu-
lations with heroic composure. One O’Hara
girl turned out in a simple tailored suit;
another walked the lawn self-consciously, in
advance of a big black velvet bustle. Mrs.
Bran ton wore, a hat that reached gaily at
the sunshine in blue tulle pleats. Mrs.
Leslie Macdonald wore a green tapestry
chair cover, or again it may have been a
coat. Mrs. Charlie Mitchell was there, but
1 did not see sister Melba about. I could
not even find her in the birdcage, which was
sufficient proof for me that the sporting
Presence was absent. Miss Nyulasy trotted
about with her bachelor brother, a tartan
cape swinging from her shoulders like a mis-
guided kilt.
A new tenor, named Levienne Davos
(which is so much more beautiful than Levi
Davis or Amos Levien) made his first
appearance at the Saturday Pops at the
Auditorium. We literary girls are quite
mad about him. He is so much a tenor that
he just misses being a soprano. He makes
much use of a “head” voice (mark the tech-
nical term, indicating a knowledge of the
musical art), and puts terrible earnestness
into the passionate parts of his songs. He
is large and fat and bald and 23 —in fact,
as I hinted before, he is little short of rav-
ishing. He has been studying since he was 17,
and has sampled all the different “schools”
in his brief time. Having plumped finally
for the Italian method he is prepared, at
any moment, to sing at you in four
languages, provided, of course, you allow him
to take one language at a time.
For some months now The Bulletin’s
“Jeames” has been prophesying the advent
of the Night Club. It would seem that he
knew a bit, as they say on the turf. An
artistic brown circular has just reached me
which sets out the aims and objects of the
“Eighty’” Club. This club intends to provide
a rendezvous for dancing bohemians (dancing
Philistines, mayors, knights, dervishes, etc..
will. I presume, be ruthlessly pilled) where
music, supper and good-fellowship shall be
obtainable, and where the Tango, Ragtime
and the Hesitation waltz may be indulged in
without restraint. (Moans from the wow-
sers!) Club night, Friday from 10 p.m. to
2 a.m. Insignia, a Silver Owl. Location,
Wyckliffe House, on the Esplanade. St.
Kilda. But why limit the membership to
SO when there must be hundreds fairly yam-
mering to join such an institution? Also,
what arguments are to be advanced against
the militant members of the local Purity
Brigade when they start lodging the inevi-
table protests? These are subjects which
will have to be grappled with bright and
early, by the committee, it seems to me.
I hear that the Alan Burgoynes have been
shooting in Scotland. However, as the bird-
slaying season doesn’t open, in those parts,
till the 12th of this month, I presume my
informant is the usual traitorous liar. Ot
course, there is the possibility that they have
been assassinating the grouse out of. season,
but I am disinclined to entertain that
theory. The rumor is no doubt due to the
fact that Mrs. Burgoyne handles a fowling-
piece with the precision with which women
of a humbler class employ a flat-iron. The
original idea was that 18 members of this
grand old viticultural family should stay at
Burgoyne senior’s Ascot house for race-week.
They may have done so. The Ascot news
has come to hand, and there is no mention
of the Burgoyne homestead having been left
untenanted. But the Scotland rumor still
remains a mystery. Perhaps it is merely a
blind to conceal from the Germans the fact
that Alan is going to the war. He is. need-
less to say, a military man of parts, and the
author of several books on the simplest and
easiest method of wiping the Teuton off the
face of the earth.
Mrs. Geoffrey Syme duly gave that At
Home to celebrate her return from Colombo.
She looked blooming, in an apple-green dress
with green apples, or potatoes or pompoms
or dumplings, or something, embroidered all
over the chiffon in velvet. The skirt was
gathered up to show tricky green silk stock-
ings and shoes. My first action, on catching
sight of her, was, as a lover of Peace, to
fall upon my knees and offer up a brief
Hosannah, inasmuch as Brother Oswald
ltobinson Snowball had not been in-
vited. In the present tense state ot
things, he would have burst. Mrs.
David Syme was there looking about
25, for all that she is a great-grand-
mother. She was wrapped in an ermine
scarf and short skirts. Mrs. Herbert was
in buff, and little Mrs. Oswald hid in the
crush under an adorable hat of flowers. Mrs.
Leslie Stuart wore the newest shape in
capes and a hat like a small torpedo. Mrs.
Burston afl eeted a flowered bonnet of the
Dolly Varden period. Kost conducted in the
gallery above the hall, and. Miss Ivrapp and
Walter Kirby sang—all of which was pleas-
ingly conducive to conversation. \ iolets
and boronia were placed loosely about the
reception rooms', after the fashion of old
Japan. Also, there was a fortune-teller in
a summer-house on the lawn, and tea and
food, and a thousand other delights.
The revelries at Banool lead me by a
perfectly natural process of thought to the
atfairs of Greta Garnett, sister of Mrs.
Geo'ffrey Svmo and a daughter of British
Trade ‘Commissioner Garnett, who circled
this continent last year. She writes from
England that she is to be married shortly to
Mr Jack Robinson. All I know about him
is that he is (1) 2?>, and (2> a nephew of
sister Marjorie’s husband. So when Miss
Greta is married her big sister will be also
her aunt. The bride-to-be recently spent
two'of her 20 years of life in Melbourne with
Mrs. Geoffrey Syme, and made a host of
friends.
Doctor Eben Gould’s children’s party to
the convalescents at the Homoeopathic Hos-
pital was a great success. The children there
have a special house all to themselves set in
the garden away from the big institution.
It has two perfectly appointed wards, and
was built with the money of some anony-
mous donor. There are trees and birds for
the youngsters to watch through big win-
dows, and the nurses cultivate carnations
and geraniums on the window ledges. All
of which helps to stimulate the hospital
atmosphere. Nurses, patients and visitors
ended the day in a blaze of colored paper
caps prized from the crackers, which were
everywhere. This signified jollity. In a
British community one of the several ways
of showing that you are jolly is to wear a
garishly-colored head-covering of paper.
Miss Helen Davis was knocked down in
the street by a too-hurried medico the other
day. She is suffering a very slow recovery,
and has had to give work the go-by for the
present.
Harry Lauder filled King’s Theatre on
Thursday nieht with a mixed audience which
interjected in every shade of Scawtch accent.
In the boxes were fashionables, in the stalls
baldheads and Tam-o’-shanters, and in the
gods retired crofters, ghillies and elders, in
addition to blokes, girls, noise and yells. An
Irishman started a sort of opposition show
at one stage ; but he was flung out amid the
approval of all. A fat merchant sat in an
upper box and laughed so much, in a silent
bianc-mange-shaking way. that the house
began to fear for his safety. He, however,
was not flung out. For the rest, it was the
usual old Lauder entertainment.
Dorothy Moss, of the Kerriwah Mosses,
intends to marry Captain Charles Dare. I
don’t know who he is, and feel too languid
to find out at the moment, but our wash-
lady’s A.D.C. —the one that delivers the
garments—assures me that he is exception-
ally beautiful. Dorothy, as you know, is the
sort of girl that bank-managers bow to from
the other side of a wide street.
Another engagement is that of Cyril Lan-
sell (one of the Fortuna magnates of Ben-
digo) and Lily Anthony. She is the one ewe
lamb of the T. I*. Anthonys, of St. Ivilda.
And, finally, Dr. Noonan, with whom the
habit of being Best Man had threatened to
grow into a lifelong hobby, proposes to make
the great adventure with Edith Lloyd, of
South Yarra. In view of past unpleasant-
nesses, let me mention, here and now, that I
take no responsibility for any of the above
intimations. I have garnered them either
from the papers or from acquaintances who
have played me mendaciously false a
thousand times in the past. If my girlish
trust has again been betrayed—you know
where to place the blame.
The Hennessys, appreciative, as ever, of
Brain, Culture and so on, propose to give a
reception on August 15 in honor _of the
Science Congress. The scientists will thus
have an opportunity of exchanging ideas
with the Governor-General, his staff and a
number of aldermen, importers, squatters,
solicitors, commission agents, property-
owners and other kindred spirits. It may
well be that a priceless treatise on “The
Properties and Causation of Acute Bore-
dom” will he the outcome of this entertain-
ment.
Melba’s Westralian company is tempo-
rarily “resting”—as the profesli puts it when
it is out of a job. Doris Madden is here,
also pianist Harold Whittle ; but McEacharn,
the baritone whom I tokl you about last
week, lias gone to Sydney. lie will pr ibably
appear later on with Melba. Meanwhile he
persists rashly in wearing a presentation lie-
pin. which flashes the Star’s name in dia-
monds. With the Sydney crime-wave in
its present state it is simply asking for
trouble to wear such a gaud, but these bari-
tones are a reckless, devil-may-care lot.
May Vale Gilfillan is showing portraits
from August 4 to the end of the week in her
studios, at 483 Collins-street.
Which reminds me that during this week
and the next the Athenaeum Upper Room
booking will be given up to Hal G.ve, Percy
Lindsay. Benson and Stoekfelt. lln 1 Gye
caricatures for food but etches for fun, so
to speak. Thoughtless persons speak of him
as a cynic. If they only knew! As a
matter of fact, he has a soul —I was going
to write, like a bullock, but perhaps it hardly
expresses exactly what I mean. Any-
how, he is practically all soul. lie
hankers for some writer of Australian
Fairy Tales to let him illustrate a
book about these ethereal creatures in
colors. His best effects are got from
eucalypts and ii-tree, with twilight purples
and greys, and dark, bunyip-infested pools,
and mist-clothed valleys, and mysterious
backgrounds, and all that sort of thing.
Elsie Mackay, Fan Dango’s pretty step-
daughter, is to play the leading part in
“Grumpy,” with Cvril Maude, at the New
Theatre, London. She left Melbourne only
IS months ago to “walk on” in London. She
got a chance to understudy Mrs. Pat Camp-
bell, and Luck and Looks and Talent did the
rest.
Melba is to sing at the G.-G.’s Reception
of the Science Congressors on August 13.
So what with the drinks and one thing and
another, they should be able to face the rest
of the entertainment with a certain amount
of fortitude.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
A woman has actually been elected to the Council
of the Adelaide University. Think of it. And Capi-
tal still remains in the country! Dr. Helen Mayo,
the chosen female, is a grand-daughter of the late
Dr. Mayo, one of the best known of this State's
early medicos. She was educated at home and at
the Advanced School for Girls, and after going-
through her course at the Adelaide University and
putting in a year as house-surgeon at the Adelaide
Hospital, she went to London and Dublin, ar.d then
on to India, where for a time she had charge of St.
Stephen’s Hospital at Delhi. Arrived back in
Adelaide, she settled down to serious Work, feeling
that her life hitherto had perhaps been a shade too
much on the frivolous side. In her leisure moments
she looks after the School for Mothers, the Babies’
Hospital, the Women's Students’ Club and the Women
Graduates’ Union.
On Wednesday the Symon dame and Mrs. Miller
entertained members of the Poetry Recital Society at
the May Club. “Esther,” a play by an at present
unknown South Australian writer, was read relent-
lessly from cover to cover by Mrs. Miller, who got
considerable moral support from a becoming gar-
ment of dull blue charmeuse and a rose-bedecked hat.
Her accomplice was in black. Dr. Souter and his
wife, the Rev. W. 11. and Mrs. Winter, Miss Prim-
rose, Miss Hammett Dyer, Mrs. .Tames Booth, Mrs.
Cleland, Miss Craigie and others of our tame poets
and men and women of letters were among the
uplifted.
Yet another musical reception—this time to Harold
Bauer at the Rubeo on Friday evening, and again
with the Adelaide Chamber Music Society as the
host. Mrs. Magarey, who received with the presi-
dent and vice-president, was an eminently successful
pale green admixture of satin and ninon with a
bunch of violets at the waist line. Most of musical
Adelaide was there.
Miss Rhoda Hanson, whose marriage to Mr. Mal-
colm Murray will shortly take place, is spending a
few days with Mr. and Mrs. Grundy at Victor
Harbor. Mrs. Tennant is another of the party.
Mrs. James Harvey has gone for a trip to Queens-
land.
The old Leawood garden has been taken over by
Miss Eva Laughton and Miss E. de Mole, who intend
to work it as an orchard and also as a flower-farm
and tea-garden. If English and American reports
can be believed, women gardeners usually do well,
and the few who have made tentative experiments in
Australia seem to be making steady headway.
Mrs. Woodhead and her daughter left for England
last week. The Arthur Waterhouses swell the list of
“departeds” next week, and Miss Deely goes at the
end of September. That is to say these departures
will occur if General War doesn’t object. A whole
lot of little details of our social life will depend
on the whim of General War.
Florence Itodway’s exhibition of portraits
in pastel comes due at the Athenaeum Gal-
lery on August 19, and Florence herself will
get to Melbourne a week before the event.
She intends to hang some 30 or 40 portraits
and studies for portraits that are mostly
borrowed from the buyers thereof, so the
exhibition, commercially speaking, will be
rather a show of samples to book orders
from than a display of pictures for sale.
Fidelity of likeness is a strong point in Miss
Rodway’s charming work, and this will at
once appeal to all Melbourne moneyed women
who know the Sydney originals of any of
the portraits. The tall winsome pasteleuse
is bound to get the commissions she is
coming for. To look at the people she has
pictured is to want to be one more of them.
And the few unsold specimens of Rodway art
will surely be snapped up by local connois-
seurs.
The Down-Trodden Females’ League, or
some other shamefully ill-used body of the
sort, has given Vida Goldstein a motor-car,
in order that she may pursue the elusive
vote-fowl on equal terms with Man. Also,
Mrs. Gregan McMahon Inis worked a
Woman’s Banner for unfurlment purposes at
meetings and so on. The banner is, I hear,
a huge silk thing with Australia’s dag in
one corner, and in another (though of this
I am not absolutely sure) a representation
of Man Rampant and Lovely 'Woman utterly
spurning him.
A children’s Fancy Dress Ball in aid of
the Epileptic Home for the Young will
simmer or boil —according to how tickets
go off —at St. Kilda Town Ilall on August
21. Prahran’s Mayor Willis is running the
show, with a committee which includes the
George Fairbairn dame and other charitable
folks.
Mrs. Mu flic Chirnside Brown has just
emerged from a rest cure, looking 10 years
lighter and you n err than when she went in.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

WHY HAIR FALLS OUT.
THE CAUSE—AND ITS PREVENTION.
Dk. N. DUCLAUD.
Your hair falls out either because the roots
shrivel up and die, or because the scalp
relaxes and loosens its hold upon the hair
roots. This is almost invariably, due to
dandruff germs which rob the hair of its
nutrition, actually starving it to death. To
stop falling hair and prevent total or partial
baldness the dandruff germ must be de-
stroyed. For this purpose, physicians and
hair specialists now invariably prescribe a
lotion composed of i dram menthol crystals,
three ounces of bay rum, and one ounce of
lavona de Rub well into the scalp
with the fingertips, and every dandruff germ
will be destroyed within two or three days.
The hair will stop falling out and. begin to
°tow at an amazing rate, and brittle and
scanty hair will ouickly become soft glossy
and luxuriant. The $5
use of this formula are so tiuly remarKame
“hat I strongly advise every reader who*
hair falls out to give it a trial at once.

Somebody lately, started the rumor that the
A.B.C. was going to put up its dividend at
once. Seeing that a new G.M. had just
taken the bridge, this looked as unlikely as
it has turned out to be. All the same, .as
soon as the new man has got the hang of
things, and feels quite sure that ordinary
contingencies are being amply provided for
apart from the sums put to formal reserve, he
may be trusted to do something. Another
i p.c. to go on with will call for (say)
£3OOO, and the average lay-by for the past
two years, when 3 p.c. has been paid, has
been £9350 per term; so there is a very fair
margin to work on. The business is growing,
too: —

cept that it has to put by £15,000 each half-
year (not necessarily out of profits) to buy
up the debt. Nowadays, with money fairly
dear, this big 4 p.c. liability is no burden;
but under different circumstances it easily
might be. In short, a big loan at a fixed
rate of interest means erratic profits—very
fine and large when other people have to
pay dear for money, and very poor when the
bank has to lend out the dear money at a
cheap rate.' Still the fact that the money is
fixed is a great advantage to other depositors,
because, if anything goes wrong, they
have the first cut at the assets. At present
the bank has £3,222.720 of these new liabili-
ties, and besides the advances and premises
(together over £4,450,000) there is £1,770,849
in liquid assets. The depositor who wants
better security than that ought to have been
a pawnbroker.
What the A.B.C. wants most, and what
it seems to be now getting, is a bigaer borrow-
ing connection. This naturally takes a little
time to wqrk up. Throughout the weary
years when A. P. Stewart and the others
were working off the mob of old dead horses
you wouldn’t pick the A.J.S. to go to for an
advance. In fact, it got a bad advt., be-
cause even those who had old deposits
couldn’t get much accommodation, as a rule.
But the old dead horses have gone (the staff
did a rather neat thing when it presented
Stewart wi+h a live horse to mark the pass-
ing-out of Hie final corpse) : and to-day the
applicant' doesn’t find the place a freezing
chamber. Still, it has got a good way to go
yet before It has as much out in advances
p.c. of capital as the very prosperous hanks:

The foundations are ready for another
£3,000,000 of business —good, solid founda-

suming the management is sound, the
shareholders will be getting a return as good
as the security for their principal now seems
to be. At present the security is excellent
and the return wretched.
* * * *
Australia Hotel Co. (Sydney).—This
c 0.,. which owns Sydney’s biggest thing in
residential pubs, has had its 23rd meeting;
and from the heights of its present affluence
it can look back on a strange, eventful his-
tory. The building, with the land and neces-
sary outfit, cost well on to £300,000. Most
of the capital came from London. For some
years the thing was a frost. In those days
Australia was crawling out from the wreck-
age of the broken boom, and the sixpenny
hash-house of Antonio Schnappero was more
in its line than the palace in Castlereagh-
street. There was a plaster of over £125,000
on the premises, and the London co. felt so
bad. about it that the directors wrote the share
capital down to a poor little £25,000. Gradu-
ally Australia got its chin off its chest, and
not only did business improve for this co.,
but there was a big recovery in the value
of the freehold. Still the London people
were very nervous—apparently they had
begun to hear vague stories of the Aus-
tralian wowser —and that and the operations
of the local-option party combined with
other things to make them eager sellers when
an offer was put before them four years
ago. The co.’s finances soon underwent a
complete change. The big mortgage was
paid off, partly by an issue of £33,000 worth
of pref. shares; and the ordinary capital was
increased to £75,000, partly by the issue of
bonus shares to absorb accumulated profit.
Profits during the past three years have
been: —June, 1912, £31,314 (including cer-
tain special profits—apparently contingency
funds no longer required) ; 1913, £24,237;
1914, £22,741. These amounts are exclusive
of sums put by to various renewal and re-
pair reserves. Including additions to these
the profits in the past year were £25,041.
The 6 p.c. prefs. took £1980: 3s. a share (on
all shares, whether paid to 20s. or 10s.), re-
quired £15,000. There was thus £BO6l to
strengthen the various reserves, which now
stand: Formal reserve, £16,000; furniture
renewal, £6500; machinery renewal, £4000;
special repair reserve, £6000; unappropri-
ated, £4624. Total, £37,124. But their
exact nature is not disclosed. If they are
merely to provide for the renewal of things
which are becoming worn or obsolete, then
they are not true reserves, and the addi-
tions made to them are not true profits.
But there is some doubt about this. Any-
way, the co. has made very extensive im-
provements during the past two or three
years, and the cost has been charged neither
to capital nor to a reserve account, but
against working exes. This 'suggests that
things are as good as they look, and perhaps
better. But whether things will continue as
good depends on the price of wool, the rain-
fall at Thargomindah, and a lot of other
extraneous things. The co.’s finances are
now very easy. On June 30 it owed alto-
gether £24,533, and liquid assets amounted
to £12,032.

When the office was. started in 1904 the State
said it could have £lOO,OOO to go on with.
All it actually took was £2000; and it made
so good a start that it never wanted any
more, and during the past year it paid back
even that little lot. Its premiums last year
were £59,418; so its reserves are equal to
110 p.c. of the premiums, and though 210
p.c. would be better, still the chances of the
office having to fall back on the State for
any of the £lOO,OOO are, as the manager
says, exceedingly remote. And, given ordi-
nary luck, the reserves should go on increas-
ing fast. The private co. does very well in-
deed if it puts by its underwriting profit, and
absorbs in dividends only the interest on its
investments. But this office has no share or
loan capital to pay dividend on; so not only
the underwriting profit but the interest on
investments as well is available for reserves.
The reserve funds are in public securities
(£45,250) and deposits, and the interest
earnings last year were £1863. The balance
of the income—£59,418 —came from premi-
ums ; so the loss rate last year was a reason-
able 47.1 p.c. and the expense rate an ex-
tremely moderate 26.7 p.c. As a matter of
fact, the expense rate at the start was too
small. Like many another State business
concern, this office wouldn’t pay enough for
special capacity, and almost invariably as soon
as it had got hold of a good man and proved
him good, some private co. grabbed him at
about double the money. The office is now
trying to wipe out that stain, first by paying
a bit more straight out and then by introduc-
ing a sort of profit-sharing system. There
is unquestionably a big field ahead of the
staff. So far the aggregate premiums are
nothing tremendous; thousands of insurers
who encouraged this office to be born were
quite satisfied to leave their business with
the old offices as soon as the latter hurriedly
reduced their premiums. But the fact that
this office can’t possibly go bung unless the
whole country goes bung must tell in the
long run; and the idea of his whole country
going bung is one that the immensely pat-
riotic Maorilander is about the last person
in the world to entertain.

Automatic Telephones (Australasia),
Ltd., formed in Sydney with a goodwill of
£5OOO to introduce the Strowger and Keith
patents to Australasia :
Towards Deficit.
June, Profit. Dividends. Reserves. £5OOO
1912 £3 £3 4997
1913 567 567 4430
1914 .. 4361 7i p.c. = £3750 611 3819
The co. is the agent of the Automatic Elec-
tric Co., of Chicago. It apparently paid
nothing to get the business: it merely put up
£10.500 as a guarantee that 10.000 lines
would be ordered before June, 1914. The
original agents—the Standard-Waygood En-
gineering Co., it seems —passed the represen-
tation on to this co. in exchange for 100,000
deferred shares of Is. each. To provide
working capital, 100,000 ordinaries have
been issued; they are of £l, paid now to 10s.
These ordinaries are entitled to a first 10
p.c. p.a.; the ordinaries and deferreds halve
the balance. The first year was mostly spent
in talking ; in the second year contracts were
entered into with the Commonwealth to
equip the Perth and four suburban ex-
changes ; and just before the third year
closed tenders for seven more were accepted.
The £10,500 deposit is thus all right: it goes
towards paying for material. What this co.
gets out of the business hasn’t been dis-
closed —presumably only a commission. Any-
way, it is still the Chicago people’s agent,
though it has to do the financing. The direc-
tors say: “The current year should be a
busy one [in view of the contract for the
seven exchanges], affording profitable em-
ployment for the co.’s capital, the bulk of
which is lying at call. Under a new agency
agreement with Automatic Electric Co.
better terms have been arranged, and a much
longer period granted upon reasonable con-
ditions” —this as the result of managing-
director Nicholson’s visit to America. It
looks as though every telephone exchange
will be made automatic; but exchanges are
not legion, and the big transformation job
won’t last many years. After that, unless,
of course, the co. gets some of the revenue
from royalty (and it isn’t likely to be
allowed much of that), the co.’s income will
slump badly. Meantime, however, unless the
holders of other patents butt in hard, it is
likely to show very fine profits. Also, it has
this advantage: when there’s no more work
to do, the capital, on present appearances,
will be available. Of the £50,000 paid in
cash £42,726 is still in that substance or
short loans, and the balance is represented
by work in progress (which is just about as
good as cash) and a few trifles.

That’s an unsatisfactory table, and nobody
knows it better than the carpenter who made
it. The capital was originally £17,500, of
which all but £2500 was represented by good-
will ; and the latter wasn’t written down,
though the lease was racing to its end. When
the end came the landlord granted an exten-
sion only on condition that the co. spent
£5063 on improvements to his property. This
brought the goodwill items up to actually
more than the capital. In other words, the
co. owed more than the value of its tangible
assets. Then a bit more capital was got in
and £8750 was written off; and as at June,
1912, when things were thus cleaned up,
£9044 of the capital of £12,250 was repre-
sented by paper assets. Since then £3OO a half-
year has been written off; but it has been
written off tangible and intangible assets
ia a lump, and tangible and intangible assets
are also lumped in the balance-sheet. If
£5O a half-year is enough to write off the
furniture, then £250 has been written off the
intangible assets; and in that case the de-
ficit is £1250 less than the table alleges.
Better, therefore, put it this way:—
1912. 1914.
Assets apart from goodwill, lease,
and furniture .. •• £1417 £3348
Liabilities . . . . . . . ■ 993 952
Surplus .. .. .. £424 £2396
So in two years, while a p.c. div. has
been paid, the more or less liquid assets have
improved by £1972, or at the rate of £986 a
year; and if the co. can keep that up it will
easily have the whole capital in hand in
hard assets by the time the lease runs out in
1930. In fact, if the surplus shrank by one-
lialf, the thing could still be just managed.
There are now 35,000 shares paid to 75.;
and though the security is very poor, the
return is irnry fine, and the chances of a
refund of capital excellent —always assuming
reasonably good management, which in pub-
running consists chiefly of knowing exactly
what the tripe and the toothpicks and the
mustard are costing.
* *■ * *
Eyre Peninsula Petroleum Oil Co. has
started boring at Wanilla, 17 miles from
Port Lincoln (S.A.). The plant, by Hor-
wood and Co., of Adelaide, is designed to
drill to 3500 ff. The co. is acting on the
advice of Dr. Basedow, who stated that the
three necessary conditions for oil were
present: (1) Sufficient carbonaceous material
from which oil can be formed ; (2) the geo-
logical structure; and (3) porous rocks over-
lain by a sufficient thickness of impervious
rocks. T. J. Whaley has started borii.g
five miles from Kingston (S.A.), assisted by
six Californian oil-drillers.
Covent Garden, London.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fog shuts in the reek of soot,
Of sleepy walls and gloomy;
But here there lurks a breath of fruit,
Of pricking bud, of sappy root,
And fields lush-green and roomy.
Sham. quivering cries and rolling wheels
The mist has tried to deaden ;
Yet Spring trips in with restless heels,
And through a madcap dance she reels
To make the apples redden.
Faint as the light a far lamp throws
Set in some vine-fleeced tower,
Where last night’s buried sunbeam glows,
Great bunches of exiled primrose
Along the wet stalls, flower.
And by their fog-blurred ranks I found
(The ripe fruits lured him most!),
Sprung like a genie from the ground,
His dun cloak folded closely round
A friar’s pensive ghost!
He left behind the skies austere.
The gates of pearl and gold,
To tread a faltering measure here,
Down ways familiarly dear
In monkish days of old!
A garden full of leafy trees
Was once this city square ;
And, rising from his gouty knees,
The abbot bland would take his ease
When blackbirds chiriuped there.
A dial carved with warning words
Taught piety was all,
And vanity the pipe of birds,
And lilies white as milkmaids’ curds
That lean against the wall,
And vanity the rose that grew
And scent and sap and root,
And vanity the bough that threw
Its green and gold across the blue,
Prest down with swelling fruit.
A penance was the friar set —
He had forgot the sin.
The penance seemed to chasten yet,
Nor Heaven had taught him to forget
The sweets the rind shuts in.
Perhaps he stole the round, pink peach
The abbot had marked down.,
As thirsty as some thready leech,
He found it, high and out of reach,
And climbing, tore his gown.
What use to bend a tonsured head
In muttered, hasty prayer?;
The abbot bland his guilt had read
Through fingers o’er betrayal spread
lie saw the fateful tear!
“The sin of covetousness” (of course
’Twas different with the prior),
“The sin of greed” (the abbot’s loss),
His fingers held the wooden cross,
His keen eye held the friar.
The punishment to fit the crime,
Stamp self out branch and root,
The friar at refection time
Could have one green and bitter lime
But no ripe summer fruit.
The vagrant wasp might prick the grape
To draw the germ of wine;
Wild birds could make the red skins gape,
The bee was free in blossom rape
To drink his draught divine.
But, in a narrow, whitewashed cell,
A very humbled friar
Must gird his soul to make it well,
For self-indulgence leads to Hell,
And Hell springs from desire!
Perhaps he heard the nightingale
In bosky ways beyond;
Perhaps he guessed the moonlight pale,
Unrolling like a lady’s veil
Across the sheltered pond.
Man-born he had his human needs.
His holiness to leaven.
What matter now? He told his beads,
And. guessing at his life, one ; reads
His altitude in Heaven!
’Twixt bounty heaped from fiHd and grove
He turned and looked at me,
A shadow where the shadows move,
A ghost come back perhaps tq prove
That all i.s vanity!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

Hit. CHAIRMAN AND GENTLEMEN,—
You do me great honor by calling upon me to speak here to-night.
I support tire toast oE my friend with the greatest pleasure that I have ever experienced at any gathering in my
business career. The world is full of men who know how to run the other fellow’s business, but we are short of men
who carr take a business of their own and run it successfully. And I say that the men who guide the destinies of
Chateau Tanunda Limited are trained protagonists of the new spirit of trade—the spirit w uti lecogmscs rat
there is a large element of science even in the smallest transaction of barter and trade.
In all the world the one desire of everything, animate or inanimate, is to be supreme. The reason is plain--
the greater the supremacy the greater tire reward. Every single stalk in a field of wheat strives to thrust its head
above the others, so that it may be the first to catch the rays of the morning sun.
And o-entlemen, the directors of Chateau Tanunda Limited have met with a reward that they deserve. Tue
service they have done this great country in the development of the Brandy Industry is one that cannot be igmy
passed over. This Company, over twenty years ago, mt out to produce a brandy that would favorably compare wun
the finest foreign spirit.' And what have they done? They have, in my opinion, gone one better nr their march of
prom-ess, and made a better, a purer, a more mellow and a finer brandy than is being made m or out of Australia
to-day. not excepting the best foreign productions. To know of the wonders of our own land is a duty as well as a
privilege, and not to know the great and romantic building, the Chateau at Tanunda, m South Austialia, is to
ignorant of one of Australia’s greatest and most costly enterprises.
Mr Chairman and gentlemen, with the greatest cordiality and feelings of friendship towards my friends—the
company of Messrs. Chateau Tanunda Limited, I support the toast.

“JEsculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water —only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured fust as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Ilopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
“Figaro”: Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no w ; ay unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way 0 f
wisdom is always to
