“Snell”: I don’t know what you think,
but to my mind it’s the limit. Two neighboring
wives in my locality were discussing intimate family
affairs recently. Mrs. Dash is a domesticated little
body, with a fair batch of children, and is usually to
be seen behind a white apron in hot pursuit of
household duties. Mrs. Nextdoor is quite another
kind of busybody, being much abroad in quest of
mysteries, decorated with an impressive pair of spec-
tacles and carrying a bunch of mixed literature. Said
she to Mrs. Dash: “No, my dear, I simply can’t think
of having children. They would interfere too much
with my church duties!”
“Black Swan”: Some figures for the
believer in a White Australia to pin in his hat. In
the pearling industry on the Nor’-West Coast at the
present moment 342 luggers are engaged, of which
300 are registered at Broome, 12 at Onslow and 30
at lloeburne. These are manned by 30S white men,
and 2388 Asiatics and colored brothers generally.
Time was when every lugger carried at least one
white man —usually shell-openers—but now each
carries only part of a white man, and next
year the fraction is pretty certain to be reduced.
Over 1381 tons of shells were raised in the year, and
putting this at an average price of £240 a ton we
have a total of £331,440, nine-tenths of which has
gone into alien pockets. And the introduction of
boats with motor power of one kind or another will
enable the harvesters to gather more crop and so still
further enrich and strengthen those whom the White
Australian has most to fear.
* * * *
The public gets its leg well pulled when-
ever there's a bit of land to be ballotted for in
N. S. Wales, and the Fat landlord with an estate to
subdivide or sell to the Government sits on his fence
and smiles. The latest instance is the announcement
that 1900-odd applications have been received for five
plots of land at Mirrool, in lliverina. They come
from all parts of the State and Victoria and S. Aus.,
says the pop-eyed paragraphist. Of course they do.
Nineteen-twentieths of the applicants never saw the
land, and don’t know whether they could make a
living on it or not. All they know is that they get
it for the first five years rent free, being merely
required to spend £6O or so a year on improvements,
and at the end of that time they can sell their
right to occupy the land for ever, the incoming man
having to pay a rent of only a few pence per acre.
So the successful applicants get rent free the use of
the land for five years, and then they have every
reason to expect that they will be able to sell it for
about £4 per acre. (Freehold thereabouts is worth
£7 or so.) They don’t have to put up any deposit:
all they have to do is to send £1 to an agent who
will book them a ticket in the sweep, put up the
necessary deposit, and attend the drawing. Agents
have their clients just like any other promoter, and
at every drawing they flood the country with prospec-
tuses of the new sweep. The amazing thing is that
the number isn’t more nearly 19,000 than 1900. Then
the papers yell about land-hunger, and the real land-
hunter, who has a poor chance in the bloated consul-
tation, turns to the private subdivision fiend, and
finds that the price of his land has been put up
another ss. an acre on the strength of the bogus
extraordinary demand for farms.
“Pick”: “Tamai” (B. 9/7/T4) apparently
thinks that all bush-ticks are identical. Not so.
Cattle, gohanna and dog ticks are totally dissimilar
in size, color and virulence. Like the wallaby tick
and parasites that hold their squattages on the
hides of many other bush animals, the ticks that
infest snakes, goliannas and the sixth-day creation
generally are non-venomous, and a gohanna may,
therefore, have every scale of his squirming length
fringed with the encumbrance without danger to life.
But the blue-bottle tick that attacks dogs will, if
fastened to a vital spot, create a fever in the victim
usually fatal and at all times paralysing in its
effects. Cattle ticks are brown in color, flat and
with a wedge-shaped head. They appear to attack
cattle and horses only, and when numerously con-
gregated upon a beast invariably cause the marble to
be passed in. Talking of cattle ticks, has any abo.
ever met one travelling any direction other than
southward ? Old hands in Queensland, and on the
far northern rivers of N.S.W., earn most of their
beer by laying odds on a tick-walking trial. The
tick is placed on a slab, and no matter in what
direction it (tick or slab) is turned, the insect will
immediately head south and stumble onward over
all obstructions and tick regulations. It is, there-
fore, only a matter of time until they will walk
into the fancied security of the South Coast, and
there will be something beside rumors in the air on
that day.
“Ivuscobin” : Of all native races, dentists
say that Henare takes the biscuit for sound teeth,
the percentage of decay among the Maoris being only
1.7 per cent. It was first believed that thiswas due
to a weakness (not so long back) for human flesh,
but the bottom fell out of this theory when it was
pointed out that long pig was tabu to the women
and only given sparingly to the elder boys, while
the teeth were equally good at all ages and in both
sexes. Another scientific highbrow then came for-
ward with the theory that the cause of it all was
the amount of sand taken with the food, adducing
as evidence in support the fact that the Maori ovens
were mostly located in sand dunes. But we know
that Henare used to wrap his game up in green
leaves before steaming it, and also covered the fires
over with mats—-so the sand theory was mere scien-
tific imagination. Not to be beaten, this same
authority then explained that the chewing of coarse,
fibrous food was the cause. This, too, was just so
much hot-air, for Henare’s fern-root flour had all
the fibres removed before being made into dough,
whilst the kumara, or sweet potato, cooks to a soft,
powdery mass. The latest reason put forth is that
Henare was a bit of an epicure in his way. All his
staple foods were distinctive in flavor—fish and game,
kumara and fern roots, fruit and berries. Of the
latter it is pointed out that tutu berries were in
great request, and “tutin,” the active principle of
these, causes intense salivation. It is to plentiful
saliva that modern dentistry ascribes the fine ivories
of the Maori. But here’s a point still unaccounted
for. Henare to-day lives mostly on strong grog and
motor-car joy-rides, yet his teeth are as good as
ever. My theory is that a good spree, with joy rides
to lend variety, is the finest of all dentifrices.” When
my rich uncle kicks the bucket I’m going to prove it.

“Centauri”: The black dismounted and
commenced attacking the butt of a hollow, stunted
coolibah with his tomahawk. A few chops and the
implement was into the rotten inner shell, through
which he pushed his finger, and a stream of yellowish
fluid squirted out. Cupping his hands, the abo. drank
about three pints, and then I drew about a quartpot
full. The liquid smelt not unlike sulphuric acid, and
tasted worse. Altogether, there was about 10 gal-
lons in this natural reservoir. The catchment area
seems to be the hollow limbs of the tree, down
which the stormwater flowed into the tank below.
“Chloe”: The Chinese, Japs and Malays
of Nor’-West Australia and the Malay States all
play a game with bowl and chopsticks like that
described by “F.Y.7” (B. 16/7/’l4). But they do
not call it fantan. They have a species of roulette wheel,
much on the principle of the European affair, and
they back the color in the same way. In another
game small discs, with insect, reptile, bird or fisb,
etc., marked on the face, are put into a bowl. The
players sit round the table. Each player backs his
fancy for a place, first and last drawing an equal
prize. The bowl is then fitted with a lid, swirled
round a time or two, and the discs trickle through an
aperture. There is as much row in the room while
the discs are pouring through as on Flemington
when the horses come up the straight. The writer
lived for some time in Port Hedland, and could
look down from the verandah into a den of this
kind. The Chinese played all night, going back to
hotel or laundry at daylight. They played 364
nights of the year. They missed Chinese New Year’s
Night, when they had fireworks instead. The gam-
ing instinct is so strong in the Celestial that they
even back their crackers, betting which will explode
first. Do the Chinese ever sleep ? That problem I
have never solved. In Singapore the coolie Chinese
work all day and all night in the tailors’ shops and
the laundries, whilst their employers gamble. In
Broome you can go into the Chow quarter at any
hour of the night or early morning, and by the
always-open windows of laundry or tailor’s shop you
can see drowsy Chinamen ironing or sewing, each
probably making sleepy bets with his neighbor as to
the number of buttons on the next garment, or any
other futile thing on which they can hang a wager.
One hotel cook has never been at home a night dur-
ing the four years of his term of service. TTe will
only cease from gambling when he is dead. An
unfair feature of the business is that the lower caste
are under indenture to the higher ; so a poor wretch
of a dhoby or punkah-wallah is obliged to hand over
all his earnings to pay up the losses of a rapacious
superior. And he invariably gets kicked for not
having won or saved more.
“Dulamakau”: The Papuan is an adept
in preparing his brother man for consumption, and
is as skilled at “cutting up” as any appendicitis
sawyer. Since cold storage is not available, partial
cooking is utilised as a preservative. Except where
civilisation has brought along the steel knife, the
cutting up is done with a knife of bamboo, which
the Papuan handles with astonishing slickness, re-
sharpening his weapon by peeling a sliver oif the
edge with his thumbnail. On the Buna Bay-
Yodda-road, in the Northern Division of Papua,
writer and others butted into a war party return-
ing from an unsuccessful raid. Among the impedi-

menta left behind by the raiders, after the ensuin'?
argument, were many parcels neatly wrapped in
banana leaf and tied with rattan flips. One which
writer opened might have gone through any parcels
post, as far as outward appearance went. It con-
tained what looked like a nice piece of pork spare-
rib, beautifully “butchered,” and cooked with the
“crackling” on. It belonged to some Papuan belle,
but it was too late to send it to her then.

The following order, dated 6th February, 1914.
“ Messrs. BEALE & COMPANY LTD., Sydney, Australia.
“ The object of this letter to-day is that we wish you to make, on account of the Strand Hotel Co. Ltd., to
be used in the Regent Palace Hotel, now in course of erection at Piccadilly Circus :
' “ One full-sized Concert Grand Piano, in burr walnut, in the latest model, with just sufficient exterior
ornamentation to be dignified. We think it unnecessary to give you any instructions as to quality,
etcetera, as you will know how to supply an article of the best, and you can put on the inside of the lid of
the piano (if you care to) ‘ Made expressly for the Regent Palace Hotel, London, by Beale & Co. Ltd.,
Sydney, N.S. W., Australia .’ ”
11 If the contractors keep to their time, the Regent Palace Hotel will be open by October next. It will be
the largest hotel, we think we may say, in Europe, having between one thousand and eleven hundred bedronns.
If you can possibly do it, will you let us have the piano here for the opening of the House; if not, please let us
know in due course, so that an arrangement can be made for the temporary hire of one.”
npHE order came to us entirely unsolicited and unexpected. There can be no doubt
that it is meant as a compliment to Australia, for the STRAND PALACE
1 HOTEL, filled from the day of its opening, has had a great many guests from
this Hemisphere. It is to be expected that its immense popularity will he even exceeded
by that of the colossal caravanserai, the REGENT PALACE HOTEL, built upon
an “island” site at Piccadilly Circus, therefore light, safe and airy. Anything under the
able management of the Directors of J. LYONS & CO. is sure to win the confidence
and approval of the public, for their enterprises are the most conspicuous success of modern
times in the World’s metropolis. Their one inflexible rule has always been: “Everything of
the verv best and absolutely genuine.” No tips are allowed, no secret commissions, all
above-board and in favor of the public. And so in return they get public favor.
Beale & Co. Ltd. have repeatedly supplied Grand and Upright Pianos to these splendid
institutions. The amusing part is that we have "been virulently attacked by the German
trade Press for thus invading Europe with high-priced pianos, because they reckon to
hold a mortgage over the piano-supply of Great Britain and of Australia in particular.
Beale Pianos are made by us only, sold under our own name only, and when they are offered without our name be
sure it is a fraud.
20,000 BEALE PIANOS have been made by us in Australia.
Where easy terms are required we issue a free life policy, so that in case of death the instrument becomes the property
of the customer without any further payment, thus removing anxiety in time of trouble.
The Beale Factories are the largest of their kind in the British Empire, and are under the direction of one of the
world’s greatest experts, Major George D. Rose.

The Maoriland Government is being urged
to do something to protect the native birds on Niue,
one of the Cook Islands. And not before it was time.
The native idea of being up to date is to buy a
gun from a trader and then go into the bush and
slaughter as many pigeons, doves and parrakeets as
he can while the ammunition lasts. The result is
that the very birds that are badly needed to keep the
insect pests in check are being rapidly and insanely
killed off. This has already happened at Rarotonga,
and a wild yell is now going up from that island for
quantities of foreign bird to be imported at great
expense to gobble up the exceedingly numerous insect
that afflicts that earthly paradise. It is suggested
that the importation of firearms and ammunition into
Niue be prohibited. Another pbace where the same
rule should apply is Stewart Island, at the southern
end of Maoriland. This is practically a bush-clad,
scenic reserve, about 40 miles across and with 20
miles ;of stormy sea between it and the mainland.
It would make an ideal sanctuary. Most of the
delightful fowl of Maoriland are still to be found
on the island, but hopeless, holiday lunatics go there
in large numbers in the summer and spend their
time slaying harmless tuis, wekas, bell birds and
even the cute little bush robin that is so tame that
it will hop on to the barrel of the gun and have a
peep down the muzzle. Unless the State wakes up
and deals with these so-called “sports” lOTJ Land
will shortly find itself without any of its feathered
natives. What’s the matter with an open season for
shooting “sports” ?
* * * *
“Snell”: Three times within a fortnight
in three distinct oyster hells I have seen damage
done to hard “knuts” by mere waiters; and I take
this opportunity of advising gay devils, night owls,
toughs and naughty boys generally to be more
Broosmithian in their treatment of waiters and bar-
men than they have found necessary in the past. The
fact is proprietors of establishments where the
“knuts” assemble have found it advisable to have
at least one waiter on the premises who can put it
across wayward youth when wayward youth is full
of stout and oyster cocktails, and burning for the
fray. Hence a demand for used-up pugilists. To
see nice, upstanding lads, with a few of the rudi-
ments of fist fighting, egrcgiously man-handled by a
low-heeled waiter in a slopped vest is as pitiful a
spectacle as to behold leopards bitten to death by a
sheep. I think restaurant-keepers who have an
ex-professional slugger on their staff should be com-
pelled to hang out a warning note —“Beware of the
Pug!”
* * . * *
“Moana”: Another little sample of the
kind of endurance and pluck they breed in the bush.
A man named Worsley, working at tree-felling in
the centre of the North Island (M.L.), 20 miles from
anywhere, had one of his thighs smashed through a
tree falling on him. This happened late in the after-
noon, and at five o’clock the other eight men in the
camp started to carry the damaged brother over the
rough and mountainous bush country to Raurimu,
on the Main Trunk line. After doing five miles they
secured a trap, but the country was so rough that
it broke down and had to be abandoned. -So they
took to the carrying again. The night was pitch
dark and cold, but they kept steadily at it, some-
times through dense bush, at other times over rough
clearings of piled up logs and frequently through
creeks and along tracks where they were up to their
knees in mud. It was a worn-out and done-up crew
that struggled into Raurimu early next morning and
put the injured man on the train for the Hamilton
hospital. I didn’t hear that they got a medal for
the act. Probably all the medals had been used
up to reward members of the bowling club or the
winner of the billiard tournament.

“Lagani-Namo” : T wish to enter turtles
for fur Bulletin’s egg-laying competition. Lady
turtles think nothing of parting with 150 eggs, or
more, at a time. These eggs are the size of a golf
ball, and a remarkable thing about them is that
no matter how long - they’re boiled the yolk remains
liquid. Mrs. Turtle scrapes a hole in the sand, well
above highwater-mark, produces her fruit,- scratches
the sand back, and leaves the rest to Providence. As
the young shellbacks find themselves at liberty they
spring straight to the water. They tip the scale at
about loz. at birth, and, if nothing interferes, they’ll
keep living for several hundred years, weighing up
to even 4cwt. There is a giddy young tortoise in
Berlin, which still takes an interest in things after
213 years at the same address.

G WADLYS: “What makes your nose so red, pa?"
OLD SPORT: "That is a sign that I am in the pink of condition, my dear.”
“Black Swan” : The financial cloud which
has hung over W.A. is about to lift. McLeod, M.L.A.,
said so in the Assembly the other day, and his argu-
ments as well as his figures paralysed the Opposition.
The State brickworks, now nearing completion, are, so
says Me., capable of turning out 180,000 bricks a
week, at a cost of £1 ss. per 1000. The market
price being £3 per 1000 it follows, according to Me.,
that the Government will clear at least £250,000 a
week. In other words, a fortnight will wipe out the
deficit, and the money market in London will be
entirely demoralised by Scaddan’s unexpected repay-
ment of the State loans. When the Opposition had
found its speech once more an unbelieving Thomas —
George is his real name—asked: “How much a week
did you say you were going to make out of
your brickworks?” McLeod: “We will make at least
a quarter of a million a week on an output of
180,000 bricks.” When I came to the lights were out
and everybody had gone home or somewhere.
* * * *
“Gazeeka” : In the many parts of Maofi-
land which are cursed with a superabundance of big
estates, the Californian thistle pest has reduced the
grazing area by a great deal. When the Noxious
Weeds Act was being enforced prosecutions were fre-
quent, and some of the squattocracy were compelled
to disgorge about £3OO per annum in wages to
thistle-cutters in order to comply with the regula-
tions. Two seasons agone, the squatters decided
that the enforcement of the Act was an undue hard-
ship on the small farmers, and the Massey Govern-
ment instructed its inspectors to administer the law
with discretion so as not to inflict any hardship on
the settlers. The inspectors went in for heaps of
discretion accordingly; but when the small farmers,
who were keeping the curse in check, realised that
the prolific growth of thistle on the big holdings was
a menace to their limited grazing areas they de-
manded that the Act be loaded to the muzzle and
fitted with a hair trigger. A commission of inquiry
was appointed, and the squatters explained the im-
possibility of keeping large areas free of the pest.
That was two years ago, and the Commission has not
yet presented its report. Meanwhile the “discre-
tion” of the North Island inspectors has been such
that there have been no prosecutions under the Act
relating to the Californian curse during that time.
There was a prosecution in the South Island last
season ; but I cannot say for certain whether it was
the cocky or the inspector who was fined. Anyhow,
it made no difference to the thistles.
“Kapina” : Re this diving argument, “Te
Pana’s” 134 fathoms ought to be the last word!
But 12 fathoms has been known to be done at
swim-diving in Torres Strait. Ten fathoms is a
common occurrence. The champion of the Brisbane
Gun Club for the last three or four years, when he
was a wild youth on one of the pearling schooners,
used to play a game with the Papuan crew. They
dived in turn and placed a stick through a ring of
the anchor chain. Each man had to carry the piece
of wood down and place it lower than it was before.
I, he failed to do so he had to drop out of the game.
The white man was usually the last in. Most of
them could go down over 10 fathoms.

Bili.v i. : His feet looked like dumplings
mapped in sacking, his hat had been chewed by a

through the old overcoat he was wearing. His nose

“A.J.8.”: The deep gulf mentioned in the
Scriptures has been dug up and dropped in between
the men engaged in the cattle and the sheep trades.
The cattle-drover—employer and employee—despises
the “monkey-dodger,” as he calls the sheep-tailer. I
saw a rattling good fight between two cattle-drovers,
all because, as one of their mates said, “the cow has
been drinking with a sheep-drover.” Another time
I was there when a cattleman rode up to our sheep
camp and inquired who was in charge of this
etcetera “mob of lice.” In the same way the beef
butcher considers it infra dig to “go on the
mutton”; but let the “mutton” man get a chance
to “go on beef” and he grabs it. lie has reached
the height of his ambition when he can “side” his
beast in “three,” or is able to take a “chain.” After
passing his exam., the meat inspector’s main desire
is to be sent to Queensland or else “put on the beef.”
The bullock is so prolific of “specimens” that he
does not mind if he gets bogged over something he
stumbles upon in the beef ; but he would reckon he
was “losing his punch” if anything in the mutton
beat him. Pigs he doesn’t like ; certainly for the
inspector there is more intricate work in pork than
in either beef or mutton, but there is less lordly
satisfaction.
* * * *
“F.Il.”: After this let all hands stop
gassing about diving. A native living at Yam Island
undertook to clear an anchor which was fouled in 11
fathoms of water. The hook belonged to a mission
boat, and it was important (on the word of the
mish) to get the vessel away that night. At 10
o’clock the boy (he was 15 years of age) went over-
board and did the job. That’s No. 1. No. 2 was a
native known as Calso. He has touched bottom at
14 fathoms and collected shell. Anybody who gives
me the harsh word does so in ignorance. lie is the
son of a monkey.
* * * *
“Moana” : A struggle is going on for a
weird sort of prohibition at Niue, one of the Cook
Islands. The drink bill there is estimated at
£3 10s. per head, but the cash isn’t directly blewed
on beer. When a native gets thirsty he toes it
up a cocoa palm, nicks a green nut, slices the
top off and drinks the milk. The Administration is
trying to break him of this old habit by placing big
iron tanks in all the villages and exhorting the brown
man to cultivate a taste for water. The loss
through wasting the nut while too immature for
copra purposes is estimated at 2000 tons per annum.
On this basis the amount done in on der-r-rink is
computed. So definite steps are being taken to
make the thirsty native leave the valuable milk
alone. But tree-climbing is man’s oldest pastime,
and cold water is hard business to push, and the
pants of the reformer are busted at the knees
through climbing up his perpendicular proposition.
* * * *
“K.Cy.”: I’ll back the Cairns hinterland
against any districts of equal extent on this planet
for variety of metalliferous ores. Taking Chillagoe
as centre and 10 miles as radius, you will find the
following ores being mined: Azurite, malachite, bor-
nite, red and black oxide and sulphide of copper;
galena, cerrusite and native silver; antimony, carb.
and sulphide, molybdenite, bismuth carb. and sulph. ;
tin, lode, “toad’s-eye” and ruby ; wolfram and scheel-
ite ; zinc, worse luck. Not to mention a number of
metals and minerals which are at present of little
or no commercial importance. Certainly no part of
Australia offers such inducements for practical pro-
spectors. Plenty of grass and water for horses are
among the advantages.

“Talk about floods! Why, last summer the
crik rose that high that it poured down
the chimney and put me fire out."
“Boronia”: I’ve already mentioned about
20 uses for Westralia’s fine tree, the “blackboy.”
Here's another: at motor-bike races recently held
near Perth the winner almost put up a record for
fastest time under ordinary road conditions, on spirit
distilled from the heart of the vegetable. I’ve got a
bit of a bank balance to repeat that the blackboy is
the most useful thing growing in Bushtralia.
A WINTER TALE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A crowd of drinkers stood around
And lent respectful ears
To anything he cared to say,
And seemed, moreover, pleased to pay
For all his smokes and beers!
“Who is this proud and noble one?”
I asked. “What has the fellow done?”
A worshipper looked at me till '
I blushed, and felt like one
Who’s seen a blind man’s beer to steal,
Or rob a tenor of his meal,
Or kick a Jew for fun!
“Gorbli’me! that’s the bloke,” cried he,
“Who stoushed th’ ’umpire, Saturdee!”

OH, ho! So that’s the reason. You know we told you how some of our
trade friends advised us not to try to sell “De Reszke” Cigarettes to
Australians—“they have no taste for such good ones.”
Well, we’ve been looking into things, and we find that these said friends are
doing quite a nice business here, despite the fact that their trade in England
no longer exists to any degree.
No wonder they don’t want the “De Reszke” Cigarette to come “jumping in.”
It makes us feel we would like to get a little of the publicity limelight on io
their names, but —well, anyway, what does it matter?
The admission is made. The truth is out. So long as the “De Reszke” was
not offered for sale in Australia, why, Australians could not know they were
better.
“ E RESZKE” Cigarettes are not only being “offered” for sale, but are
| ) being bought by appreciative Australians, whose numbers are rapidly
increasing. Tobacconists have sold out of “De Reszkes” so quickly
that to fill their re-orders we were compelled to cable to England for trebled
supplies.
Are you one of those who have given the “De Reszke” a trial and found its
flavor finer, its aroma more pleasing, its mildness exquisitely agreeable? Or
are you one of those who are still sticking to their old brand without troubling
to find out a better Cigarette? If you are in the latter class, and want to get
into the former, the quickest way to do so is to get a box of “De Reszke”
Cigarettes from* one of the “De Reszke” Agents whose names appear below.
The “ De Reszke’ Cigarettes you buy in your own Store are identical in every way with those
that are sold in England. They are made by J. Millhoff & Co., Ltd., 86, Piccadilly, London,
England, and reach you in exactly the same condition as the smoker in England receives them.

The Inky Way.
Dear Bulletin, —My attention lias been
drawn to a paragraph contributed to your
Inky Way (9/7/44) column, which deals
with Labor papers in W.A., and reprints
some misstatements which have been assidu-
ously circulated by some of the minor jour-
nals in this State. Firstly the Westralian
Laborites have not yet expressed an inten-
tion of starting a daily newspaper on
£15.000. A company is being promoted for
that purpose with a capital of £50,000, and
the promoters have stated that they will not
even register the company (not start the
paper) till £15,000 cash is in the bank. The
present position is, I believe, that about
£ 20,000 worth of shares have been applied
for, and about £14,500 cash has been re-
ceived. Now, as to the Worker. Its circu-
lation has been increasing rapidly of late,
and for the last two weeks has been just on
14,000. Of this number less than 5000
copies are purchased by the Goldfields and
other provincial unions on account of their
members, and the balance are disposed of
through the ordinary channels, newsagents,
bookstalls, etc. There is no foundation for
the statement that jobs on the literary stall'
are precarious or underpaid. The permanent
staff includes five members of the Australian
Journalists’ Association, all of whom have
had previous newspaper experience, and the
general rate of pay is higher than that stipu-
lated by the A.J.A. log or that paid by other
offices running similar journals. The posi-
tions are permanent, the directors do not in
any way interfere with the literary staff, and
there is an entire absence 'of “pinpricks.
We send you a copy of the journal for in-
spection. that you may judge whether our
10cS columns and half-dozen illustrations
would not command a sale at a penny on
merits. It may also be of interest that our
paid up capital is only £ 3OOO . but under the
control of our directors, “without any
special Qualification,” a reserve fund of
nearly £750 has been accumulated out of
profits. The genesis of the complaint is not
really Hint the Worker is doing badly, but
that it is doing too well to be pleasant to
envious persons.—Yours, etc., Arthur
Levetus, General Manager.
Buried at the age of 74, Hermann Wil-
helm I’uttman, Melbourne’s most ancient
printer, or thereabouts. After some years
in Tasmania lie crossed to Melbourne, and
joined Fergusson and Moore, a well-known
printing firm. In 1872 he became one of
the partners in McCarron, Bird and Co., and
was still in that business at his death 42
years later. Away from type be did a good
deal of literary work, was secretary of the
Deutscher Turn Verein, one of the founders
of the German Club, and for a time presi-
dent of the Master Printers.
“Melb ” : Florrie Finn gives chatty lec-
tures in our suburbs on her Grand Tour of
England, Egypt, - etc., illustrated with photos
of her own taking; and the little entertain-
ment is much appreciated. I do not, off-
hand, remember another woman taking this
line, essayed by so many men, though Miss
Finkelstein and Miss Hughes have lectured
us on their visits to Jerusalem. Miss Finn
is granddaughter of Edmund Finn, author
of “Garryowen’s” extremely minute
“Chronicles of Melbourne,” in two big ( vol-
umes of small print. When Governor Stan-
ley recently declared Northcote a city the
Mayor presented him with a copy, and Stan-
ley said he really could not receive anything
more valuable for his office. The son of
“Garryowen,” Edmund 11., father of Flo-
rence, is a well-known litterateur and dra-
matic critic; and bis brother, I'at, is some-
what noted in that line both in Sydney and
Melbourne. Edmund 11. competed worthily
with Marcus Clarke, Akhurst and Garnet
Walch in supplying the Melbourne stage
with Christmas pantomimes, works of art
in their way, carefully rhymed, well localised
and infinitely superior to the maudlin im-
ported trash of Hickory Wood and the like.
I want Dyson to get to this work. And
Dennis.
* * * *
“Curlew”: Tassy is now the only State
that ambles along without a Hansard to
preserve the weighty utterances of its
mighty statesmen. For years South Aus-
tralia put thousands into the pockets of the
Conservative newspaper proprietors, who
printed pages of dry-as-dust speeches, then
lifted the type and published the matter in
book-form. But the Flyspeek State does not
work on such an elaborate scale. It merely
subsidises the Fusion organ, which gives a
more or less full account of the proceedings,
then clins the reports and pastes them in a
book. Tasmanians never seem to worry
about the pearls of wisdom that must be
lost under this system. Neither do I. The
new S.A. Hansard staff began its work last
week, and the Register and Advertiser will
suffer an annual loss of £lOOO or so each.
This verbatim reporting of Parliament had
an evil influence over all journalistic work
in that State, with the result that journalism
there has been the dullest in the Common-
wealth for long past. The staffs strive after
length, and merit is accredited by the foot-
rule. It’s a fool of a system that does that.
“0.C.C.”: Heaven forbid that I should
hurt anybody’s feelings, but — Well. In
the dear, old S. M. Hekald the other day
there appeared an example, or instance —if
it wasn’t a symptom or portent —of blithering
asininity. A Yankee parson, who had never
even seen Australia before, landed off the
Yank mailboat, and (of course) Granny
and Auntie, not forgetting the other “great
dailies,” sent along their young men to sit
at the feet and hymn-book of our visit- r.
It must have been Grandma’s young mar
who asked the question, because this is hov
it appeared in her hallowed columns
I can say nothing about Australia yet. I have not
been here long enough.
The inference is that the old lady's young
man actually asked the lamentable question,
“How do you like (or, What do you think
of) Australia?” It reminds me of the first
time I ever realised the sublime uselessness
(in the circs.) of such a quesion. In 1880
the late J. Chamberlain went to the United
States as Fisheries Commissioner. When
the old Cunarder arrived off Sandy Hook it
was met by the usual tug, conveying about
30 myrmidons of the New York press, who
badly wanted to “intervoo” the redoubtable
Britisher. They went aboard, were “inter-
dooced” and waved ,to seats. Then (of
course) the fatuousest reporter of the lot
put the absurd old N’York question, “Well,
Mr. Chamberlain, and how do you like our
city?” Joseph screwed in his eyeglass:
“One moment, gentlemen. Before 1 answer
that question let me ask you one.” Cer-
tainly.” “Yep.” “Go right ahead, sir,”
etc., etc. “What” (winningly) “do you
think of Birmingham?” Significant silence;
then one chump burst out. “Say, Mr. Cham-
berlain ; we never saw Birmingham! \\ e
don’t know a thing about it.” “Exactly,
my dear sir; nor I of New Yorl;! The
pressmen took it in good part, though not
to know all about New York before you’d
even left the steamer was —in those days—
a horrible admission of ignorance. After
that the New York reporters got out of
the way of asking distinguished strangers
that question until they (the strangers) had
been in the City of Gotham for quite three
days.
* * * *
“J.F.H.”: A London literary agency
announces its readiness to supply plots of
amorous, humorous and sensational short-
stories at 2s. each. This is fairly cheap raw
material for manufacturers, provided the
stuff is really capable of being worked up
into readable matter. The scheme is cer-
tainly quite feasible; thus, in the literary
partnership of Besant and Rice which pro-
duced “Ready Money Mortiboy” and a bushel
of other first-class novels, Rice furnished
the plots and Besant did most of the writing.
At this new London literary bureau a norm
will also procure you “jokes suitable for
illustration,” also “scenarios and synopses of
plays” and “notes for speeches, sermons and
addresses on any subject.” But a florin for
the mere “notes” for a sermon won t do.
There are other agencies in London ready to
sell not merely notes but complete sermons
for a bob aoiece. These are the old stock
of red-nosed, unfrocked parsons, who aie
plentiful in Babylon-beside-the-Thames.
A LITANY FOR SOME WHO BADLY NEED ONE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A correspondent recently suggested that there
should be prayer in the churches for journalists,
who, he said, were the most difficult class to reach
religiously. The Devil, the flesh and Mammon are
unceasing in their desperate efforts to sieze and ex-
ploit this coveted influence. 1 have asked _ if any
reader knew of a diocesan litany which included
prayers for the Press, but 1 have not heard of one.
I hope the time will soon come when every diocesan
litany will include a prayer for editors, authors and
journalists.— News item.
General Prayer.
Lord, in deep anguish and distress,
We kneel and ask Thee now to bless
The stubborn, wilful, wicked Press!
We beseech Thee to hear us!
Of all the miserable sinners
Who love strong liquors and good dinners,
None are so vile as these word-spinners—
Have mercy upon these miserable writers!
Prayers for Editors.
Give ear, O Lord, to this our earnest prayer
For Editors —the good ones are most rare ;
The other sort don’t either know or care—
Good Lord, enlighten them !
Harsh, proud, unfeeling—off home at eleven ;
With hearts set on all things, save those of Heaven;
Heedless of wrongs to “Justice” and “Mother of
Seven” —
Good Lord, still pity them !
Remember not their offences or their capers
In “forwarding” the weird “policies” of their papers ;
The fault lies with the importers! and big drapers—
Have mercy upon Editors!
Prayer for Leader-Writers.
For these invisible men, censorious—
Full of quotations and vain-glorious—•
We ask Thine aid; make them victorious
Over Infinitives split, uproarious!
Save all leader-writers, we pray Thee!
Prayer for Sub-Editors.
Ilowe’er so deeply sunk in Sin —-
Howe’er so near Thy Wrath —-
Still let the blessed work begin,
Nor sweep these from the Path !
How black their misdeeds none may say;
Though, Thou Lord, knowest well;
Yet, for sub-editors we pray
To save their souls from Hell !
Spare even sub-editors, O Lord!
Prayer for Reporters.
Note. — 7/ any be still hostile or hot-minded among
the Brethren, for that a Reporter hath injured
or slighted him {as he thinketh), it is incum-
bent upon him to refrain from uttering the
responses herein. But if he do fully and freely
forgive any that hath slighted, spurned or
cruelly condemned trim (as he thinketh), he
shall join heartily in the responses.
Hardened and unregenerate—deaf and blind;
To mirth much given; to roystefing inclined;
Oft late at duties in Thy House “assigned”—
Yet. have mercy upon Reporters, 0 Lord!
But blow upon them with Thy chastening wind,
For wayward lambs are 'these,' who do not mind
Long vigils in vile service; who, unkind,
Write not the blessing and the joy they find
Among Thy servants, Lord, meek and resigned—
Spare—after due chastisement —the Reporters, O
Lord !
i’ltavmt for Sporting and Other Department
Reporters.
Look, O Lord, with deep compassion
On all who draw a daily ration
From Horse or Mammon, Stoush or Fashion—
We beseech Thee to convert them, 0 Lord!
Prayer for the Religious Editor.
Not to appeal, 0, Lord, so much as praise,
Do we uplift our voice upon this phase ;
For lo! as was the One Just Man in Sodom,
In the inky mirth appeareth Brother Goddom!
Graciously look upon the Reliyious Editor, 0 Lord!
0 Lord, arise, help us; and incline his heart to
print us more!
Prayer for Newspaper Proprietors.
0 !
[At this moment the church was struck by light-
ning, and the Litany closed with a bang.]
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

Confidences
Concerning
the Modern
Blarney Stone
The ancient Blarney Stone is known to
practically everybody. As it is situated near
the top of Blarney Castle an acrobatic feat
was necessary to reach it.
In recent years, however, a little alteration
has been made to the building, and now
visitors can kiss the Blarney Stone quite
easily.
But the modern Blarney Stone is not a stone
at all, and you never have to imperil your
neck to kiss it.
It is a cylinder that fits any Edison Phonograph, and upon it you can record your
voice in the quiet of your home just as easily as you speak into the telephone.
Then, by a simple adjustment, ■ the phonograph will repeat to you what you have
spoken or sung into it.
Thus you have an opportunity "to study your own voice and manner of speech,
correct the faults you discover and ultimately acquire the gift of tongue, which
according to the old Irish legend can be achieved by kissing the Blarney Stone.

“0.K.”: In his “Pygmalion” play Bernard
Shaw is much more of an unreasonable re-
publican than a caustic philosopher. His
heroine is a matured cockney flower-girl, of
degraded parentage, whom an eccentric mil-
lionaire trains in the airs and graces of
super-polite society. She is presented as
a soft-spoken and elegantly-mannered young
woman after a. year’s tuition. But she re-
mains true to her old vulgarities of diction,
and she startles a company of “upper middle
class” admirers by saying “Not blanky
likely” or “No blanky fear” in the dulcet
tones of a delicately nurtured duchess. This
is an absurd assertion of democratic doc-
trine. Shaw apparently would suggest that
because the same human nature is common
to all grades of womanhood, and refinement
is no-t born but made, therefore a gutter-
bred girl can be taken in her teens and
taught refinement of accent. But the edu-
cation of speaking tones begins in infancy.
It is a matter of environment rather than
precept. A good “choice” of language can
he acquired by any intelligent, self-educated
woman up to the age of 40, but her tones of
utterance can be improved only a little after
her speaking voice is formed. You can
make a graceful, stylish, well-mannered
woman out of a 17-year-old vulgarienne, but
you can’t make her speak like, say, the late
Queen Victoria, any more than you can teach
,a brainy youth from a factory to talk
exactly like the empty-headed son of a
hundred earls. , Tuition in accent comes to
a child through the ear, and it comes to
stay. As an aid to the obliteration of class
distinction in a democratic country gentle
speaking should be taught in State schools
for all it is worth.
“J.F.IL”: (from London) : Oswald Stoll,
of the Coliseum, has made few mistakes, but
he probably puts high up in the list his
weakness in permitting Marinetti, the leader
of the Futurists, to produce liis “Grand Con-
cert of Noise Tuners.” There were 23 of
these fearsome instruments, including buz-
zers, exploders, thunderers, whistlers, mur-
murers, gurglers, rattlers, cracklers, roarers
and other ear-splitting inventions. They all
combined to reproduce the “awakening of a
great city” in the morning. The audience’s
idea was that the uproar provided
a pretty fair imitation of a riot
in a menagerie, and it signified its disap-
proval in the usual manner. Music niay
have been all wrong in the past, as the
Futurists assert; but it will take a century
or two of diligent education before the aver-
age music hall-goer will be able to appreciate
the “synthetically-treated noise emotions”
which caused such ructions in the Coliseum.
“Mime”: A cable message mentions that
Miss Gladys Cooper (Mrs. Buckmaster) is
suing “a London weekly paper” for libel, the
journal having alleged that her husband was
going to divorce her. Gladys Cooper is one
of the many theatrical stars who have risen
from the chorus. Seven years ago she was a
lovely but quite obscure inhabitant of the
“Girls of Gottenburg” ballet. Since then
she has played lead in musical comedy,
passed on from that to starring parts in
Wilde and Shaw comedies, and got married.
The weeklies, such as Modern Society, the
London Mail, etc., which specialise in ex-
clusive. information as to what is about to
occur in social and theatrical circles, have
;been having a torrid time of it lately. One
judge termed them a “pest” and another “a
disgrace to British journalism.” They pub-
lish paragraphs such as this: “We hear that
the little lady from Daly’s Theatre again
week-ended at Brighton with the sporting
Marquis. When is her husband going to
object, or is he paid to know nothing?” This
sort of thing thrills the suburbs to the
marrow even when —as is usually the case—
it is meaningless. On the rare occasions
when someone can take it as applying to him-
or herself there is a libel action and rage
and fury everywhere but in legal circles.
“Bobbie”: Courtneidge seems to have
scored a bull’s-eye with his latest production
at the Shaftesbury (London). “The Cinema
Girl” is pretty much of a comic opera, bright
and tuneful, with a strong farcical comedy
| scene for the central episode in the plot.
The Girl is a “star” film actress, and she
1 makes an ungodly show of a Pecksniffian
humbug who has been proclaiming the im-
; morality of picture plays in his addresses to
the Y.M.C.A., and who also happens to be
the father-in-law-elect of her faithless lover.
The actress dupes the pompous purist into
the belief that she is a Russian princess
violently enamored of him. She makes an
appointment; he proceeds to offer her his
! endearments, and then he discovers that they
I have been photographed all the time for
moving-picture purposes. The fooling of a
fatuous admirer by a pretty Delilah is a
familiar subject for farcical comedy, but the
trick of the Cinema Girl gives it a new and
up-to-date interest. The result is as com-
pletely successful as the second act of “The
Arcadians.” where the conventional melo-
drama scene of the horserace is presented
under novel conditions.
“Melb”: Weathersby, producer here of
“The Yeoman of the Guard,” has a letter
from Gilbert explaining its genesis. This
piece was so different from former Savoyans
that Gilbert and Sullivan were anxious about
it; but they felt they must develop a fresh
vein. Gilbert got the first idea of “The Yeo-
man” from a placard of the Tower Furnish-
ing Co., with a Beefeater on it. This sug-
gested the Tower and Beefeaters. Yet for a
while the author did not get beyond the
notion of a modern piece, with eccentrics of
the day in the Tower and a Beefeater
chorus. These were to be young ladies,
guardsmen and so forth. Then he bought a
magazine, in which the picture of a jester
struck him, suggesting Jack Point. Further
thinking made him put the time back to the
16th century. His first title was “The Beef-
eaters.” Sullivan advised “Yeoman of the
Guard,” although the yeomen have no pro-
per relation to the Tower. Gilbert thought
it their best piece of work, taking one with
the other, and Sullivan agreed.

King.” before King George, with stars
shining all round him. It reminds me of
Ellen Terry once taking the part of a ser-
vant girl, whose only job was to fetch in a
letter. This was at a fashionable charitable
amateur show, held at the zenith of her

The Hum That Failed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O, mister, I’ve got no time for them
I city blokes after the way me and
me mate Scotty was treated dirty by
one of ’em in co’lition with an ole cove about
’ere wot we thought was a friend of ours up
to then.
“It was like this: Me an’ Scotty had done
in our chippin’ cheques an’ a bit over at this
’ere pub, and the boss had hinted pretty
plain, though not too perlite, that our slates
was full an’ our swags gettin’ fat for want
of exercise. He guv us an eye-opener w’en
he opened the bar that mornin’, but w'en we
put it on ’iin for another he got personal and
said things wot wouldn’t be took from no-
body but a publican w’en a feller’s stiff.
“Well, about nine o’clock me an’ Scotty
was sittin’ on this same form feelin’ p’ticular
bad —nearly as bad as we do now, Mister —
w’en a toffy-lookin' ten traveller or somethin’,
wot had treated us with spurm w’en we put
the acid on ’im, came on to tli’ verandah to
meet the cockies who were coming in for
their sugar money. Me an Scotty said
‘good-day,’ affable-like, to the cockies, an’
never sed ‘Bill’ or ‘Jim’ or such to them we
used to work with before they was cockies.
An’ we called the clarsy ones ‘mister’ and
‘sir.’ But our amiableness didn’t do no good.
Did y’ ever notice ’ow dry talkin’ makes a
feller, Mister?
“Anyhow, then up comes our quandong
friend wot I mentioned before, ole Joe Lovett.
W’en we saw ’im we knoo we was all right,
’cos Joe ’adn’t been in for months, an’ was
free with his money. We sits up an’ nods
to Joe careless-like, an’ he ses ‘Mornin,’ Jim;
inornin’, Scotty. Come an’ ’ave a’——
“We was standing up by this time.
“ ‘Game of quoits for three pots.’
“We knoo he was too good for us an’ was
just sittin’ down agen very dejected-like w’en
he ses, ‘Well, then, come in an’ ’ave a’-
“Me an’ Scotty was half-way to the bar
w’en that rag-sellin’ tailor’s dummy from
Bundy chips in with ‘l’ll give you a toss-up,
ole chap.’
“Ole Joe was very ondecided, air me an’
Scotty was jerkin’ our ’eads towards the bar
like ’ell, but he goes off with the swell, him
not thinkin’ like you, Mister, that a pot does
a man good after a booze up.
“Well, an idea gets a.hblt on me an' I
ses: ‘Come on,. Scotty. We’ll run the game
for ’em, so’s one of ’em must blow, off.’ I
appoints Scotty ref’ree and me judge, an’
we acted in them capacities, at the same
time criticisin’ the play an’ callin’ out, ‘Good
quoit, sir !’ an’ ‘Well thrown, Joe !’ an’ ceterer;
ah’ measurin’ distances with a strip of cane
rind till the end, w’en ole Joe won. Me an'
Scotty was jub’lant an’ led the percession
t’wards the bar. There we turned an’ were
s’prised to see Joe on’y half-way an’ the
goggle-eyed bloke holdin’ the quoits in his
’ands an’ sayin, ‘Let’s ’ave another game.’
The ole feller looked at the bar kinder linger-
in’, an’ wiped his lips with his tongue, so I
knoo he was hoggin’ for a pot same as me
an’ Scotty are now, but he went back; Me
an’ Scotty never sed nothin’—just resoomed
our onerous juties silent-like, but I looked at
’im and he looked at me.
“The second game the stranger won, an’
as it was up to Joe to shout the judge and
ref’ree guv their verdic’ accordin’. We was
breastin’ the barrier before the last quoit:
stopped rollin’, w’en we heard that blessed;
how-to-keep-thin advertisement sayin’, plaus-:
ible like : “Exc’lent, exc’lent! Game all! |
Let’s have the cornkrer!’ I admit, Mister,,
that ole man Lovett tried manfully to resist
an’ get the cove to put his chest against the
mehog’ny, but; eventchally he let hisself be
persuaded agenst his good resolutions an’
resoomed playin’, not thinkin’ of us two wot
'ad been doin’ all the work and barrackin'
an’ gettin’ wobbly at the knees an’ specks
before the eyes, ’cos too much talkin’ on a
dry throat is bad for fellers after a spree.
Ain’t it. Mister?
“Anyhow, bad as we was—and are —we
never sed nothin’ out loud, but I looked at
Scotty, an’ Scotty looked at me, as we took
up our positions agen, consolin’ ourselves by
thinkin’ that this game would settle it an’
that w’en Joe had got outside of the first pot
there’d be plenty for a few days.
“T.’ resoom —in the third game, ’leven up.
they was nine all an’ the excitement intense
the judge and ref’ree losin’ their blocks an’
barrackin’ for all thev was worth, though
feelin’ cruel weak through want of a pint
and husky in the throat from talkin’. You
might notice them kinder symptoms on me
now. Mister. At any rate, the stranger won
an’ we slung up our ’ats, ’cos Joe wouldn’t;
leave his ole mates out w’en he blew. Well
while the winner was offerin’ Joe his condo-
lations for losin’, he says: ‘Will y’ join me
in a glass of somethin’, an’ Joe sed he didn’t
mind if he did, an’ Scotty an’ me sed we
didn’t mind too. So we adjourns to the bar.
me an’ Scotty praisin’ up the play, w’en the
winner looks straight through my head an’
ses to Joe: “Wot’s yours, Mr. —. I
haven’t the pleasure of your name. ‘’Ere’s
my card,’ Joe ses. ‘Mine’s a pint, no frorth.
I’m Joe Lovett.’ Then the city cove calls for
a sherry and bitters, an’ you may throw me
down a cliff if the treacle-haired son of a
Scotch Jew didn’t plank down a bob, an’
me an’ Scotty in the comnanv, so to speak,
an’ bavin’ done all the judgin’ an’ refereein’.
an’ sufferin’ terrible in the inside for reasons
I’ve told you about. Now. Mister, wot do
you think of fellers that’ll let a feller graft
and talk like that for ’em an’ then forget
he’s got a neck to be wetted? I ses it show?
their ign’rance. but a gent, like you wot’s
been about a bit wouldn’t turn a cove wot’s
sufferin’ a recovery down that way. would
yer now?
“Well, w’en we seen the bob on the counter
me an’ Scottv never said nothin’, but 'I
looked through the door an’ Scotty looked
through the winder. No. we never spoke
till we hit Currajong Creek, where we both
looked up for the first time an’ sed simul-
tanvus: ‘Well, I’m damned.’
“Did I mention. Mister, that me an’
Sco+ty have done in our cheques again, an’,
as I might have let slip while talkin’, we are
feelin’ real particular crook just now for
want of a couple of P" fs . me throat bein’
particular dry an’ husky?’’
W. T. COLMAN.
From London: Old Australian playgoers
will remember George Fawcett Rowe, who
ran the Princess’s Theatre (Melb.) for some
years. His name now rarely appears in
print, but it has been figuring of late in the
papers here in connection with a new version
of “David Copperfield,” by L. N. Parker,
which Beerbolim Tree is about to put on at
His Majesty’s. G. F. Rowe is recalled as
“the finest and most realistic impersonator
of Mr. Micawber on record,” and the theatri-
cal gossips are wondering whether Tree’s
Micawber will equal it. Parker is making a
fresh dramatisation of the novel, and has
taken some liberties with Dickens. Micaw-
ber was sent off to Australia by Dick-
ens, who left him there; but Parker brings
the story to an end with the return of the
old drifter, prosperous and benevolent, and
eager to pronounce a blessing upon the union
of Agnes and David.

WHAT DYSPEPTICS SHOULD KNOW.
PHYSICIAN’S TIMELY ADVICE.
The fact that few dyspeptics ideally under-
stand the cause of their trouble is probably
the reason why drugs are still used to such
an alarming extent. The practice is wrong,
however, and is strongly condemned by many
physicians.
The presence of excess acid in the stomach
is the cause of practically all forms of
digestive trouble, and this can be neutralised
and rendered harmless by the occasional use
of ordinary bisurated magnesia—a product
which can be obtained of any chemist.
Physicians use bisurated magnesia them-
selves as well as recommend it to their
patients, because they recognise that it not
only overcomes dangerous stomach acid and
thus ensures easy digestion, but by its use
no harmful drug is introduced into the system,
and therefore there can be no unpleasant
after-effects. Half a teaspoonful taken in a
little water after meals is usually quite
sufficient to keep the food contents of the
stomach free from fermentation and acid,
thus ensuring normal digestion.*
What is the Best Beauty Soap
for the Complexion ?
There is at present a great controversy
going on in the English newspapers about
the best soap to use for the complexion.
Some advocate the use of one and some
another kind, but they are all satisfied on
one or tw y o points, and those are that for a
soap to be beneficial to the skin, must be
one without coloi'ing matter, and witlmut a
lot of strong, coarse, irritating perfume being
added, and also that the soap must be made
from good ingredients without a preponder-
ance of soda, which makes the skin hard.
An English soapmaker has been able to
combine all the above good qualities in a
soap, and* at the same time to incorporate
some of the puritol wax, which is so much
in demand as a complexion beautifier and
protective, so that on washing with Puritol
Wax Soap there is a trace of the wax left
on the skin, which, by its continuous sooth-
ing and corrective action on the skin and
its pores, keeps - itr in a healthy and pure
state, and orevents the formation of pimples
and blemishes on it.

A POLITICIAN'S GHOST .
THE KID AUSTRALIA: “Gant, es trying to
kid me that foe's doin' that talking."
[Devised and drawn by OSWALD PRVOU, Moonta
mines, South Australia.]
Said the Sydney Telegraph a very few
days ago : —• *'
'there lias , even been a suggestion that the London
Stock .Exchange should close, which is unthinkable.
And promptly the unthinkable happened.
Even John Bull is mortal.
Justice Bring, of N. S. Wales, made an
absurd mistake during the Boorabil pro-
ceedings last week, when he asked Premier
llolman if he had kept the minutes taken by
him of a certain Cabinet meeting, and if
not why not, or wolds to that effect. Under
the British Governmental system, which
Australia has imported, a Cabinet that was
convicted of taking minutes of its delibera-
tions would be hanged—or at any rate,
something serious would happen to it.
Leonard Courtney lays this down very firmly
in “The Working Constitution of the U.K.
and its Outgrowths.” Also Gladstone said
once, in that er cathedra manner of which
lie was such an admitted master, “No one
may take set-notes during Cabinet proceed-
ings. Even the Prime Minister must rely
on memoranda jotted down on a scrap of
paper for the material on which to make his
report to the Sovereign.” In Australia, of
course, the convention of reporting the pro-
ceedings in Cabinet does not exist. Before
George I.’s day the Sovereign used to attend
Cabinet meetings. George abandoned the
practice, as he couldn’t understand his Min-
isters’ language, and had more interesting
things to do ariyhow. Which was how the
reporting custom originated in England.
FSigned: Hans Tlempirb, 8 Lager-strasse, Sydney.]
A Tic. viewpoint:—
The result of the Essenclon (Vic.) State by-elec-
tion should give Willie Watt the cold shivers, if rot
the horrors. The Liberal was well known and popu-
lar in the district, the Laborite a comparative stran-
ger, yet the latter turned Watt’s 1400 majority of
his last election into a Liberal deficit of 1000 votes.
But the Liberal had been assisted by several Mini-
sters and selected and helped by Watt himself —and
apparently the electors had little use for Watt's
nominee. The alleged “great sacrifice” didn't gull
them, any more than it does the Balaclava Liberals.
At the yery hour of Labor's victory Carty Salmon,
in St. Kikla Town Hall, was announcing his retire-
ment from the Balaclava contest before the pushful
insistence of William Watt and the A.W.N.C. That
Salmon was foolish is undoubted. Possibly lie lacked
stomach for a fight; but with pluck and energy the
scat .was his for the taking. Now it is more than
possible it will go to Labor. When he told how,
after canvassing the electorate for months, he was
pushed out by the A.W.N.L. because of his connec-
tion with the “old Deakin party,” the meeting
groaned, and when he finally announced his with-
drawal one listener blurted out, “This underhand
business is no good to me. I will not vote for
Watt; I shall vote for Labor,” and was cordially
( cheered. Which looks by no means healthy for
Watt.
[F. C. .Tones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
C. A. Jeffries (214 George-street, Sydney) :
Joe Cook, speaking at Bathurst (vide
S. M. Herald, 23/7/’l4), stated that
Fisher had deliberately misrepresented him
when he (Fisher) said the Liberals in-
tended to borrow for Defence purposes—
they had never thought of such a thing. I
do not know whether the Fusion crowd did
think of borrowing for Defence purposes.
But I do know that Cook himself, when
addressing the Farmers’ Conference at
Maryborough (Vic.) said: “The man is
mad who proposes to pay for Defence out of
revenue.”
The cat that goes too often to the well
is drowned at last. Something like live
years ago Germany warned Russia not to
interfere with Austria’s annexation of Bosnia
and Herzegovina, and Russia, being crippled
by the unlucky Jap disturbance, backed down
with curses. It may not have pro-
posed to interfere, but the warning that it
was to play the Scriptural part of Rahab-
sit-still hurt its pride. The other day it
received an ultimatum from the same source
that it must not interfere on behalf of its
fellow-Slav in Servia. Then Rahab-sit-still
grew utterly wild, and refused to be dictated
to. and the party who sent the ultimatum
had to declare war or lose all his dignity.
So the Russo-German trouble started.
Canada threatens to rlo a burst of cheap
“loyalty” by sending 30,000 ill-trained men
to help the Ma Country in the embittered
hour of scrap. If, by any chance, Germans
or others were to attack Canada while these
scrappers were away the Yankees —between
ninety and a hundred millions of them—
would have to defend the country in pur-
suance of their policy of preventing any
European race getting a new foothold on the
American continent. So Canada can afford
to do a dash. The offer was reported in the
Maoriland Legislature, and that wild and
foolish Jingo conglomeration shouted “Well
done, Canada!” and sang “God save.” But
Maoriland has no ninety or a hundred million
white citizens just across a long land border
to depend upon in time of need, so it had
better not burst out in emulation of Canada.
Joseph Ward, of course, heaped himself on
his feet, and talked of sending troops for the
defence of the Empire. But unfortunately
Wellington is likely to be in much more
danger than London. Still Ward can get
a,way from Wellington, while poorer citizens
can’t. And Ward’s possible peerage is wait-
ing in London—not in Wellington.

The war trouble in Europe lias made
things very cold and bitter for the Austra-
lian candidate. Pages and pages of news-
paper space which he expected to get have
been filled with military intelligence. On a
scanty and miserable back sheet it is men-
tioned that Mr. Jones addressed the elec-
tors, or that Mr. Smith also ran, or that
Air. Brown fell at the first hurdle, or that
Mr. Robinson was left at the post, or that
Mr. Blenkiusop sprung a leak and sank, or
that the Liberal or Labor League nominated
Mr. Podsnap, but the marine insurance com-
panies refused to take the risk at less than
20 guineas per cent, for fear of capture by
bushrangers in the Red Sea.

LAW AND ORDER.
[?] episode in the life of James Dooley, of Dublin.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Imsii Timks, the Dublin Unionist paper,
suggests that the Nationalist and Ulster volunteers
should co-operate to preserve the public peace.
—Cable item.
Now here’s a line suggestion
To settle all the question;
A proof to every Irishman that merricles don't cease ;
For wid our right arms swingin’,
And wid our lift arms dingin’,
Man an’ boy we’ll all of us preserve the pubic
peace.
I met wid Dan Macfarhin;
Oclione, but Dari’s a darlin’ ;
’Tis Dan of all me Ulster frinds is just the frind 1
want.
“Danny,” sen I, “me beauty,
’Tis me an’ you for duty,
Upholdin’ law an' order in the fields a bit bevant.”
Now what a boy is Danny!
Cold fear? lie hasn’t anny ;
lie stripped his coat and came wid me to Murtagh
Ryan's door.
Wid sponge an’ tow’l an’ water
Did Ryan watch the slaughter,
While Dan an’ me uphild the peace a solid hour an’
more.
“Begad,” sez Daniel, spittin’
The tooth I had been hittin’,
While I was washin’ down me face and feelin’ for
me cars, j
“1 love ye, Shamus Dooley,
For ye have taught me trooly
Mrtro points in law an’ order than I've felt in forty
years.”
“Me frind,” sez I (still coughin’),
•*v" “I’ve fought wid Curran often,
For him and me was shipmates when lie started in
the game;
But Dan, me. boy, be aisy;
Your lift hook is a daisy ;
It caused me for to keep the peace more times than
I could name.”
“Dan has a jolt,” says Murty,
“A good bit worse than dirty ;
lie brought it underneath ver gyard as often as he
could.
; And James, ye crossed him sweetly,
And uppercut him neatly ;
F’r law-preservin’ citizens ye both was pretty good.”
“ ’Tis felony and arson.”
Sez I, “to shtand wid Carson.”
“,’Tis Hell to pay,” says Daniel, “if Ulsther’s hot
‘set right.” *
“Aw, close yer mouths,” sez Rvan;
“That’s fish for others’ fry in’ ;
Yez two came here to keep the peace ; and now ye’ll
want to fight!”
“Ryan,” sez I, “is right, Dan ;
There’s manny ways to fight, Dan ;
And phwat the Hell is twinty rounds fought fair
’twixt friend an’ friend?
Bruises will heal, that’s sartin ;
But bad words keep on smartin’ ;
Till law an’ order gets a hurt no man on earth can
iner.d.”
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
"S.T.R.”: Adelaide has always had a
speciollv-kind remembrance of Albert Trott. It- was
on the Oval that he made his meteoric first appear:
a nee in international cricket —January, 1895, when
Blackham and Turner and Lyons and Jarvis were all
finishing their careers, and 'Jones and I redale and
Darling and Howell were starting. Stoddart's XI
had won the two test matches so far played—in
Melbourne by sheer superiority; in Sydney by a
change of weather just as Australia had the game
apparently won. Albert came in for the third test,
and caused a shock. The Englishmen couldn’t get
him out, either innings, and in one of their own
innings couldn’t play his bowling. The finest series
of catches I have ever seen (by Harry, Worrall,
Bruce, Darling, Trott) helped his average. That
he showed inferior form afterwards, and so was not
chosen for the 1896 XI, whereupon he emigrated, is
part of the history of cricket.. My point is that his
brilliant showing in Adelaide led to the historic
fifth test in Melbourne, the only time (in defiance of
•the law of probabilities) that the scores have been
two all when the fifth began.. Also, till the. South
Africans came, Australia never lost a subsequent
match on the Adelaide Oval.
N.S.W. Cricket Association last week
was still climbing the thorny path by kick-
ing at compulsory training,, owing to the in-
roads which the national duty is alleged
to be making on cricket. '"At the annual meet-
ing one W. Bardsley marie- an awful noise over
it. declaring that compulsory ‘ training was the great-
est enemy the Association ha’s, also that it was “an
evil influence.” In spite of -the fact that War Lord
Milieu announced he was prepared to receive a depu-
tation on the subject, the roars of Bardsley and the
s iave platitudes of Milieu are- alike drowned in the
boom of cannon and the shouts of a continental war,
which, before' it is finished, threatens to eclipse
Chalons and Pannipr.t.
Some Australian cyclists in France recently
look part in the Paris-Nancy road race. Nancy is
the place where the French are reported to have
srfiashed up a German detachment at the beginning
of the week, and is 211 miles from Paris. Kirkham,
Munro, Snell and Bell all started. For the first
80 miles (lie pace was slow. ' Munro was the first
to go. His machine started to go to pieces, and
compelled his retirement. Snell and Bell punctured
and were left, Snell finishing 23rd. With 100 miles
to go the pace became swift and wild, and Broc-co
< the winner), Kirkham and J.wo others got away.
The others dropping out, Brocco and Kirkham
settled down to a duel. Right on the finish Kirk-
ham punctured, and Brocco rode borne an
easy winner in 11 hours 23niin. 30sec. Kirk-
ham finished fourth in llhr. 36min. However, Kirk-
ham and Munro were selected _-by the Gladiator Go.,
for whom they ride, to take part in the great Tour-
de-Franee (3340 miles) this week.
H. Haydon put up another splendid per-
formance in the three-mile amateur road-race at
Sydney .on Saturday, by bounding over the dis-
tance in 15min. 34sec. from the scratch mark. This
labor goes on the record-slate as the best extant.
Probably many a burglar with the law in pursuit
ran the distance in less time, but it hasn’t yet been
recorded. W. Sweeting (lmin. 30sec.) took the
cup attached to the event, while Haydon’s fine spin
brought him -into sixth place. Another athletic
effort worth notice was the tussle between W.
McLaren and A. Y\ r . Pickard (both on scratch) in
the three-mile road walk, and Mac. covered himself
with glory by downing the walking champion with
19sec. to spare.

The Australian Union Rugby XV was
utterly routed bv the Maorilanders at Brisbane on
Saturday. The weather was-perfect, and as a result
nearly 13,000 people rolled up to hear Freedom
shriek at their country’s downfall. Three times in
the first half the Maoriland back machine ran right
vhiough the Australian defence, but, lortunatelj,
these Maorilanders are no lackers and, not oue of
the three tries was converted. Ihe All Blacks
arrived at half-time with nine to ml. .Australia had
the wind behind it during- the second halt, but it
made no difference. Maoriland went on scoring, and
this time it did manage to convert a try, and won
dead easily bv 17 to nil.
At Auckland on Saturday tlie English
XI]V ran with its mouth open at a representative
Maoriland team, arid after a wild and strenuous
time crawled to the top of the heap with 1C points
to 13. The English were the dominating, high con-
tending party from the jump, and rattled up eight
points to nil before Maoriland broke: its egg, and
(hen an unconverted try gave it three. At half-time
England was 11 to three. During the second half
England put in most of the time strenuously defend-
ing, but the Maoriland attacks were all but one
repulsed, and one counter-attack got 'riglit home.
Ho England won as before mentioned.
When the sun climbed out of the ocean
last Saturday morning South Sydney held a pre-
carious position at the top of the Rugby League poll
with 17 points to the 16 of Newtown, and it had to
meet Eastern Suburbs, the premier team. Its row
was a hard one. But when the sun fell with y splash
into the slush beyond Pyrmont the uncertain and
always dangerous Balmain team had smashed up
Newtown bv 10 to eight, and South had trodden on
Eastern Suburbs by 10 to five, and Sopth had got a
clear margin of three in the competition race, with
19 points'to Newtown’s 16. Eastern Suburbs lags in
third place with 14, Glebe has 12, Sydney
n. Western Suburbs holds four and Apnandale one.
Eastern Suburbs never had a show with the men
from South Svdney, who, at half-time, led with
seven to nil. Balmain’s victory was as unexpected
as it was destructive. At half-time t Newtown led
with five to two, and the two was secured only on
the moment by a lucky kick. Close up to time the
score was -five all, and then a burst of excitement
took place, and the whistle blew with Balmain leading
by two. The struggles between North Svdney and
Western Suburbs and Glebe and Annandale had no
bearing on the campaign. North won an uninterest-
ing game with 13 to three, and Glebe outer! Annan-
dale by 27 to nine.
In the Union division Glebe is fit the top
of the tree with 22 competition points.to the 20 of
South Sydney, the 17 of Eastern Suburb?, and the 14
of University. In the B division. Western Suburbs
tops the list with 15, St. George has 18, and so has
North Sydney, and the baby team, Haridwick, has
12. Below that are three with single figures. Glebe
overthrew University by 16 to 12 after a strenuous
struggle which was anybody’s game right up till
time. Randwick gave Manl.v an awful astonishment,
jumping on it to the tune of 29 to five. South Syd-
ney smashed Eastern Suburbs bv 17 to 11 ; St.
George heaped infamy on Balmain by 20 to nine:
and Western Suburbs dog-walloped Newtown hv 33
to three.

bun-acker has Vie. hoofball seen a season to equal
this —gorgeous “gates,” glorious weather, .throughout

Saturday two more League teams were passed out —

Geelong came up to Carlton and set about handing

Had the Saints’ kicking been a shade surer the tale
had been different. South tackled Richmond and won
by 57 points to 43, but neither team played up to
form. South seems to have got the jumps or
hysteritis or something; Richmond is unaccountable
anyhow. Melbourne walloped the Students by 75
to 48, but the episode interested nobody.
Saturday’s game finished the first round
of the Victorian Ass. tourney, and North, ’Scrav,
’Don and ’Town, in that order, will now battle
out the issue. The only scuffles that mattered were
between ’Don and Brunswick (the Brickies lost by
81 points to ’Don’s 100), and between ’Town and
Brighton. ’Town was very gradual in starting and
at lemon-time began to see visions of disaster, but
it bogged in its darndest in the last quarter, piled on
26 points to Brighton’s nil, and so got there by 02
to 39. North kept its leadership by heaping up
130 points to Port’s 63. ’Scray annexed 76 to
R’ran’s 53, and ’Cote went under as usual, to Haw-
thorn this time, by 31 to 92. This latter, the baby
club of the Ass., retires from the contest without
glory but with some credit, having commandeered
three wins and a draw, a vastly better showing than
that of the regrettable Northcote, with one humble,
threadbare and moth-eaten victory. 'Cote ought to
tackle some deliberate recreation like quoits.
During the week tlie Leagues commis-
sioners overhauled Essendon’s captain. He had been
reported for jobbing a spectator who had jumped the
fence in a recent game and handed him (the said
captain) “one to go on with.” The commissioners
dismissed the charge with the remark that the cranky
barracker had only got what he was looking for, and
“it was a pity he hadn’t had to be carried out.
This was doubtless true, but the custom is hardly
one to be encouraged.
Aviator Guillaux had a narrow escape
from death at Ascot Racecourse (Sydney) on Satur-
day, and, incidentally, so had a few score of the
onlookers. He was something over 100 ft. high when
his engine suddenly stopped, and as the presence
of the crowd made anything like volplaning impossi-
ble, without killing a few scores, Guillaux very
bravely dived straight downward with a view tc using
tlie “tail” to check the fall. The distance was too
short to allow the “tail” to get in all its work,
but it got m sufficient to savg the aviator from
sudden death. He was passed into hospital badly
but not dangerously smashed, lie may be a giddy
ox, but he is a cool and daring bild-man.
Even as Zephuron ran away with the
G. N. Steeple at Flemington, so Capintesta bolted
with the Australian Hurdle at Caulfield —only more
so. Hot-headed and light-weighted, the pacemaker
was allowed to do his darndest, and although he
heat no time records he had from 20 to 10 lengths
the best of it for three miles and three furlongs,
lie was soon in front, and. to the horror of punters,
who reckoned that he must pump himself out, the
impetuous animal continued to flee at that distance.
’I lie fact that the consistent old sticker Taurus
(third) was “running on" at the finish argues that
(lie leader’s pace was then losing a little of its early
vigor, but his performance was remarkable all the
same. Of the two heavyweights, Obi did best in
running second. Arquebuse, who lias evidently gone
stale, cut a poor figure in the birdcage and in the
race. In a field of 11 only one horse fill, and tlie
first favorite, llarrawa, happened to be the victim.
Apart from Capintesta’s fine performance the most
striking incident of tlie busy afternoon at Caulfield
was Battleship’s easy victim- in the Steeplechasers’
Mat Race. At Moonee Valiev a week earlier the
Darrawa people were called upon to explain their
gee’s strange reversal of form, so it should have been
up to the V.A.T.C. stewards to call the Battleship
party to account. The son of Flagship, starting at
an outside price, had galloped like a donkey in a
.lumpers’ Mat Race at Moonee Valley a werk ago.
His running was conspicuously bail. On Saturday
he started a warm favorite, and won in commanding
style. Battleship, as tin's scribe suggested last week,
is considered good for a long journey over tlie big
sticks, and unless his form suffers another startling
reversal, he or Coradjil should win the big four
miles Steeplechase on Saturday.
