“The Valkyrie” writes of Fred Storbock,
who is to meet Bill Lang shortly in Sydney:
Frederick is heavyweight champion of South
Africa, an Afrikander, 6ft. tin. in his socks, scales
14st. 81b., and when in his fighting gear on tlie
voyage from Capetown he showed not one ounce of
superfluous flesh. His chest development is almost
abnormal, and though neither his arms or lcgs_ are
unusually large, he should possess “some wallop,” as
the 'Murkans say. He served his time as a black-
smith, but is fast enough on his feet to have prac-
tised as a dancing master. He started as an amateur
in 1909, beating George Rodel on points on
four occasions. He fought Lane Donaldson twice,
winning in the sixth and tenth rounds respectively.
He chased Cunningham off the face of the earth in
the 10th, I-Jartog in the fifth, and Jacobsz in the
third, and having done these deeds he stood forth
the amateur champion of S’Africa. In 1910 he was
in England, where he grabbed the British Amateur
Championship Belt. After a, final amateur campaign
in S’Africa he appeared in London as a professional,
and his first victim was Bill Chase, who was carried
home earlv bv reason of a knockout. In his fight
with Bombardier Wells the referee stopped the scrap
in the 12th, as Storbeck had both eyes closed and
was finite unable to see. He fought Australia’s old
friend, “Nuttv” Curran, three times, and got points
decisions over Jim Johnson and Bob Scanlon. He
tackled Jewev Smith at Wonderland, and the up-
heaval resulted in a 10-round draw. Moran got a
decision against him on a foul, and the spectators
bowled that the decision was unjust. A trip to the
States was brought to nought by a beat wave, and
he went back to South Africa to meet Mike Williams ;
tut falling ill lie went to Rhodesia and led the
simple life killing lions and elephants, and tearing
up crocodiles, and throwing pythons away. On Box-
ing Day. 1913. lie crashed into Mike at the Wan-
derers, but was disqualified in the second round.
Another meeting was arranged, and after lniin. 7see.
the proceedings interested Mike no more. Some
people accused the loser of lying down, but it
is prettv evident that he was pushed. After that, he
was only onee in the ring, when he met Jack Lalor,
the idol of the Rand. The Idol got the decision.
Storbeck was one of the stalwarts who lined up
round the Johannesburg Trades Hall to protect the
Labor leaders what time the police and military from
the veldt trained Maxims on the forlorn hope of .
Great Strike. When lie is through with his Aus-
tralian trip he will hie him unto the Yewnited
States.
Though Half-Round Smith was outed in
Sydney Stadium by Eddie McGoorty it took quite a
crowd of wallops to put him definitely down and
out. When he got into the ring with Jimmy Clabby
on Saturday night he made a very fine start. 1 hen
flabby hit him twice, and in lmin. 20sec. from
the sounding of the gong he was being assisted to
his corner. Rage, disappointment, derision and
contempt sent forth a storm of hoots that literally
blew David Smith out of the big building, and, as
many thought, out of the boxing game. It was in
a clinch, and Smith neglected to protect himself.
He failed to hold Clabby, who, having both hands
free, very properly used them, and a neat uppercut
and a good left hook ended the battle in a moment.
The truth is that, since the disaster he endured at
the fists of McGoorty, Smith is no longer a fighter.
Writer has seen lots of examples of how one licking
will cow an erstwhile very stormy warrior. In the
timber districts of Hawkes Bay, Maoriland, there is
alwavs a little colony of men who, on pay week, keep
apart from the mass and drink quietly by themselves
and without violence. Writer once tried to hold up such
a party as an example to a drunken figfiting crowd,
and learned that the parties held up were the fellows
who had been hammered into a peaceable frame of
mind. Also, the writer knows that he himself has
not been the same war-like spirit he was before
“Totara Jack” smote him with a dead bull-pup at
the end of a chain. But in spite of this obvious fact
it is said that Reg Baker is going to give Half-
llound Smith another chance against Clabby at Bris-
bane. Reg Baker is a long-suffering Christian.
“Bilrock,” from Paris: How long, ye
prophets (and losses), must a much-harassed public
be compelled to suffer the bloated arrogance of .1.
Johnson, Esq., champ, pug. and world's star buffoon?
If Yankee Moran is the best Pale Hope of the day,
then the cullud brndder need harbor no fear oi
losing his title, for Moran’s display at the Velodrome
D’Hiver on Saturday night last was that of a mere
tyro. The collision on the eve of the Grand Prix
drew some 10,000 sports, females and bloques to the
big saucer-track stadium, where the ring, under the
powerful einema. lights, looked like a sunspot on a
timbered waste. The crowd comprised the most re-
markable gathering that has attended a mill on this
side of the world for many a year. Pearls and dia-
monds sparkled on hundreds of bare necks. More
than half the ringside seats were occupied by women.
In addition to a baroness and a duchess, amongst the
bunch of titled dames were two princesses whose
titles can stand inspection, the diadem being blown
in the bottle. A duke and at least one earl- were
with the crowd of English snorts that crossed the
silver streak with the hope of seeing Johnson scat-
tered. At this time of the year Paris is half-full
of Americans, and there was a good deal of “guess-
ing” in nasal tones. Admission prices ranged from
seven to 250 francs, and it is said that Johnson will
receive between £5OOO and £6OOO as his share of
the brigandage. That Paris has tired of its ebony
idol was evidenced by the somewhat feeble applause
that greeted the Shady One and the storm of cheers
with Which the house hailed Moran. Followed by an
army of satellites that looked like one of the lost
tribes of Israel, Johnson entered the ring a picture
of inky arrogance. The Caretaker of the Diamond
Collar Stud removed a towel from his head. The
First Admiral of the Wardrobe relieved him of his
long dressing-gown of grey and black silk decked
with a white lace collar. Then all Africa stood
resplendent in blue plush knickers trimmed with
gold, and with the evidences of his laziness plainly
visible in the rolls of superfluous fat over his ribs.
For a space of five minutes the dusky Nabob gave an
exhibition of his histrionic powers that he has not
hitherto equalled. He fairly wallowed in the lime-
light. He preened before the public. He posed , tor
the cinema. He passed the glad grin over to : his
new white girl-wife sitting semi-clad in silk in the
front row. He smirked at the haute monde that
filled the £l2 10s. seats. He joshed the officials.
He was the High Panjandrum of Swank. In com-
parison with his Darkness, the Czar of all the
Russias would have figured as a grease spot. He
was supreme. He was It.
The ruddv Moran showed up pale by contrast, the
pallor of his skin accentuated by the fierce blaze
from the powerful white lights above the ring.
Moran is a splay-footed, bull-necked boxer with the
dimensions of aii elephant and the movement of a
snail. Alongside the nigger the Yankee’s efforts
seemed puerile. He did not even justify a claim to
the right to fight for the championship. Stiff and
awkward in his movements, he -advertised in the
plainest manner what he was about to do. Moran is
a boxer with a punch, but without the skill to use
it. Crafty as ever, Brud. John sing completely out-
generalled him from one end of the contest to the
other. Immaculate in flannels, Georges Carpentier,
who is himself looking Johnsonwards, refereed the
mix-up for the record fee of 10,000 francs.
The fight was disappointing. Only thrice in the
mill did Moran jar the dark streak. The last_ of
those accidents was a straight right to the jaw
which must have tasted like a bunt from a railway
locomotive. It brought a roar of approval from the
crowd, and a smile from Johnson, who stepped back
and joined in the applause by clapping his 4oz.
gloves. Twice in clinches Johnson resorted to an
old by-plav, standing perfectly still and allowing
Moran to pound his cast-iron stomach, while ho
flashed the golden grimace at the crowd. After the
tenth round, when he had the points well in hand,
Johnson toiled not neither did he punch. From this
stage onwards the scrap would have gladdened the
members of a Society for the Promotion of Peace.
One marvelled at the patience of the French citizens
in the galleries, who had parted with so many francs
to witness the fiasco. Time and again the alleged
“hope” came in head down, arms whirling like the
blades of a propeller, wide open to the knock-out,
and the Inky One merely toyed with him. He re-
ceived him as one receives an aged and wealthv
relative. He took him to his black bosom, patted
him affectionately on the back, and wiped the sweat
of his dusky brow on the white man’s shoulder.
Towards the finish the crowd went baresark. It
clamored in idiomatic French for the knock-out. It
reared itself upon its hind legs, and called the nig.
27 different kinds -of a loafer. But it moved him
not. The fiaht -ended in an uproar. Bv permission
of Mistah Johnsing, Moran had stayed the 20 rounds.

RITA MOYA discourses on
NATURAL BEAUTY,
and how to obtain it.
To-day every woman of taste and refinement avoids the use of cosmetics or made-up
toilet preparations which give an artificial appearance. The fresh, natural complexion that
few women have, and every woman desires, is best obtained by use of original ingredients
already at hand or which can be obtained from any reliable chemist. My advice is to avoid
the use of most made-up face creams, rouge and beautifying preparations. They are usually
obvious and often disappointing. If you will get only the proper original ingredients you will
be satisfied with the result. Insist on having what you ask for. If the chemist does not
happen to have it in stock, he can immediately get it from his wholesaler.
Good Shampoo Important.- —The hair should
he allowed to breathe, and the greasy film
around each strand must he removed with a
mild non alkaline shampoo. Soaps should he ]

The Shark Turns.
“Gordon G.” applies for a stay of proceed-
ings :
This never-ending gufT against law and lawyers
makes me weary and angry all in one. I doubt
whether a search through ail the English literature,
from the time of old Bede to the present, would
reveal one writer who has had a good word for me
and my learned friend or the system we toil under.
They’re all the same—from Siinksi’kare and Dickens
to the brightest constellations on (he Melbourne
’Erai.ii. Whenever a writer is hard. up for copy, it
seems to be his practice to scratch his scalp and
produce a long-winded spasm on “Costly Litigation ’
or “The Law's Delays.” As a consequence, the aver-
age individual seems to regard the six-an’-eight pro-
fession and every body and thing connected with it.
as in the nature of a bad smell to lie carefully
avoided and harshly spoken of.
In the first place, Gent. One is abused as a
successful licensed plunderer with a tremendous
capacity for easily amassing the hard-earned wealth
of others. That is perhaps the chief grievance. For
this reason he is called “hog,” “shark” and other
predatory animals as hard and as often as possible.
But, as a matter of fact, Victorian statistics (which
are less or more reliable") show that “barrister and
solicitor” is eight lines down in a group of 12 tax-
payers, set out in a recent official income-tax list.
In the face of that, Gent. One can’t be called a.
well-fed shark, anyhow. Another fact worth noting
is that lawyers, although they have their own indus-
trial union, are the only people now who are work-
ing under Wages Board conditions which fix a maxi-
mum and not a minimum wage. And whenever a
scale of costs is altered by custom or competition
(there are “scabs” in this as in other professions),
or by legislative Act or judicial direction, it is
invariably reduced. Compare this with recent
changes in the charges of every other profession,
business, trade, occupation, job, game, pastime or
boil-over.
Certainly, neither barristers nor solicitors’ fees
have gone up in proportion to the increased cost of
living. On the contrary, they have gone • down —-
and, in many instances, with a wallop. Also, the
solicitor is, for some unaccountable reason, humili-
ated by being compelled to render signed detailed bills
of costs, with every trifling item specified and the exact
amount charged therefore set down against it. Fur-
ther, he is not allowed to sue for his account until
one month after it. has been delivered. No such
lestriction is placed on dentists or doctors or any-
body else. Even lawyers’ clerks, who, in the mass,
do the most responsible and important work in the
whole clerking world, have no semblance of a Wages
Board, and are, for the most part, underpaid by
bosses whose good old days of big bills are over and
done with. So much for the sordid monetary aspect.
Then, again, the same batch of writers, with the
same brilliant originality, make The Law synony-
mous with The Ass. The system whereby Gent. One
earns his humble crust undoubtedly does possess cer-
tain asinine qualities (for instance, a penchant for
moving leisurely), but, considering it has been the
carefully-evolved work of centuries on the part of
the most eminent judicial and other lights interested,
it is a trifle over the odds to decry the whole ami
every portion of it in a series of vague, sweeping
generalities. Admittedly, reform in law is needed.
But so is reform in nearly everything and in riianv
places more urgently.
Once more, the lawyer is credited with all the
cunning and roguery and chicanery usually attri-
buted to the Devi n. Vet, incongruously enough,
more men will be drawn from the ranks of lawvefs
to represent, the people in the next Federal I’arlnP
meiil; than from any other class of the community.
And a member of Barliament is said to he in a posh
t ion of t rust.
The truth about it is that the lawyer is tinder the
disadvantage of being one of the few whose work is
constantly and publicly reviewed and checked. Nearly
cierr little mistake or backslide is eagerly pounced on
by an opposing lawyer and triumphantly pushed in
front of a Judge. Further limelight effects are left to
the reporter and the leader-writer, and they do their
work with the ardor and thoroughness of men who
are capable of better tilings.
♦
The mourning of “0.K.” :
The need for closer settlement, and further agri-
cultural development in Victoria, has found new
expression in the cry of the Bendigo unemployed. It
ixn t Melbourne only that suffers from congestion of
the < ity. Bendigo, the second most crowded spot
in the State, has also got the complaint pretty
badly, and {migration offers no immediate cure for
that. Immigration under the present Vic. system, or
want of system, spells more congestion, for it is
only the boy portion of the human goods from ’Ome
that can be easily placed on the land to stay there.
One good effect of the Bendigo unemployed evil, how-
ever. is the Government’s promise to stir the Country
Hoads Board into action. Three or four hundred
men will be put to roadmaking, and a reasonable
country road does at least suggest a way of escape
to the townsmen employed in the making. Besides,
it ought to lie the most productive tract of land in
the district. The policy of waiting for casual settle-
ment to lead to the cutting of roads has given
Victoria the spotty appearance of a workhouse pud-
ding. wherein one large raisin and a few currants
break the dull monotony of suet. If the Board has
a notion of making roads that will lead to settle-
ment, every man on the job will produce .">0 times
the cost of bis wages. A path through the wilder-
ness for civilisation to travel is a more payable
proposition than a tunnel in a Bendigo mine.
4
“Fitzwilliam” : Will some earnest student
cl. economics please explain how the Work-
man is aided by the Hot.man brand of Labor
policy in this instance? Down in Kisuna
(N.K.W.). where the blue-metal comes from,
the quarries were run for some 2o years
by Private Enterprise, the metal being carted
to the railway and wharves in drays. This
gave employment to a large number of
drivers. A couple of years hack the Govern-
ment resumed the quarries. Instead of
drays it laid down a tramway. No fault
to lie found with that; hilt the two locomo-
tives were imported from the United States,
the trucks were imported front Germany, and
a new steamer to cope with tin* increased
output of metal was imported from Holland.
(The steamer sank off the W.A. coast, but
that didn't worry the Labor Government—
it ordered another.) I haven’t been able
to discover where the rails were imported
from —Japan, likely. The money that paid
for everything was imported from England.
As an added joy, the German trucks were
found to he too heavy for the United States
locomotives. New trucks were, of course,
procured locally—T don't think. They came
from Germany. T suppose it must' he all
right, else a Labor Government wouldn’t
have done it. hut I expect I’m too hopelessly
ignorant to see how the unemployed drivers
benefit, not to speak of the other Australian
workmen who didn’t make the tramway
plant.

“A.Jt..” concerning an importation:—-
I*. B. Clapp. Chairman of the Melbourne Cable
Tramways Trust, is a remarkable man. lie was
brought from the t .S. for this position when the
cable trams first started, and, though nearly blind,
is considered worth liis £3OOO per annum. llis
wife or one of his daughters leads him to and from
the Trust's office in Collins-stroot every day. The
sons have been trained in electrical schools in the
l .S.. and one of them has been commissioned to come
to Melbourne to advise the Trust regarding the
electrification of flic cable trams. Another son is in
Broken Hill. Four daughters have for years run a
dress-designing establishment in Flinders-lane. It, is
still carried on by the elder daughters, the two
younger having lately married.

I Saw My Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a subtle relation between mood and color.
. — Chatter.
I saw my love in pink arrayed,
And burned to clasp her to me-;
And every graceful turn she made
With magic potence drew me.
Again she passed in pearly grey,
To suit the varying fashion :
And though she seemed one half a fay
She raised no throbbing passion.
I saw her next in azure clad ;
No goddess swept diviner.
Unconsciously my heart grew glad,
Attuned to issues finer.
In violet silk anil Veiled niiion,
Sin- c-irtscyed in the Tango;
My heart-strings then were played upon
As gaily as a banjo.
But when she swept in old brocade
Attired by hands Parisian.
A flower >he seemed that could not fade, ■
A bloom of lands Elysian.
I wonder would it calm the mind
If Delia's garb were lmeless?
(tods, make her victim color-blind
Or make her beauty viewless!

PHILANDER. F.
“Curds and Woe” : The Australian cow
cocky is up against what is known as the
margarine menace. Butter easily fought Un-
original margarine, which was a crude com-
pound of animal fats. But to-day, in Lon-
don, a palatable and presentable product
from copra and other vegetable fats is being-
retailed as prime margarine at 6d. per
lb. To compete with this best butter
must sell at Is. per lb. in London, and it
hasn’t done that sort, of thing for a long
while; also the people concerned don't want
it to make the attempt. If Australia’s sur-
plus finished-efl'ort-of-the-cow has to be sold
in the best foreign market at Is., then the
price must dron locally, and the bull’s niece
may give up all hope of a new spring bonnet
to put on her horns, and a red ribbon to th-
ou her tail when it is her birthday. Of
course the cow will come again, even if she
suiters temporary eclipse, because the sources
of the new margarine supply are limited —
that is to say, she will come again unless
someone discovers a way of making edible
grease out of icebergs or mineral tailings, or
something hitherto unsuspected. But it looks
as if, for a few years, she might have to gird
up her caudal extremity and do an uphill
fight for her crust.
«.
A Melbourne woman lately evolved an
effective plan for getting rid of a superfluous
infant. She took it to a crbche, paid Is.,
and left it there. It is still there.
“Junior Bar”: The Bulletin mentions
(18/(5/T4) the “strange but usual judicial
assumption that a judge doesn’t know any-
thing beyond what counsel tell him.” That
is so. Called to the Bar in London, I some-
how got the idea that a judge knows all the
law there is, and would sweep aside the
spurious arguments of the Bar with a mas-
terly offhand statement of the correct article.
I shall never forget the first application I
made to an Australian judge—to allow or
disallow something. “But have I the power
to do this?” he asked me earnestly. “It
seems a deserving case, on the facts. But
can you show me that I have the power?”
“Well, d—n it all, your Honor,” I replied in
a firm tone, “I am getting a guinea for this
application, and it is probably what I’m
worth. You get £2OOO a year or thereabouts
for sitting there and hearing this sort of
tiling. Don’t you know whether you have
the power or not?” As I found afterwards
that nobody had heard this spirited outburst,
it is probable that I had only been thinking-
hard. But, anyway, it does seem that Aus-
tralian judges have more respect for the Bar
than is quite healthy for either side. What
a poor figure the Bench cuts, generally, when
there is any altercation between them! It
is difficult to imagine “one of ours” charging
a jury as I have heard the Lord Chief Justice
in London: “Gentlemen, the learned counsel
on either side have addressed you as though
the Constitution of England were at stake.
But, gentlemen, the Constitution of England
is not at stake. It appears to me a very
ordinary case for small damages”—and in
three minutes lie had straightened out the
legal tangle which had been getting worse
and worse in the previous three hours.
“D.” : If pugilism develops into a sort of
world-wide religion, as seems possible in view
of the fact that France and England are
lialf-inad about it. while neither America
nor Australia can be said to be absolute]v
sane on the question (and a measure of in-
sanity is notoriously the basis of all live
faiths) —in this event, I say, Hugh I).
Mclntosh, of Sydney, will have first claim on
the billet of Prophet. When Mclntosh pro-
jected himself into the fight business, half a
dozen years ago. pugilism was a furtive, dis-
reputable art carried on in dens by villainous-
looking principals, and in the presence,
chiefly, of tin- criminal classes. The National
Sporting Club, in Castlereagh-street, had
stood for the only attempt, in years, to make
stoush more or less respectable. It failed
utterly. Then out of the murk stepped
Mclntosh and showed the way to higher
things. . The Burns-Johnson contest was a
poor thing regarded as a sporting event, but
as a social function it was unimpeachable
From that day to this, in Australia, not only
have the crowds at boxing matches swollen
to a size that was unimaginable a few years
back, but they number some of the 'most
reputable people in the community. In Eng-
land people like ex-Prime Ministers attend
fights with their families. In France a box-
ing contest of importance is said to rival a
first night at the Opera House as regards
the costumes of the fashionable people who
sit in the front seats. And the genesis of it
all was in the brain of a small, resolute N.
S. Wales citizen whose chief financial asset
at the time was credit, and its Eden was a
Chinese garden in Rushcutter’s Bay. From
such paltry beginnings do the world’s great-
est superstitions invariable spring.

IN
BANANA LAND
“Cleveland”: Let me into this argument
about scooping harbors out of rivers, or bays
with rivers discharging into them. Queens-
land has a lot of these silt traps, which are
usually held up as shining examples of the
power of money. The following figures are
taken from the annual report of the Rock-
hampton Harbor Board for 1913. They
cover a period of 10 years, and apply to the
attempt to gouge out the Fitzroy River to,
enable ships to reach the town wharves: —
Average amount dredged and deposited,
per year . . . . . . . . 859,910 tops.
Average cost per ton, including in-
terest and depreciation .. .. 3.53, pence.
Average amount spent per year on
dredging . . .... .. £12,648
The average number of ships that used this
majestic wmterway was 361.3 per year.
Most of the shipping was of the
mosquito variety, averaging only 449 tons.
It would be rational to suppose that, after
experimenting for 16 years and spending
£202,365 for dredging alone, there would be
a marked improvement in the gutter; but
the number of ships that negotiated the.
streak during 1913 was only 238, which is
considerably less than the average for the 16.
years, viz., 361. During the whole period;
only 13 vessels of between 18 and 20 feet
draft went up the river, and none at all
since 1907! “Rockie” is singularly
blessed with so-called ports, having also got
Broadmount and Port Alma; and not one
of them is an honest, straightforward pro-
position. I would not have said a word only
that, while all this wild waste of public
money has been going on, the neighboring
Gladstone has been sleeping on an utterly
neglected natural harbor that could take the
whole of “Rockie’s” 16 years’ dredgings,
shipping, Harbor Boards and debt, and hide
them from the scandalised eyes of honest
men. And there would still be ample room
for the British fleet, an odd coaster and an
occasional barramundi.
The luck of some railway trains ! The
other day a tender ran for 19 miles into
Ipswich with a broken axle, from which an
18-inch section was missing. It waltzed
alopg Soppily and damaged, neither goods
nor passengers. A trifle of this sort might
easily have cost a less lucky Department
£20,000 in compensation.
Brisbane Courier! funeral notice (July
27)
The friends of Mr. Ji. Christopher Columbus are
respectfully invited to, attend his funeral, to move
from his late residence, Petrie Ter,race, this after-
noon at 4 o’clock, to the Toowong Cemetery.
Mark Twain’s Innocent Abroad, who para-
lysed the Italian guide by asking, in refer-
ence to thp statue of the great Genoese
navigator, “Is he dead?” ought to be quite
satisfied now.
Staunch old Queensland navigator Cap-
tain W. Collin, who died some few months
ago, has had, his life’s story put into !a book
by his, sop. Collin knew aU the vicissitudes
of a sailor’s life: —mutiny, shipwreck,, starva-
tion and arrest. He was on board the
Grimeneza, whep that yellow hulk wept down
with 600 cooli.es on the Bampton Reef.
Ope patch of his experiences, while drifting
after the wreck :
On the fifth morning before daylight a Bengalee
boy, one of the crew, rushed into my arms as I sat
in the stern of the boat, declaring that the men were
about to kill and eat him. I declared they should
not do him any harm; but there was no telling how
long I might be able to . protect him.
He hints that such grim experiences were
commqn epough in those days. “Jolly tars
were our men,” but their playful habits
weren’t always relished by their prospec-
tive victuals.
“-P.F.” : Queensland's way of treating its
neuropaths is more insane than Bedlam
itself. Some months ago a half-demented
female burnt down the Salv’army’s home at
Taringa, under a blind delusion that she was
wreaking vengeance on someone. Two
deaths resulted from that horror. No con-
viction was recorded against the incendiary,
as her mental condition was only too evi-
dent. Since then she has made more than
one appearance before the police court, and
distinguished herself recently by smashing
the plateglass front of a drapery store*
What blazing folly to allow a person of this
kind to ramble at large! Arson, man-
slaughter, wilful damage to property are, it
seems, insufficient to justify the deprivation
of a lunatic’s liberty, while the asylum at
Goodna harbors scores of people whose only
crime against society is that they imagine
the, moon is a pork-pie, or Joe Cook the
Great Mogul. Is it Queensland’s belief that
every crazy person should be. allowed one
case of arson, just as a dog is allowed to
hfve his one bite without incurring legal
penalties?
N.S.W. Justice Pring, sitting as a Com-
missioner on the Boorabil land-resumption
business, got square on to the jaw of the
men who mix the jobs of land agent and
M.P. “What would you do,” asked Pring,
“if a deal in which you had acted as agent
for the vendor came up in Parliament for
approval?” “I wouldn’t vote, of course,”
said the virtuous agent M.P., and thought
that ended it. But it didn’t. “Exactly!’
remarked Pring. “You would sacrifice your
public duty to your private interests.”
“Praeco,” regarding a new baronet: —
A most interesting little personage is Sir Robert
Nivison, the mighty atom who underwrites the
public loans of India, Canada and Australasia. In-
cidentally, he underwrites the Metropolitan Board of
Works (Melb.) loans, though that is not generally
understood'. Most of the big corporation loans
floated by the capital cities of Australia are nominally
underwritten by local brokers, but in reality by
Nivison, for whom they merely act as agents on
fractional commission. The public frequently won-
ders how Mr. So-and-So, of the Melb. Stock Ex-
change,. or Mr. This-and-That, the Sydney share-
broker, can offer to find half a million so easily
when it is all poor So-and-So or This-and-That can
do to pay the rent of two small dingy rooms. Most
of the big debenture issues of Melbourne and Sydney
lie snugly in the vaults of the London and West-
minster Bank, where “Bobby” Nivison was once a
bill-clerk, getting £l5O a year at 30 years of age. He
& nicknamed “the Canary,” because the feathers
on his face are yellow, his legs are thin, and he
sings ballads. It is not known whether he moults
occasionally, and is given duckweed and mutton-
fat. Nivison is very like the late W. A. Zeal, Presi-
dent of the Vie. Leg. Council, except that he is
not snappy. On the contrary, he is a suave, bland'
little chap from Aberdeen, whose self-admitted
habits, according to Who’s Who, are giving good*
dinners and' collecting big commissions. And nobody
ever makes the mistake of thinking Nivison is a 1
glorified bank clerk. He is easily the most astute'
man amongst the 4000 London stockbrokers.
From the “Old Soldier” :
The murder of Jaures, the millionaire leader of f
the extreme Radical group in the French Chamber,,
may affect the European situation. Says the cable:'
“The murderer belongs to a royalist organisation,
and' is the soft of a law-court official at Rheims'. He
declares that' he had shot M. JaurOs because of the'
latter’s attempts to maintain international peace.”
The organisation in question is the old “Camelots*
du Roi,” who, for more than 40 years, have been 1
trying, by murder, conspiracy, etc., to restore the
Bourbons. The Boulanger and Dreyfus scandals 4
were their achievements, and now they seem ready
to, disunite France, merely to give the Orleans gang
and the Bonapartes a chance of pushing their rival’
claims. The kingly possibilities are the Duke of
Orleans and Victor Bonaparte, son of “Plon-Plon,”’
known in soci-iy as Prince Napoleon. He used to-
have a commission in the Russian army, but, since'
Russia lias become France’s ally, be has been dis-
pensed with for obvious reasons. So he has settled!
down in Brussels as the husband of the late King
Leopold’s managing daughter, Clementine, who is-
lavishing her father’s money on an attempt to get 1
her husband on to a French Imperial' throne.
Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Like the swift beat of rain along the Quay
Come the doomed feet of market-driven
sheep,
Under the figs that gloom the river’s edge
Some night thing stirs and shivers at the
dawm,
For, after all, the wine-flushed sleep was
best
And the drugged grief that slumbered in
the brain
Grows clear beneath the gold search-light of
day,
Unkind to that confused dishevelment.
Sparrows, alert, take plunges in their bath
Of dew or dust, across the grass-fringed
walk.
A lily lifts from by the paling fence
The scalloped goblet of its sensuous red,
And one moonflow'er, where all the rest are
blind.
Will not forget the naked lady, who
The night long kept her Queenshlp of the
sky,
But through the ashen leaves, still wude-
eyed, yearns
After the pale abandon of her heams.
Though Day makes empty Love’s regretful
arms
And Night’s dear nearness is prim-cloaked
again.
Beneath the kerchief decorously spread
Sweet subtle achings swell the hidden breast
Where his warm kisses, soft as petals fell!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 1, 1914.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
This has not been a particularly gay week,
although the squattocracy are flocking into
town, so as to get in a bow or bend or a
scrape and forelock tug when the Governor-
General’s august presence overshadows the
lilliputian landscape and stills the war-
throb in the air. All human accommodation
in the hotels is booked up. Humble ani-
mals, however, are still being catered for in
the stables, kennels and bars. The which
is all so much wasted energy. Australia’s
most expensive Governor has been hurriedly
dragged from Sydney (his half-way house
to Brisbane) back to Melbourne, where he
will open foreign despatches and hand them
for Consideration to the collective intelli-
gence, wisdom, discernment, craft and dip-
lomacy of the Cabinet. Yet the crowds
assembling at Brisbane will not be disap-
THE ELEVATOR ,
HE: “Ours wouldn't be an, unequal match. Married to me you would rise to, my position."
pointed altogether. The annual Sho\v; is
some sort of recompense.
Leslie Harris, one of oui; prettiest girls,
has contracted herself matrimonially to one
Bullivant, a Victorian squatter, and is
gathering in her trousseau for the wedding,
which will take place towards the end of
the year.
The naval and military ball last night was
a mighty and uplifting success. The South
Brisbane City Hall was made beautiful with
palms and blue and white drapings, also by
the presence of the Lieutenant-Governor
and his nice, cheerful wife, Inspector-Gen-
eral Briggs, Colonel and Mrs. Lee, Colonel
and Mrs. Bruche and Colonel Sutton. The
tout ensemble (which is no relation to race-
horse touts, my dear) was pleasingly effec-
tive. The men outnumbered the girls by
just about three, so that there were no
wallflowers. :
Miss Monje, of your State, was among the
dancers. She wore a becoming black and;
tan confection, glittering with diamonds.
Among the representatives of local femininity
were Mrs. Espie Dods (in black), Gladys
Pollard (in green), and Lottie Bond (in
peacock blue). Mrs. White, of Pikedale,
chaperoned two handsome daughters.
Mrs. Packer, a member of the Wimmen’s
Patriotic Club, Sydney, is visiting here, and
will stay until after the marriage of Miss
Constance Bloom and Mr. Spiegel.
Mrs. John Hicks gave a cheerful tea party
on Wednesday, in honor of Miss McCut-
cheon, of Melbourne. About 30 celebrants
assembled and were given rations in the con-
servatory. Daughter Gladys kindly assisted;
her mother in the work of distribution. She,
by the way, recently acquired the diamond
half-hoop of impending matrimony from a
northern banking man.
Mischa Elman and Eva Gauthier arrived
here on Wednesday night. They gave their
first concert on Thursday, when Mischa,
looking oddly gnome-like on the huge Exhibi-
tion platform, and in the bad light (it was
mostly shadow where Mischn was), plucked
the quivering soul out of his violin and
offered it (the q.s.) to the audience at the
advertised price.
Eva, wearing the greater portion of some
black-plumed bird in her head-vegetation,
warbled musically. Feminine Brisbane had
had hopes of seeing the lady in a purple wig,
and was disappointed, but it couldn’t get its
money back at the doors. However, it took
some comfort in committing to memory the
lines of her old gold frock.
Antonia Dolores arrived back from the
North on Thursday, and left for the Downs
on the following day. She will return next .
Friday, and will give two concerts in Exhi-
bition week.
The amateur golf championship of :
Queensland was played off this week and
was won by E. Fitzpatrick. He was the
winner in 1011.
The shortsightedness of the Government
in appointing the German Consul to a seat
in the Legislative Council is as nothing to
the mole-like blindness of whoever was re-
sponsible for putting the. same alert gentle-
man into a chair of directorship at the
Queensland National Bank. I’m expecting
any minute to hear that Prime Minister
Asquith has cabled produce-storeman Den-
ham for the whyfor of the joke which enables
the official representative of an ever-menac-
ing foreign Power to help run an Australian
State.
Just before the battle, Thargo, while the
dove of peace in vain beats against war’s
cruel embargo, I remain,
Australia

It’s many years ago they told young John to get his
gun;
No doubt expecting that the lad would like to see
the fun.
And then they went away, asserting: “After all, it’s
wrong;
There’ll be no war; fair Peace will reign from
Belgrade round to Bhong.’’
But John had cleared like Holy Smoke —’twas just
as well he had;
He brought his rifle back with him, which made them
very sad;
They said: “You are too hasty, John; you see, we
quite forgot
We meant to hold a meet of prayer upon this very
spot!”
Just then Hans threw a brick at John, who promptly
turned and fired,
And thereupon the row began—and they at once
expired!

A SENSE of satisfaction is experienced
every time one wears a pair of Bos-
tock boots, and. from this consciousness
of their fine quality springs a natural agd
legitimate pride. One feels that they wilhj
contribute to ease" and elegance on the most
exacting occasions, and one knows that their

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Colin Clout: Your proposal that every
worker of every sort be compelled by Act of Parlia-
ment to be a unionist is duly noted. But, after all,
the great point about a unionist is that he proves, in
a general way, a better worker and a better citizen
than a non-unionist. And if the non-unionist is
abolished, then the unionist can’t be better. As soon
as everybody is roped in, then everybody is on the
same level, and is just a human being. What is the
good of an Act to compel each human being to be a
human being? Unionism, like many other move-
ments, will probably die of complete success. Prefer-
ence to unionists depends on the survival of the man
who stands out. With no man standing out, it would
be preference to all people—which would be prefer-
ence to no person. Some day the unions may be
reduced to paying a few people to be hostile....
John W.: “Casindra” is the thinnest possible skeleton
of a story. The only sign of life or fluff or meat
about it is the entrance of a pet animal, which may
be a cat or a tame gorilla or almost anything. , Of
course, it isn’t an eel. No pet eel ever walks in....
T.S.H.: It is a musical fragment, but it is only
music. A quarter-century ago a humorist published
a picture of a mild and weeping curate discoursing
soft verses to a Dorcas meeting. And when he read:
Sweet lady mine who —ah —glitterest
In shimmah of summah athwart the lawn,
Canst tell me which —er —is bitterest —
The glimmah of eve or the glamali of dawn,
the Dorcas meeting wept also. There is something of
that same soft nothingness about .your verses. Yet
you can write. Go out and kill a bullock and pen a
virile howl about Blood. Go and kill a money-lender
and turn his corpse into verse. Slay a solicitor.
Throw a cabman into the harbor. In fact, get a real
live subject. .. .327th Duke of Derrimut: The differ-
ence between an allodile and a crocogator has been
related till it becomes a weariness.... Harold J.
Brown: The Red Page never cursed those novels of
Harrison; the curse came from the U.S., and the
Red Page merely quoted it, mentioning its original
roosting place. Opinion of “V.V.’s Eyes” was.
printed on August 14, 1913. .. ..June T: No prize
was offered by Red Page for the “Australienne’s.
Creed.” All that was promised was the publication
of the best creed submitted. Five of the best were
printed, and, as usual, space rates will be paid to
the authors on application. .. .M.D. II.: Not bad; but
we don’t care for the allegorical form. And, apropos
your covering letter, remember, “Easy writing makes
hard reading.”. ..-A.8.: Written, apparently, for
the girl's Album of Stodge, and suitable for the pur-
pose, .. .Vamper: Gush. We prefer grit.... Stewart
Gee: All rather commonplace. .. .Revka.v : Ingenious.
But somehow it recalls the ingenuity of a man in
Adelaide who fixed up a most skilful device for
keeping alligators out of his fowlyard. .. .Isa: Lamb
without mint sauce. .. .Jobber: Don’t remember your
letter.... Trapper: You’re a good starter, but you
fail at the post.... Gladys M. P.: Smooth little
trickle, which ran over the rocks without a murmur.
Tyrone: Smartly written. Pity there wasn’t.
more to write about.... Quilibet: Lines from your
sonnet on your sister's birthday:—
And when, fair maid, abroad thou tak’st thy ways
The. stricken swains in wonder stand to gaze,
The little feathered choir forget their song,
The traffic standeth still in dumb amaze.
Why, what's wrong with her? Skirt split more than
usual, or what?....G.: Received, one epic, in 220
lines of verse. We might have printed it only you
forgot to give it a title, and if we chanced it and

there would be.... Sydney S.: Not quite the sort of

keep well. ...W.J.11.: The hut-door with the message
on it, and the squatter who couldn’t read and rode

refuse to support another par, let alone a ballad....
J.W.E.: “Midnight Scare” falls flat.... Stephanie:
“Progress” may be a very good “morality.”. But it
is outside the limits of our space, and we don’t print
plays, anyhow tkT.B.: Cheerful doggerel, which
would have dodged the basket if it had.
been a bit better brushed and combed....

THE WILDS OP MAORILAND.
By JAMES MACKINTOSH BELL,
M.A., Ph.L., with 8 plates in color,
many other illustrations in black and
white, and several maps. Demy Svo.
(155.)
**,The author of this work was for six years
Director of New Zealand’s Geological Survey,
and during this time of almost constant travel
his work and the love of exploration led him
into many little visited corners of this wonder-
ful country. The volume concludes with a
description of the geography and climate of
New Zealand.

Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 25th July, 1914.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVY.
Tenders for East India Teak.
TIIENDERS will be received, subject to
JL the conditions of tendering, until 12
noon on Monday, 17th August, 1914, for the
supply of East India Teak. The material is
to be delivered F. 0.8. at Commonwealth
Dockyard, Cockatoo Island, Sydney. Tender
forms and specifications can be obtained
from the Director of Navy Contracts, Navy
Office, Melbourne ; or from the District Naval
Officer, Naval Staff Office, Sydney, Bris-
bane, Largs Bay (S.A.), Fremantle or
Hobart.
Tenders, which are to be in all cases on
the printed form, should be endorsed
“TENDER FOR TEAK” on top left-hand
corner of the envelope, and addressed (if by
post prepaid) to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; or to the
Director of Navy Contracts, care Naval
Staff Office. Sydney, Brisbane, Largs Bay
(S.A.), Fremantle or Hobart.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
E. D. MILLEN,
Minister of State for Defence.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 25th July, 1914.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVY.
Examination for Appointment as Pay-
master’s Clerk (Sea-going).
A COMPETITIVE examination fox-
appointment as Paymaster’s Clerk
(Sea-going) in the Royal Australian Navy
will be held in the capital cities of the Com-
monwealth towards the end of 1914.
The syllabus and standard of examination
will be the same as for examination as Clerk
in the Commonwealth Public Service.
Candidates must attain the age of 18 years
in 1914.
Forms of application and further parti-
culars may be obtained from the District
Naval Officer, Brisbane. Sydney, Largs Bay
(S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart; and from the
Naval Secretary, Navy Office, Melbourne,
with whom applications must be lodged not
later than Wednesday, 30th September, 1914.
T. TRUMBLE,
Acting Secretary.
Books and Publications.
The First
Sydney Rationalist Annual
will be issued this week-end. It will contain “The
Task of Rationalism,” by Mr. Joseph McCabe, and
14 articles with other matter by local writers. Among
the subjects treated in their relation to rationalism
will be The Press, Bible in School, Women, .Tews,
1-ree Speech, Existence of God and Evil, Passing of
Dogmatic Religion, Jonah as a Satire, the .Tesus-
Myth, Mark Twain, Ernst Haeckel, Sir Oliver Lodge,
and a striking piece of autobiography bv Miss Mary
11. Stephen, entitled “How I Became a Rationalist.”
THE ANNUAL
will be published by

DIAMONDS
REDUCED ENORMOUSLY
Never before in the experience of Australia were so many
Diamonds sold as last year. Our Sales were Enormously
Large.
WHY?
Well, we some years ago took on a Sole Agency calling
for an enormous output, and to live up to it we decided to
offer Diamonds at merely a broker’s commission above
cutters’ landed cost.
The first year it did not pan out too well; the second
year the very large output told, and so we are continuing
the experiment for another year.
We only sell the Higher Grade of Diamonds, but our
prices are lower than those of London.
Fairfax & Roberts, Ltd.

HAVE YOU GOT
CATARRH ?
If you have a dropping; of phle&m Into the throat,
If your nose is stopped up,
If crusts form in your nose,
If you are easily susceptible to colds,
If you suffer from frontal headaches,
If you have noises in the head,
If your hearing is affected,
If you have to clear your throat & expectorate often,
If a change in the weather affects you,
If you are a victim to shortness of breath,
If you sneeze frequently,
If your sense of smell is affected,
If you have a heavy, tired feeling on awaking,
If you suffer from a husky throat,
If you are affected with any of the above symptoms,
the chances are that you are suffering from
Catarrh. It is an exceedingly dangerous com-
plaint, and is so insidious that the sufferer hardly
realises how serious is his or her condition until
matters have reached an almost chronic stage.
itarrh often begins with a cold in the head, but it never stops there.
j_he simple cold becomes a protracted one, while the discharge from the
nose grows more profuse and uncomfortable.
The inflammation extends to the throat and bronchial tubes, causing
hoarseness, a tickling sensation, and an aggravating cough. The matter
that is continually dropping back into the throat finds its way into the
stomach, resulting in a distressing form of dyspepsia, nausea, and loss of
appetite and strength.
The catarrhal poisons are absorbed into the blood, and all the membranes
of the body become infected, and what was supposed to be a purely local
.disease has become constitutional, deep-seated, and chronic, and may
eventually result in Deafness, Bronchitis, Asthma, Neuritis, Catarrh of
the Stomach, or that dread scourge of the human race, Consumption.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“The Jib”: Socialism has stalked forth
unexpectedly at Nukualofa, in the Tongan
Islands. The Union S.S. (M.L.) Co. re-
cently entirely altered the service between
Auckland and these islands, and to show
there was no ill-feeling elevated the saloon
fare, likewise the freight rates. These pre-
sents, added to the general inconvenience of
not being able to get fruit away regularly
(to say nothing of not knowing when beads,
mirrors and safety razors from Auckland
were going to arrive) has proved the last
and most ultimate straw ; so the little group
of islands is going to get a boat of its own.
Capt. Anderson, well and favorably known
all over the Pacific, is at the head of the
venture, which has been promised the whole
of the trading support of the group, as well
as a subsidy from the Tongan Government.
A steamer of modern design, specially built
for carrying fruit, has been ordered, and
skipper Anderson goes Londonwards shortly
to bring it out. So it looks as if the Union
Co. is due to get the axe right in tin' small
of the back this time. Still, you never can
te!l. .
“Black Swan”.: The W.A. State Steam-
ship Western Australian, which is so in-
dustriously assisting Premier Scaddan to
pile up his deficit, is a craft with a history.
It was built by an Austrian firm to the
order of the Russian Government, and was
intended to act as a hospital ship .and tender
to the Bear’s navy at the time the Jap. was
making trouble. It did act in the former
capacity for a time, and then took home a
cargo of wounded after tin 1 lamentable at-
tempt of Admiral Rockywhisky to fight im-
possible odds at Tsushima. It is a fast boat
and can still do IS knots when asked, but
has no special fitness for the Nor’-West
trade, having been built for something quite
different. A kangaroo may be first-class in
its own line, but it doesn’t follow that it will
be much good in a plough. And the best,
chimpanzee, if taken away from its tree and
set to preaching, may fail to draw a congre-
gation.
* * * *
“Silas Snell”: This is Porter’s story.
Porter had been wakeful, and, after counting
all the sheep in creation and trying 25 other
infallible methods of provoking sleep, he
was wider awake than ever. So he got
up, and, feeling his way out of the dark hotel
vibrant with the snores of SO boarders, he
walked along the adjacent pier, and studied
moonlight effects on the moderately-troubled
waters. New interests developed with the
appearance of a couple standing on the
extreme end of the pier, hand in hand, look-
ing down into the sea. The hour was
2 a.m., no time for poetic canoodling,
and Porter’s sudden belief was strengthened
when the couple joined forces in a convul-
sive embrace. lie was just in time to pre-
vent them going overboard, locked in the last,
long hug. “Here, here ! What’s all this
aboutV’ said Porter. “It looks infernally
suspicious,” he added after a pause. The
man answered him, “Go away, do,” he said.
“This is no business of yours. If people
won’t let us live, they’ve got no right to stop
us dying.” “That be d —d for a yarn!” an-
swered Porter. “What’s your trouble?
Come, cough it up ! There should be another
way out.” “Our trouble is this. We are
immigrants, and strangers, and broke. To-
morrow we’ll be starving. We have tried,
and we can’t get a living in this country.
That seems the simplest way out.” He
pointed down Into the water. Porter took
each by an arm. “Give it another chance,”
he said. He towed them off the pier.
"Here’s two quid to help you over,” he added.
“Now get a move on. or I’ll give you in
charge.” A week'later Rogerson, another'
lodger, told at dinner an interesting tale
of saving a young couple who were just
going to throw themselves from the pier.
Rogerson had given them a sovereign, and
exacted a promise that they would buck up
and endeavor to beat their troubles.
* * * *
“ J.F.H.”: Resist the temptation, and
don’t be a Rabbi. I thought once that it
was " a pretty good trade; but, from all
accounts, here in England it’s so rotten that
the union is demanding a living wage and
seriously talking strike. The London syna-
gogues attended by the Rothschilds and
other big moneylenders who hold Australia
in pawn pay their ministers pretty hand-
somely; but in the provincial cities little
Rachel scarcely has a hanky to wipe her nose
with. At a mass meeting of the wildest
rabbis, one of them declared that “the suf-
ferings and privations of our colleagues in
charge of provincial congregations have
reached a point which appeal with overmas-
tering force to the feelings and consciences
of the brethren in the more favored incum-
bencies.” As Australia contributes a pretty
good percentage of the income of the Cohens
of England this seems to be a case in which
King O’Malley or somebody should inter-
vene.
* * * *
“Hamer”: A Sydney firm burst its bottle
the other day when a cable came from Eng-
land announcing that the Wine Trade Club
had tasted a number of samples of the juice
of different countries, and had decided that
one particular brand was “head and shoul-
ders above the others.” The firm, which fizzed
over with indignation, declared that it had
no samples in the competition; if it had,
there would have been one other brand two
storeys at least over everything else. As the
wine which actually did the trick was the
brand of another Commonwealth firm, Aus-
tralia has nothing to complain about in
the verdict, which is merely another of the
“firsts” that this country secures as a
matter of habit. Though the trade is still
young, Australian wine already circulates in
England much more freely than knowledge
of the country that produces it. A Birming-
ham paper recently reported the remarks of
one prominent man in the trade: “We send
Australia bad convicts and it sends us good
wine.” The speaker was merely a trifle out
of date; or, again, he may have been think-
ing of friends of his who have been sent
“abroad” lately.

The Seven Joys.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[ T was the Purple Room in the Palace.
The lofty stained-glass dome that
ceiliuged it glowed with rich purple,
deep almost to blackness, and, although the
light that filtered down through it was
thinner in coloring, it filled the room with
a flush so that the air itself appeared
luminous. Yet from the absence of the
midge-movement one sees in sunlight, the
very air seemed dead. The silent, staring
walls of grey marble heightened this effect
of quiescence ; so, too, did the oaken plank-
ing of the floor, whose darkness of age was
made wealthier by the uncanny purple that
seemed to rest rather than shine upon it.
In the centre of the room was a huge bed,
over which was spread a coverlet of silvern
silk, wrought in purple thread with bar-
baric embroiderings. Supporting the bed
at its four corners were fearful bronze
dragons, crouched as if ready to spring, and
with an expression of concentrated malice
in their glittering, jewelled eyes. Indeed,
they seemed as live things transformed into
bronze at a moment of springing upon their
victim, with all the murder-will yet upon
them and their power of action merely sus-
pended.
But most startling of all in this silent
place were the Seven Joys, who stood
shoulder to shoulder, at the far end of the
room, mute and motionless, their callous,
unchanging gaze set directly before them,
straying neither to left nor right.
llow long the Purple Room had stayed
thus there was no telling. An Eternity of
Silence rather than Time seemed in posses-
sion here. Yet an atmosphere of expectancy
pervaded everything. The quiescence was
too rich in portent for perfect calm. It
was as though the Silence might itself
scream at any moment from the agony of
suspense.
Whether or not Her coming had any sig-
nificance could not be told. At least for a
while She did introduce §ome motion into
the stillness, but it was in eerie accord
with the Purple Silence. For a portiere in
the corner of the room swung open without
the slightest sound, a wan hand drew the
arras folds apart, and, with the merest
muffled press of naked feet on the oaken
planking and the faint rustle of silken gar-
ments.... She entered.
A face like Ilers could never be for-
gotten. It showed traces of the rarest
ethereal beauty, a beauty that might have
been either posthumous or antewital, but
could never have belonged to life. But
whilst the contours of beauty still hovered
in her looks, the skin, waxen in its white-
ness, was drawn closely though smoothly
over the bones of the face, producing an
effect of the strangest haggour. The
scrawny neck that showed above and the
bare feet that peeped below the black
robe She wore, had their terrible pallor
made more marked by its sable coloring.
It was only when Her eyes were looked into
that it could be seen why Her presence
made such a harmony with the Purple
Room. In their great pupils was that same
expectancy that was atmospheric in the
room itself. They seemed to rather smoulder
than shine; the lustre within them could be
felt as were they pregnant with a dire light.
With slow, measured step She glided
towards the Seven Joys, standing mute and
motionless at the bed end. and. turning on
Her heels, faced the portiere through which
She had just entered. Then She leaned
eagerly forward and listened with fearful
intentness for some expected happening.
The hush was terrible as She waited thus
—a dead ; disastrous silentness that, com-
pared to it a heart beat would have been as
thunder and as a tempest gust the sound of
breathing. But no heart beat and no lips
breathed in this silent place.
The Seven Joys stood mute and motion-
less !
After a space—of what duration was no
telling, for every second seemed eternal here
—a wondrous smile, infinitely cruel in its
prescience, came upon Her face. Her arms
sank- to Her sides, and slowly lifting Her-
self from Her leaning posture She stood
erect. Her head flung slightly backwards,
but Her eyes still fixed upon the arrassed
portiere.
The Seven Joys remained mute and
motionless!
Now for the first time was heard a defi-
nite sound, as of hesitant footsteps passing
along a distant corridor, there rushing in
eager approach, here suddenly stilled. It
seemed that, the cruelty of Her smile in-
creased with every nighing of this sound,
for now it expressed perfect surety. The
air quivered a moment as if from expectant
hope.
At length the arras by the portiere was
again drawn apart, and He stood at the
entrance, looking frightenedly round the
room. With all his rude vigor of perfect
health, his rich energy of physical youth,
there was blended a feverish lusting. It
flamed from his eyes, it trembled in his
limbs as. seeing Her before him. lie walked
towards Her as one torn between Love and
Fear, walked as one somnambulous and
distraught.
The Seven .Toys remained mute and
motionless !
He halted a few steps from Her. that
terrible smile still upon Her lips, that war-
ring Fear aud Love si ill a t travail within
him. He spoke: “Who are you. what are
you, that you have lured me to follow you
(Continued on next paijc.)

hereV I liate you, do you know that: I
hate you as I hate a Thing of Hell! ’
She made no answer, still smiling that
terrible smile.
lie took another"'step forward—he was
almost frantic now. “You beautiful dead
thing, does not my hate move you. Then I
tell you my love shall, for I must make you
mine ! You must be mine !”
With all his frenzy, how sullen- and
still was the tenuous -purple air, how blaz-
ing with menace were the dragons’ jewelled
eyes, how fearful too with portent was the
Purple Room.
And the Seven Joys remained mute and
motionless!
Now he had hurled himself upon Her in
an agony of abandon. His ravenous lips
were storming kisses upon Her face and
w-axen neck, his arms pinioned Her to him
in a baresark of desire. And 10, a faint
sigh escaped from Her lips, a Hush of color
teased Her whited skin and Her bosom, in
its rise and fall, pulsed the purple air into
visible waves.
But his face was pale now, and a tremble
was in his limbs as he held Her at arms’
length from him, gazing at those eyes that
now gloated on his with so fearful bright-
ness, those lips that now leaned towards
him in suppliance for his own. Her
beauty, too —was it indeed a thing of earth !
Perhaps he wondered so too, for he gave
a quick glance to either side as though seek-
ing a way of escape. On one side only the
grey marble walls, on the other the Seven
Joys, standing mute and motionless. There
was no escape!
But with much of his life run out and
his strength sapped from him. again he
turned to Her victress lips. Yet now was
She the assailant; now it was She clutched
him to Her and rioted her kisses on his
blanching skin, Her breath purring in long-
drawn inhalations, his answering in quick,
sudden jets.
A moment, but just for a moment, a rush
of strength came upon him. and hurling Her
away he gasped, “You ghoul!” She made
no response but to press him again to Her
bosom, devouring with ardent kisses the lips
that no longer had power to repel Her own.
The Seven Joys remained mute and
motionless !
Such beauty as now was Hers was never
seen on earth ; never before was witness to
such glory as triumphed in Her eyes as, -
holding him. limp and haggard in Her per-
fect arms, She gazed upon him breathing his
last gasping breath.
The Seven Joys remained mute and
motionless!
Quietly She carried him to the bed and
laid him, dead, upon its silvern coverlet.
And never word She said as, with slow,
exultant tread. She walked in Her fatal
loveliness from out the Purple Room.
No longer were the Seven Joys mute and
motionless. With measured step they
marched them to the bedside, and. shrouding
him in the silvern coverlet, wrought in
purple thread with barbaric embroiderings,
bore him also, shoulderwise, from out the
Purple Room.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
Malone’s Adventure with a Dog.

“Well, that was all right, mind yeh, ’n’
I’m for sayin’ nothin’ again a man slapin’
ez it pleases God ’n’ his own ar-r-guments ’n’

or where it sprung from. It jist dthropped
in on him forchewitous, ez you might say, ’n’

necdondrums.
“ ‘Hist!’ sez Malone. ‘Shoo!’ sez he.
‘Gwan wid yeh!’ he sez. But ’tis a matter
iv no consequence at. all would yeh say
‘Hist!’ to a roarin’, rompin’ brindle bulldog
iv spirit ’n’ pedigree whin he have a tidy bit
iv a cowhound be the troat, ’n’ is amusin’

inurther ’n’ divilment, accompanied wid

Malone.
“‘Hist!’ sez Malone, ’n’ the great fight
progressed t’ the chicken coop, ’n’ wrecked
the woodshed, a trimulous structure of Tim’s
own conthrivin’.
“Be this time the cattle dog kem t’ the
conclusion he was bate intoirely, his troat

out iv the polemical dishputation wanst ’n’
for all. The bulldog, bein’ a bundle iv

iv a barney, ’n’ lookin’ round for further

Malone. ’Twas recalled to him Malone had
nnjul.v interfered in the racent confabula-

Yell niver did. No matter, bein’ a gintlemau

“Malone was oiicommon alert be this time,
’n’ whin the dog bounded on his cintre piece,

under, wid all the beddin’ t’ bind him ’n’
hobble his inthoosiasm, divil doubt it. Now
Malone has the voracious ’n’ most timpis-

he wastes no time in the makin’ iv a great

wid a great swag iv beddin’, inside iv which
is a convulsive ’n’ guttural bulldog, rightly
strugglin’ to be free, ’n’ ditarmined when
liberty ’n’ equality comes to him he’ll ate
the two logs off a man iv Malone’s height ’n’
complexion.
“What does Malone do in this ixtrimity?
Bein’ an Irishman ’n’ a man iv risource, he
grips the bundle, dog, ’n’ beddin’, ’n’ all, up
in his two arrms, ’n’ he shtarts out in his
thriflin’ shmall night habilimints t’ wander
all across Cochrane’s township, lookin’ fer
the rightful ’n’ risponsible owner iv wan
brindle bulldog weighin’ a negligible pound
’r so short iv tin stone.
“Malone kicked five minutes ’r more et
Brogan’s dure wid the heel iv his fut, V
whin Mi's. Brogan’s head shot through tin;
windy, ’n’ she cursed him onsparin’ fer a
wild, dthrinker ’n’ a dishturber iv the peace,-
sez Timmy:—

dog would eat a man, ma’am V’
“ ‘The divil a dog at all. Yeh roysthcrin’
bla’gard, go way home!’ sez the widdy
Brogan in one breath, ’n’ smashed down the

“Then Malone wint on to Teddy Burnc’s
house, ’n’ he kicked ’n’ made outcry most
impious ’n’ onseoml.v, ’n’. sez he:-
“‘Damyeh, d’yeh own a ruinous dbg
weighs wan hundthred weight wan quarter?’
“ ‘Go home t’ yer bed yeh ruffyin,’ sez

rayson at all to sispict ’n’ admire Dan Calla-

t’ Dan’s house, ez that iv the on’y man fer

rilasin’ him at all, he’s wan would turn on.

“ ‘I do,’ sez Dan.

care is shown in the selection of a steam plant to ensure the greatest economy,
yet the insulation which is of equal importance is in many cases partly neg-
lected. If you strive to make a saving in the steam consumption of your
engine, why not make certain that it is not wasted again through faulty in-
sulation ? The claims we make as to the exceptional efficiency of Newall’s
Coverings are fully borne out by actual test, and, further, by the enormous
sales, including repeat orders, and we do not know of a single instance where
any other covering has been used once Newall’s have been tried.
The Ultimo and White Bay Power Houses are insulated throughout with
Newall’s Coverings, and this material has also been adopted exclusively by the
N.S.W. and Victorian Governments. These are facts which will tend to fur-
ther prove our claims. The innumerable advantages of this material over all.
other forms of lagging are described in interesting booklet, which we will be
